The Hanafi Principle: Prohibition Does Not Render an Act Invalid
Explaining the distinction between prohibitions that invalidate and those that do not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a prohibition invalidates an act by determining if the prohibition is for an external reason (nahy li ghayrihi) or for the act itself (nahy li nafsihi).

Core Idea
Not every prohibition invalidates: if the prohibition is due to an external factor, the act remains valid.

The Hanafi school makes a crucial distinction regarding the effect of prohibition on legal acts. A prohibition (nahy) does not automatically render an act invalid; rather, the validity depends on whether the prohibition attaches to an external circumstance or to the essence of the act itself. In this lesson, we explore the key examples from the source text: divorce during menstruation, traveling for sin, and seizure by a non-Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the prohibition.
In the examples given, what is the act? What is the prohibition? For instance, divorce during menstruation is prohibited but is it valid?
Note the act: divorce. The prohibition is due to harm to the woman.
2
Classify the prohibition.
Determine if the prohibition is due to a meaning in something else (li ma'nan fi ghayrihi) or due to a meaning in itself (li ma'nan fi nafsihi).
Use the criterion: is the prohibition because of an external factor or because the act itself cannot be constituted?
3
Apply the Hanafi rule.
If the prohibition is for an external reason, the act is valid though sinful. If the prohibition is for the act itself, the act is invalid (batil).
Divorce: external → valid. Fasting on Eid: inherent → invalid.
4
Contrast with the case of fasting on Eid.
Why is fasting on Eid invalid? Because the prohibition relates to the essence of the act—the act cannot be constituted as a valid fast.
The day itself is not a valid day for fasting.
5
Understand the rationale.
The prohibition aims at non-occurrence by choice, so it presupposes the possibility of the act. If the act cannot exist (like fasting on Eid), the prohibition confirms its impossibility.
This rationale distinguishes nahy from naskh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهي عنه؛ لمعنى في غيره، وهو الضرر بالمرأة، بتطويل العدة، أو بتلبيس أمر العدة عليها. ولهذا لم يكن سفر المعصية سبباً للرخصة؛ للنهي. ولا يملك الكافرُ مالَ المسلم بالاستيلاء؛ للنهي أيضاً، فلم يصلح سبباً مشروعاً. ولا يلزم الظهار؛ لأن كلامنا وقع في حكم مطلوب تعلَّق مشروع له أيبقى سبباً له، والحكم به مشروعاً مع وقوع النهي عليه؟ فأما ما هو حرام غير مشروع تعلق به جزاء زاجر عنه: فيعتمد حرمة سببه، كالقصاص ليس بحكم مطلوب بسبب مشروع، بل جزاء شرع زاجراً، فاعتمد حرمة سببه. ولنا ما احتج به محمد رحمه الله في كتاب الطلاق أن صيام العيد وأيام التشريق منهي والنهي لا يقع على ما لا يتكون. وبيانه أن النهي يُراد به عدم الفعل مضافاً إلى اختيار العباد وكسبهم، فيَعتمد تصوره ليكون العبد مبتلى بين أن يكفّ عنه باختياره، فيُثاب عليه، وبين أن يفعله باختياره، فیلزمه جزاؤه. والنسخ لإعدام الشيء شرعاً؛ لينعدم فعل العبد؛ لعدم المشروع بنفسه؛ ليصير امتناعه بناء على عدمه، وفي النهي يكون عدمه بناء على
Prohibited due to a meaning in something else, which is harm to the woman, by prolonging the waiting period or by confusing the matter of the waiting period. For this reason, a journey of disobedience does not become a reason for license; due to the prohibition. Nor does a disbeliever own the property of a Muslim by seizure; also due to the prohibition, so it does not become a valid cause. And zihar is not binding; because our discussion is about a ruling of a desired [act] that has a legal cause; does it remain a cause for it? And the ruling is valid despite the prohibition? As for what is unlawful and not valid, attached to it is a deterrent penalty: it depends on the prohibition of its cause, like qisas is not a ruling of a desired [act] due to a valid cause, but rather a penalty legislated as a deterrent, so it depends on the prohibition of its cause. And we have what Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, argued in the Book of Divorce that fasting on the day of Eid and the days of Tashriq is prohibited, and the prohibition does not apply to what cannot come into existence. Its explanation is that the prohibition is intended for the non-occurrence of the act attributed to the choice and acquisition of the servants, so it depends on its conception so that the servant is tested between refraining from it by his choice, so he is rewarded, and between doing it by his choice, so he incurs its penalty. And abrogation is for the legal removal of a thing; so that the servant's act ceases due to the lack of legality itself; so that his abstention is based on its non-existence, while in prohibition, its non-existence is based on
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary discusses the effect of prohibition (nahy) on the validity of acts. The author begins by giving examples: divorce during menstruation is prohibited due to harm to the woman (prolonging the waiting period), yet the divorce is still valid. Similarly, traveling for sin does not become a license for shortening prayer because of the prohibition, and a disbeliever's seizure of a Muslim's property is invalid due to prohibition. The author then raises a question: does a prohibition nullify the act's status as a valid cause? The answer distinguishes two types of prohibited acts. The first type is prohibited due to an external reason (like divorce); such acts remain valid. The second type is inherently invalid (like fasting on Eid), where the prohibition goes to the essence. The author cites Imam Muhammad's argument from Kitab al-Talaq: fasting on Eid and the days of Tashriq is prohibited, and the prohibition does not apply to something that cannot come into existence (i.e., the act cannot be valid). The reasoning is that prohibition aims at non-occurrence by choice, so it assumes the act is possible. In contrast, abrogation (naskh) removes the legal basis of the act entirely, so the servant's abstention is based on the act's non-existence. The Hanafi position is that not every prohibition implies invalidity; the key is whether the prohibition is due to an external factor or to the act itself.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): هو طلب الترك، لكنه هنا يعني حرمة الفعل مع بقاء صحته إن كان النهي لمعنى في غيره، بخلاف ما إذا كان النهي لمعنى في نفسه فيوجب البطلان
النسخ (al-naskh): هو رفع الحكم الشرعي بدليل متأخر، ويؤدي إلى عدم مشروعية الفعل نفسه بحيث لا يتصور وجوده شرعاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle, why is divorce during menstruation valid despite being prohibited?
A) Because the prohibition is for the act itself.
B) Because the prohibition is for an external reason.
C) Because the prohibition is abrogated.
D) Because divorce is not an act of worship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a prohibition does not invalidate an act unless the prohibition is due to the act's own essence.

Naskh — Abrogation

Nahy vs Naskh: Different Modes of Legal Removal
Explaining the conceptual difference between prohibition and abrogation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prohibition (nahy) and abrogation (naskh) in terms of their effect on the possibility of the act.

Core Idea
Nahy retains the act's possibility; naskh removes the act's legal existence entirely.

The passage concludes with a crucial comparison: prohibition (nahy) assumes the act can exist, leaving it up to the servant's choice, while abrogation (naskh) removes the act's legality so that it cannot exist at all. This distinction has profound implications for understanding how rules are lifted or modified in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts: nahy and naskh.
Nahy is a command to abstain; naskh is the removal of a previous ruling by a later evidence.
Both involve a change in the ruling, but in different ways.
2
Analyze the effect on the act's existence.
Nahy presupposes the act's possibility: the servant can either commit the sin and be punished or refrain and be rewarded. Naskh makes the act legally non-existent: the servant's abstention is due to the act's absence, not choice.
In nahy, the act remains 'possible' in the sense that it can be performed.
3
Apply to the example of fasting on Eid.
Fasting on Eid is prohibited (nahy) but also the act cannot be constituted (the time is not valid for fasting). Explain why that is more like naskh? Actually the passage says the prohibition does not apply to what cannot come into existence, so it's a case where the act is impossible. That shows that nahy only applies when the act is possible. Naskh makes the act impossible by removing its legal basis.
Fasting on Eid is not a valid act even without the prohibition.
4
Contrast with divorce during menstruation.
Divorce is possible and prohibited due to external factors, so the nahy applies and the act remains possible. In naskh, the act is no longer legislated.
Divorce remains a valid act despite the prohibition.
5
Formulate the Hanafi view.
Nahy leaves the act's legality intact but prohibits the choice; naskh removes the legality. Therefore, the two are fundamentally different in their effect on the act's existence.
This distinction is essential for correct istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary discusses the effect of prohibition (nahy) on the validity of acts. The author begins by giving examples: divorce during menstruation is prohibited due to harm to the woman (prolonging the waiting period), yet the divorce is still valid. Similarly, traveling for sin does not become a license for shortening prayer because of the prohibition, and a disbeliever's seizure of a Muslim's property is invalid due to prohibition. The author then raises a question: does a prohibition nullify the act's status as a valid cause? The answer distinguishes two types of prohibited acts. The first type is prohibited due to an external reason (like divorce); such acts remain valid. The second type is inherently invalid (like fasting on Eid), where the prohibition goes to the essence. The author cites Imam Muhammad's argument from Kitab al-Talaq: fasting on Eid and the days of Tashriq is prohibited, and the prohibition does not apply to something that cannot come into existence (i.e., the act cannot be valid). The reasoning is that prohibition aims at non-occurrence by choice, so it assumes the act is possible. In contrast, abrogation (naskh) removes the legal basis of the act entirely, so the servant's abstention is based on the act's non-existence. The Hanafi position is that not every prohibition implies invalidity; the key is whether the prohibition is due to an external factor or to the act itself.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): هو طلب الترك، لكنه هنا يعني حرمة الفعل مع بقاء صحته إن كان النهي لمعنى في غيره، بخلاف ما إذا كان النهي لمعنى في نفسه فيوجب البطلان
النسخ (al-naskh): هو رفع الحكم الشرعي بدليل متأخر، ويؤدي إلى عدم مشروعية الفعل نفسه بحيث لا يتصور وجوده شرعاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between nahy and naskh?
A) Nahy applies only to acts of worship, naskh to all acts.
B) Nahy presupposes the act's possibility, naskh removes its legal existence.
C) Naskh is a type of nahy.
D) Both have the same effect on the act.

Key Takeaway
Prohibition (nahy) does not remove the legal existence of an act; it only makes it sinful, while abrogation (naskh) eliminates the act's legality entirely.

Naskh — Abrogation

Proof of Abrogation vs. Proof of Specification: The Usul Rule
Explains the difference between naskh and khusus with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a proof of abrogation (dalil al-naskh) and a proof of specification (dalil al-khusus), state their respective effects on the general ruling, and apply the distinction to furu' examples.

Core Idea
Abrogation cancels the prior ruling entirely; specification clarifies a case was never covered.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul tackles a critical analytical skill: how to tell whether a later specific ruling abrogates the general or merely specifies. The difference determines whether the general ruling remains binding for other cases. We'll see this through sales examples involving mixed items and option conditions.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types
Identify the definitions of dalil al-naskh and dalil al-khusus from the passage. Naskh operates by opposing the original text, while khusus shows exclusion from the general.
Dalil al-naskh abrogates; dalil al-khusus specifies.
2
Contrast their operation
Note how each interacts with the original general text. Naskh opposes the text; khusus clarifies that the specific case was never included under the general formulation.
3
Understand the effect on remaining cases
For abrogation, the ruling on remaining cases is unchanged because the abrogator opposes the original. For specification, the general remains for other cases because the specified case was never covered.
The passage emphasizes that for naskh the ruling does not change; for khusus the general is unaffected.
4
Map the examples
Connect the sales examples to the appropriate category: mixed items (free/slave, live/dead, vinegar/wine) as specification? Actually the passage says that when one item cannot enter the contract, it is like istithna (exception), which is analogous to khusus. The option condition (khiyar) is compared to naskh in cause and istithna in ruling.
The khiyar case is the key illustration of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالاحتمال
* وهذا بخلاف الناسخ إذا ورد في بعض ما تناوله النص معلوماً، فإن الحكم فيما بقي لا يتغير ؛ لاحتمال التعليل ؛ لأن الناسخ إنما يعمل على طريق المعارضة للنص، لا على تبين أنه لم يدخل تحت الصيغة، فتصير العلة معارضةً للنص.
وأما ها هنا : فإن التعليل يقع على ما وُضع له دليل الخصوص، وهو أن لا يدخل تحت الجملة، فلا يصير معارضاً للنص، فإذا ثبت الاحتمال، فلم يخرج عن الدلالة بالشك: صار الدليل مشكوكاً بأصله، فأشبه دليل القياس فاستقام أن يعارضه القياس.
بخلاف ما ثبت بخبر الواحد؛ لأنه يقين بأصله، فلم يصلح أن يعارضه القياس.
ونظير هذه الجملة من الفروع أن البيع إذا أُضيف إلى حُرِّ وعبدِ بثمن معلوم واحد، أو إلى حي وميت، أو خل وخمر: فهو باطل؛ لأن أحدهما لم يدخل تحت العقد، فبقي الآخر وحده بحصته ابتداءً.
وكذلك إذا قال بعتُ منك هذين العبدين بألف درهم، إلا هذا بحصته من الألف، فصارت هذه الجملة نظير الاستثناء.
وإذا باع عبدين فمات أحدهما قبل التسليم، أو استحق، أو وُجد أحدهما مكاتباً أو مدبَّراً صح البيع في الباقي ؛ لأن الآخر داخل في البيع.
وكذلك المدبر والمكاتبُ يدخلان في البيع، وإنما امتنع الحكم؛ صيانة لحقهما، فصار الآخر باقياً في العقد بحصته، فصار هذا من قسم دليل النسخ. ونظير دليل الخصوص: مسألة خيار الشرط.
[وسئل؟] قال في «الزيادات»: في رجل باع عبدين بألف درهم على أنه بالخيار في أحدهما: إن البيع لا يصح حتى يُعين الذي فيه الخيار، فأما إذا أجمل الثمن، ولم يُعيّن الذي فيه الخيار، أو عين أحدهما ولم الآخر: لم يجز البيع؛ لأن الخيار لا يمنع الدخول في الإيجاب، ويمنع الدخول في الحكم، فصار في السبب: نظير دليل النسخ، وفي الحكم نظير الاستثناء، فقيل: لا بدَّ من إعلام الثمن والمبيع؛ لجواز البيع، بمنزلة الحر والعبد، وإذا وجد التعيين، وإعلام الحصة: صح البيع، ولم يُعتبر الذي شرط فيه الخيار شرطاً فاسداً في الآخر.
بخلاف الحر والعبد، وما شاكل ذلك في قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه يعتبر شرطاً فاسداً في الآخر، لا محالة، فيفسد به البيع، والله أعلم.
By probability.
* This is contrary to the abrogator (nasikh) when it occurs regarding some of what the text addresses in a known way, for the ruling regarding what remains does not change due to the probability of causation (ta'lil); because the abrogator operates by way of opposing the text, not by clarifying that it was never included under the formulation, so the cause becomes opposed to the text.
As for here: the causation (ta'lil) occurs regarding what the proof of specification (dalil al-khusus) was established for, which is that [the specified item] is not included under the general statement, so it does not become opposed to the text. When probability is established, it does not exit from indication by doubt: the proof becomes doubtful in its origin, thus resembling analogical proof (qiyas), so it is proper for analogy to oppose it.
Unlike what is established by a solitary report (khabar al-wahid); because it is certain in its origin, so analogy cannot properly oppose it.
An example of this principle from the branches: if a sale is linked to a free person and a slave for one known price, or to a live and a dead [animal], or to vinegar and wine: it is invalid; because one of them did not enter the contract, so the other remains alone with its share from the beginning.
Similarly, if he says, 'I sold you these two slaves for one thousand dirhams, except this one for its share of the thousand,' then this [phrase] becomes analogous to an exception (istithna).
And if he sold two slaves and one of them died before delivery, or was reclaimed, or one was found to be a mukatab or mudabbir, the sale is valid for the remaining one; because the other entered the sale. Similarly, the mudabbir and the mukatab enter the sale; the ruling was prevented only to protect their rights, so the other remains in the contract with its share, and this falls under the category of abrogating proof (dalil al-naskh). And analogous to the proof of specification is the issue of the option (khiyar) condition.
[He was asked?] He said in al-Ziyadat: Concerning a man who sold two slaves for one thousand dirhams on the condition that he has the option regarding one of them: the sale is not valid until he specifies the one in which the option is, and [states] its price. As for when he aggregates the price and does not specify the one with the option, or specifies one but not the other: the sale is not permissible; because the option does not prevent entry into the offer (ijab), but it prevents entry into the [full] ruling (hukm). Thus it becomes in terms of the cause (sabab) analogous to abrogating proof, and in terms of the ruling analogous to exception. So it is said: it is necessary to declare the price and the sold item for the validity of the sale, similar to the case of a free person and a slave. And when specification is found and the share is declared: the sale is valid, and what was stipulated regarding the option is not considered a corrupt condition (shart fasid) affecting the other [slave].
Contrary to the case of a free person and a slave, and similar [mixed items], according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): it is necessarily considered a corrupt condition in the other, thus it invalidates the sale. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul, likely with commentary drawing on al-Ziyadat, addresses a key distinction in Hanafi usul between the proof of abrogation (dalil al-naskh) and the proof of specification (dalil al-khusus). The author explains that when a text is general and later a specific ruling comes, it could be either abrogation (which cancels the general ruling for all cases) or specification (which shows the specific case was never intended). The difference affects how the remaining ruling is treated. For abrogation, the ruling on the remaining cases is unchanged because the abrogator opposes the original text. For specification, the specific case is excluded from the general, but the general remains for others. The passage then applies this to sales: mixing a free person with a slave, or a live with dead, or vinegar with wine, invalidates the sale because the contract cannot include both simultaneously; one is left out. Exception (istithna) is analogous to specification. Then, quoting al-Ziyadat, the passage discusses the option condition in a sale of two slaves: if the buyer has the option to cancel regarding one, the sale is invalid unless the option is tied to a specific slave and its price is known. This is because the option prevents the full ruling from taking effect for that slave, analogous to abrogation in the cause, but the other slave remains validly sold. However, Abu Hanifa held that in cases like mixing free and slave, the condition affects both, invalidating the entire sale. The passage uses these examples to illustrate the usul principle that the mode of operation of a proof (abrogation vs. specification) determines its legal effects.

Key Terms
دليل النسخ (dalil al-naskh): A proof that abrogates a previous ruling by opposing the original text, without claiming the original text never applied.
دليل الخصوص (dalil al-khusus): A proof that shows a specific case was never included under the general text, thus the general remains for other cases.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulation in a contract granting a party the option to cancel within a specific period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key factor that distinguishes a proof of abrogation from a proof of specification?
A) The authority of the proof (Quran vs. hadith)
B) Whether the proof opposes the original text or clarifies that a case was never included
C) The number of cases affected
D) Whether the proof is explicit or implicit

Key Takeaway
A later specific ruling can either abrogate a general ruling or specify that the case was never included; the distinction depends on whether the proof opposes the original text or merely excludes a case.

Analogy in Action: The Khiyar Condition as a Hybrid of Naskh and Khusus
Explains the khiyar condition as a hybrid of naskh and khusus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the khiyar al-shart example from the passage, identify its dual analogy to naskh and khusus, and apply the same reasoning to other furu' cases.

Core Idea
The option condition is like naskh in cause and like istithna in ruling—a hybrid proof.

In the previous lesson, we established the conceptual distinction between naskh and khusus. Now we dive into a subtle example: the option condition (khiyar al-shart) in a sale of two slaves. The passage from al-Bazdawi, citing al-Ziyadat, shows that this condition operates partly like an abrogation and partly like an exception. We'll unpack this analogy and see how it applies to other sales scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Recall the khiyar scenario
The passage describes a man selling two slaves for a fixed price with the condition that he has the option to cancel regarding one of them. The sale is invalid unless the specific slave with the option is identified and its share of the price is known.
This scenario is from al-Ziyadat.
2
Analyze the cause (sabab) analogy
The option does not prevent the slave from entering the contract (ijab), but it prevents the full ruling (hukm) of ownership from taking effect. This is analogous to naskh, where a later ruling prevents the earlier ruling from applying.
The passage says 'في السبب: نظير دليل النسخ'.
3
Analyze the ruling (hukm) analogy
In terms of the final ruling for the other slave, the option condition acts like an exception (istithna), which is analogous to specification (khusus). The other slave remains validly sold.
The passage says 'وفي الحكم نظير الاستثناء'.
4
Contrast with the mixed items case
In the mixed items case (free/slave), the free person never enters the contract at all; this is purely specification. In khiyar, the slave does enter but the ruling is suspended. This difference explains why Abu Hanifa treats mixed items differently from khiyar in terms of the condition affecting the entire sale.
The passage ends with Abu Hanifa's view that mixed items condition invalidates the whole sale.

Source Text

بالاحتمال
* وهذا بخلاف الناسخ إذا ورد في بعض ما تناوله النص معلوماً، فإن الحكم فيما بقي لا يتغير ؛ لاحتمال التعليل ؛ لأن الناسخ إنما يعمل على طريق المعارضة للنص، لا على تبين أنه لم يدخل تحت الصيغة، فتصير العلة معارضةً للنص.
وأما ها هنا : فإن التعليل يقع على ما وُضع له دليل الخصوص، وهو أن لا يدخل تحت الجملة، فلا يصير معارضاً للنص، فإذا ثبت الاحتمال، فلم يخرج عن الدلالة بالشك: صار الدليل مشكوكاً بأصله، فأشبه دليل القياس فاستقام أن يعارضه القياس.
بخلاف ما ثبت بخبر الواحد؛ لأنه يقين بأصله، فلم يصلح أن يعارضه القياس.
ونظير هذه الجملة من الفروع أن البيع إذا أُضيف إلى حُرِّ وعبدِ بثمن معلوم واحد، أو إلى حي وميت، أو خل وخمر: فهو باطل؛ لأن أحدهما لم يدخل تحت العقد، فبقي الآخر وحده بحصته ابتداءً.
وكذلك إذا قال بعتُ منك هذين العبدين بألف درهم، إلا هذا بحصته من الألف، فصارت هذه الجملة نظير الاستثناء.
وإذا باع عبدين فمات أحدهما قبل التسليم، أو استحق، أو وُجد أحدهما مكاتباً أو مدبَّراً صح البيع في الباقي ؛ لأن الآخر داخل في البيع.
وكذلك المدبر والمكاتبُ يدخلان في البيع، وإنما امتنع الحكم؛ صيانة لحقهما، فصار الآخر باقياً في العقد بحصته، فصار هذا من قسم دليل النسخ. ونظير دليل الخصوص: مسألة خيار الشرط.
[وسئل؟] قال في «الزيادات»: في رجل باع عبدين بألف درهم على أنه بالخيار في أحدهما: إن البيع لا يصح حتى يُعين الذي فيه الخيار، فأما إذا أجمل الثمن، ولم يُعيّن الذي فيه الخيار، أو عين أحدهما ولم الآخر: لم يجز البيع؛ لأن الخيار لا يمنع الدخول في الإيجاب، ويمنع الدخول في الحكم، فصار في السبب: نظير دليل النسخ، وفي الحكم نظير الاستثناء، فقيل: لا بدَّ من إعلام الثمن والمبيع؛ لجواز البيع، بمنزلة الحر والعبد، وإذا وجد التعيين، وإعلام الحصة: صح البيع، ولم يُعتبر الذي شرط فيه الخيار شرطاً فاسداً في الآخر.
بخلاف الحر والعبد، وما شاكل ذلك في قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه يعتبر شرطاً فاسداً في الآخر، لا محالة، فيفسد به البيع، والله أعلم.
By probability.
* This is contrary to the abrogator (nasikh) when it occurs regarding some of what the text addresses in a known way, for the ruling regarding what remains does not change due to the probability of causation (ta'lil); because the abrogator operates by way of opposing the text, not by clarifying that it was never included under the formulation, so the cause becomes opposed to the text.
As for here: the causation (ta'lil) occurs regarding what the proof of specification (dalil al-khusus) was established for, which is that [the specified item] is not included under the general statement, so it does not become opposed to the text. When probability is established, it does not exit from indication by doubt: the proof becomes doubtful in its origin, thus resembling analogical proof (qiyas), so it is proper for analogy to oppose it.
Unlike what is established by a solitary report (khabar al-wahid); because it is certain in its origin, so analogy cannot properly oppose it.
An example of this principle from the branches: if a sale is linked to a free person and a slave for one known price, or to a live and a dead [animal], or to vinegar and wine: it is invalid; because one of them did not enter the contract, so the other remains alone with its share from the beginning.
Similarly, if he says, 'I sold you these two slaves for one thousand dirhams, except this one for its share of the thousand,' then this [phrase] becomes analogous to an exception (istithna).
And if he sold two slaves and one of them died before delivery, or was reclaimed, or one was found to be a mukatab or mudabbir, the sale is valid for the remaining one; because the other entered the sale. Similarly, the mudabbir and the mukatab enter the sale; the ruling was prevented only to protect their rights, so the other remains in the contract with its share, and this falls under the category of abrogating proof (dalil al-naskh). And analogous to the proof of specification is the issue of the option (khiyar) condition.
[He was asked?] He said in al-Ziyadat: Concerning a man who sold two slaves for one thousand dirhams on the condition that he has the option regarding one of them: the sale is not valid until he specifies the one in which the option is, and [states] its price. As for when he aggregates the price and does not specify the one with the option, or specifies one but not the other: the sale is not permissible; because the option does not prevent entry into the offer (ijab), but it prevents entry into the [full] ruling (hukm). Thus it becomes in terms of the cause (sabab) analogous to abrogating proof, and in terms of the ruling analogous to exception. So it is said: it is necessary to declare the price and the sold item for the validity of the sale, similar to the case of a free person and a slave. And when specification is found and the share is declared: the sale is valid, and what was stipulated regarding the option is not considered a corrupt condition (shart fasid) affecting the other [slave].
Contrary to the case of a free person and a slave, and similar [mixed items], according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): it is necessarily considered a corrupt condition in the other, thus it invalidates the sale. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul, likely with commentary drawing on al-Ziyadat, addresses a key distinction in Hanafi usul between the proof of abrogation (dalil al-naskh) and the proof of specification (dalil al-khusus). The author explains that when a text is general and later a specific ruling comes, it could be either abrogation (which cancels the general ruling for all cases) or specification (which shows the specific case was never intended). The difference affects how the remaining ruling is treated. For abrogation, the ruling on the remaining cases is unchanged because the abrogator opposes the original text. For specification, the specific case is excluded from the general, but the general remains for others. The passage then applies this to sales: mixing a free person with a slave, or a live with dead, or vinegar with wine, invalidates the sale because the contract cannot include both simultaneously; one is left out. Exception (istithna) is analogous to specification. Then, quoting al-Ziyadat, the passage discusses the option condition in a sale of two slaves: if the buyer has the option to cancel regarding one, the sale is invalid unless the option is tied to a specific slave and its price is known. This is because the option prevents the full ruling from taking effect for that slave, analogous to abrogation in the cause, but the other slave remains validly sold. However, Abu Hanifa held that in cases like mixing free and slave, the condition affects both, invalidating the entire sale. The passage uses these examples to illustrate the usul principle that the mode of operation of a proof (abrogation vs. specification) determines its legal effects.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulation in a contract granting a party the option to cancel within a specific period.
دليل الخصوص (dalil al-khusus): A proof that shows a specific case was never included under the general text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the option condition (khiyar) considered analogous to naskh in terms of cause (sabab)?
A) Because it prevents the slave from entering the contract
B) Because it opposes the original text of the contract
C) Because it prevents the full ruling from taking effect for that slave
D) Because it is a later condition that abrogates the earlier sale

Key Takeaway
The option condition (khiyar) is a hybrid proof: in cause it resembles naskh (prevention of ruling), but in ruling it is like istithna/khusus (valid for the unaffected item).

Takrir vs. Multiplicity: The Repetition in Surah al-Inshirah
Refuting Ibn Abbas' claim about 'ushr and yusr based on definiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of takrir to distinguish between emphatic repetition and multiplicity in Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Repetition of a definite noun in a single context is takrir (emphasis), not proof of multiple instances.

This passage addresses an interpretation attributed to Ibn Abbas regarding the repeated phrase in Surah al-Inshirah (94:5-6). Ibn Abbas understood the repetition of 'yusr' as indefinite to indicate two distinct eases, but the Hanafi usul scholar al-Bazdawi refutes this, arguing that the repetition is takrir (emphatic repetition) similar to Surah al-Qiyamah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from Quranic texts that employ repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested phrase and its linguistic features
Examine the repeated phrase 'فإن مع العسر يسرا إن مع العسر يسرا'. Note that 'al-‘usr' appears with definiteness (alif lam) in both instances, while 'yusr' appears indefinite.
The definiteness of 'al-‘usr' is identical, while 'yusr' is indefinite each time.
2
State Ibn Abbas' interpretation and its basis
Ibn Abbas reportedly concluded that there are two eases because 'yusr' is repeated as indefinite, and one hardship cannot overcome two eases. This relies on the idea that repetition of an indefinite noun signals a new instance.
The narration is introduced with 'if this report is authentic'.
3
State the Hanafi position and its evidence
Al-Bazdawi asserts that this is takrir (emphasis), not multiplicity. He cites the parallel repetition in al-Qiyamah (75:34-35) 'أولى لك فأولى ثم أولى لك فأولى' as an example of pure emphasis.
Takrir is a well-known linguistic device in Arabic rhetoric.
4
Apply the principle of takrir to the text
Since both occurrences of 'al-‘usr' are definite and identical, and the context does not introduce new information, the repetition is for emphasis. The definiteness of 'al-‘usr' indicates a single known hardship; the indefinite 'yusr' simply affirms ease alongside it.
The Hanafi principle: repetition of the same word in the same form without change is takrir.
5
Conclude the Hanafi tarjih
The author prefers the takrir interpretation over Ibn Abbas' view, emphasizing that even if the report is authentic, the linguistic principle overrides.
This reflects the Hanafi methodology: prioritizing established usul principles over isolated narrations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك معنى قول ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما في قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ بُرا له الشرح: لن يغلب عسر واحد يُسرين (۱) ؛ الْعُسْرِ. يُسْرًا
لأن العسر أُعيد معرفةً ، واليُسرَ أُعيد نكرة، إن صحت هذه الحكاية عنه.
وفيه نظر عندنا، بل هذا تكرير، مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَول لَكَ فَأَوْلَى ثم أَوْلَى لَكَ فَأَوْلَى . القيامة.
And that is the meaning of the statement of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) regarding the statement of Allah Most High: 'For indeed, with hardship comes ease. Indeed, with hardship comes ease.' — the explanation: one hardship will not overcome two eases: because al-‘usr (the hardship) was repeated with definiteness, and al-yusr (ease) was repeated as indefinite, if this narration from him is correct.
And there is consideration in it according to us. Rather, this is takrir (repetition for emphasis), like the statement of Allah Most High: 'Woe to you, then woe to you, again woe to you, then woe to you.' (al-Qiyamah).
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage discusses an interpretation attributed to Ibn Abbas regarding the repeated phrase in Surah al-Inshirah (94:5-6): 'فإن مع العسر يسرا إن مع العسر يسرا'. Ibn Abbas reportedly said that the first 'عسر' is definite, the second 'عسر' is also definite, but 'يسر' is repeated as indefinite, so there are two eases and one hardship, meaning hardship will not overcome ease. The author al-Bazdawi comments that this narration is questionable, and even if authentic, the interpretation is not sound. Instead, the repetition is takrir (emphatic repetition), similar to the repetition in Surah al-Qiyamah (75:34-35): 'أولى لك فأولى ثم أولى لك فأولى'. Takrir is a rhetorical device for emphasis, not a sign of multiplicity. This shows the author's methodological approach: he prioritizes the established usul principle that repetition of the same word in the same form is for emphasis, not for creating a new entity.

Key Terms
تكرير (takrīr): Repetition for emphasis, where the repeated word adds no new meaning but strengthens the statement.
معرفة (ma‘rifah): Definite noun; in this context, 'al-‘usr' is definite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) As takrir (emphatic repetition) similar to 'أولى لك فأولى'.
C) As a grammatical error in the Quran.
D) As evidence for abrogation.

Key Takeaway
Repetition of a definite noun in a single context is takrir (emphasis), not proof of multiplicity.

The Conjunction Waw Does Not Necessitate Sequence: The Case of Sa'y
Demonstrating that the conjunction waw does not necessitate sequence, with application to sa'y

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that the conjunction waw does not impose order to analyze a Quranic text and distinguish between obligation of the act from the text and obligation of sequence from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
The waw in 'Safa and Marwah' does not command sequence; sequence becomes wajib through the Prophet's practice, not the verse.

In Hanafi usul, the simple conjunction 'waw' carries no inherent implication of order or sequence. This lesson examines the verse on Safa and Marwah (al-Baqarah 2:158) to show how the obligation of sa'y comes from the text, but the order of starting with Safa is derived from the Prophet's practice (sunnah fi'liyyah) through the principle of tarjih (giving preponderance). By understanding this distinction, you will be able to correctly attribute the source of obligations and avoid conflating the Quranic command with the Sunnah's specification of sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Usul Principle
State the rule: the conjunction waw does not indicate order. Confirm that the mere mention of two items with waw is neutral regarding sequence.
This is a settled Hanafi rule, not derived from the verse but applied to it.
2
Deconstruct the Verse
Separate the verse into two parts: the first ('Indeed as-Safa and al-Marwah are among the symbols of Allah') and the second ('that he should circumambulate between them'). Note that the first part only affirms status; the second establishes the act of sa'y.
The waw in the first part is purely conjunctive.
3
Apply Takhsis and Bayan
Recognise that the first part is not a command (takhsis), while the second part provides the ruling (bayan). The waw in the first part does not order sa'y.
This shows the function of waw in declarative statements.
4
Understand Tarjih
Since sa'y must be performed in some order, the mention of Safa first is taken as an indication (qarinah) of stronger concern. This is used as a basis for tarjih, establishing that starting with Safa is given preponderance and thus becomes wajib via the Prophet's practice.
Tarjih is a method to resolve when the text is silent on sequence.
5
Analogise with Bequests
Note the analogy with bequests: when a deceased mentions multiple supererogatory charities, the executor starts with the first mentioned, indicating priority. This supports the method of tarjih based on order of mention.
This analogy strengthens the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قلنا: إن قول محمد رحمه الله في الكتاب»: وينوي من عن يمينه من الرجال والنساء والحَفَظَة : إنه لا يوجب ترتيباً.
وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَائر الله﴾ البقرة / ١٥٨ : لا يوجب ترتيباً أيضاً، ألا يُرى أن المراد بالآية : إثبات أنهما من الشعائر، ولا يتصوّر فيه الترتيب، وإنما ثبت السعي بقوله تعالى : ﴿أَن يَطَّوفَ بِهِمَا﴾ البقرة / ١٥٨ .
غير أن السعي لا ينفك عن ترتيب والتقديم في الذكر يدل على قوة المقدَّم ظاهراً، وهذا يصلح للترجيح، فترجح به، فصار الترتيب واجباً بفعله، لا بنص الآية.
وهذا كما قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله في الوصايا بالقُرب النوافل: إنه يبدأ بما بدأ به الميت؛ لأن ذلك دلالة على قوة الاهتمام، فصلح للترجيح.
And therefore we said: Indeed the statement of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) in the book: 'And he intends from those on his right from men and women and the guardians' does not impose an order. Similarly, the statement of the Most High: 'Indeed, as-Safa and al-Marwah are among the symbols of Allah' (al-Baqarah 158) also does not impose an order; do you not see that the intent of the verse is to affirm that they are among the symbols, and order is not conceivable in that? Rather, the sa'y (ritual walking) is established by His statement: 'that he should make tawaf (circumambulate) between them' (al-Baqarah 158). However, the sa'y cannot be free from order, and the mention (of Safa) first apparently indicates the strength of the first-mentioned, and this is suitable for giving preponderance, so order is preponderated by it, thus order became obligatory by the act (of the Prophet), not by the text of the verse. And this is as our companions said concerning bequests for supererogatory acts of worship: that he begins with what the deceased began with, because that is an indication of the strength of concern, so it is suitable for giving preponderance.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi addresses the principle that the conjunction waw does not inherently indicate order or sequence. The author starts by referencing Imam Muhammad's statement in his book regarding intention in a line of witnesses: the word 'and' (waw) in 'men and women and guardians' does not impose a specific order. He then applies the same principle to the Quranic verse about Safa and Marwah (al-Baqarah 158). The verse itself merely declares them as among Allah's symbols, using waw without implying sequence. The actual obligation to perform sa'y (walking between them) comes from another part of the verse: 'that he should circumambulate between them.' However, since the physical act of walking cannot occur without order (one must start at one hill and then go to the other), and because the mention of Safa before Marwah in the text suggests a priority, the sequence (starting at Safa and then Marwah) became obligatory. This obligation is derived from the practice of the Prophet (sunnah fi'liyyah), not from the text of the verse itself. The author draws an analogy with bequests: if a deceased person specifies multiple supererogatory charities, the executor should start with the one mentioned first, as that indicates stronger concern, giving it preponderance over others.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartib): Order or sequence; the lack of obligatory order from the waw in the verse, but the sequence in sa'y becomes obligatory due to other indications.
السعي (al-sa'y): The ritual walking between Safa and Marwah during Hajj or Umrah; its obligation is from the Quran but sequence is from Sunnah.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): Giving preponderance to one element over another based on an indication (qarinah), such as priority in mention indicating strength of concern.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the verse about Safa and Marwah (al-Baqarah 2:158) not impose an order for sa'y?
A) Because the verse only affirms that they are among Allah's symbols, not the act of sa'y itself.
B) Because the waw in the verse is a waw of isti'naf (resumption), not conjunction.
C) Because the verse is abrogated by other texts.
D) Because the word 'safa' comes before 'marwah' indicating order.

Key Takeaway
The conjunction waw does not impose sequence; the order in sa'y is wajib due to the Prophet's practice, supported by tarjih from the order of mention.

Ta'liq and Irsal: A Ruling Existing Through Two Ways
Explaining how a single ruling can be both conditioned and unconditioned before its existence, and critiquing Shafi'i on kaffarat al-yamin sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a ruling can be both suspended and released before its existence, and evaluate the Hanafi critique of al-Shafi'i's position on the consecutiveness of kaffarat al-yamin.

Core Idea
A ruling can exist as a possibility through two contradictory descriptions before it becomes actual.

In this passage, the author deepens the concept that ta'liq (suspension by a condition) does not negate the ruling absolutely. Instead, a single ruling can be both mu'allaq (suspended) and mursal (released) before it comes into existence. He illustrates this with the permissibility of marrying a slave woman, then applies the reasoning to critique Imam al-Shafi'i's stance on kaffarat al-yamin. Finally, he notes that rulings are divided into 'azima and rukhsa.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle: ta'liq does not negate
The author builds on a previous assertion that tying a ruling to a condition does not imply negation of the ruling before the condition occurs. Instead, the ruling remains possible in two ways: suspended (tied to the condition) and released (absolute).
This is a foundational Hanafi usul principle about the nature of conditional rulings.
2
Examine the example: marrying a slave woman
One text (Quran 4:25) permits marrying a slave woman only if one lacks the means to marry a free woman. Another text (general permission) seems to allow it absolutely. Before any actual marriage, the ruling exists as both conditional and unconditional possibilities.
The two descriptions are contradictory in actual existence but not in potential existence.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i contradiction on kaffarat al-yamin
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the fast for kaffarat al-yamin need not be consecutive, but he agrees that the fast for zihar and manslaughter must be consecutive. The author argues this is inconsistent because the same reasoning should apply to all expiations.
The Hanafi position is that all three expiations require consecutive fasts.
4
Refute the objection from tamattu' fast
Al-Shafi'i might object that the tamattu' fast (during Hajj) must be scattered, showing that scattering is sometimes required. The author replies that the scattering is due to time restriction (the fast must be before slaughter, which has a window), not because non-consecutiveness is obligatory.
The tamattu' fast is tied to a specific time by the word 'when', similar to Zuhr prayer's time tie.
5
Identify the classification into 'azima and rukhsa
The passage ends by stating that rulings are divided into 'azima (original obligation) and rukhsa (concession). This is a separate but related topic.
The author merely introduces the division; the lesson focuses on the earlier part.

Source Text

وهذا نظير ما سبق أنا قلنا إن التعليق بالشرط لا يوجب النفي، فصار الحكم الواحد معلقاً ومرسلاً، مثل جواز نكاح الأمة، تعلَّق بعدم طَوْل الحرة بالنص، وبقي مرسلاً ذلك ؛ لأن الإرسال والتعليق يتنافيان وجوداً، فأما قبل ابتداء وجوده فهو معلّق أي معدوم يتعلق بالشرط وجوده، ومرسل عن الشرط، أي محتمل للوجود قبله، والعدم الأصلي كان محتملاً للوجود ، ولم يتبدل العدم، فصار محتملاً للوجود بطريقين، وذلك جائز في كل حكم قبل وجوده بطريقين وطرق كثيرة. وقد قال الشافعي رحمه الله : إن صوم كفارة اليمين غير متتابع، ولم يَحمله على الظهار، والقتل، وهذا تناقض 19 พ ห فإن قال : إن الأصل متعارض؛ لأني وجدتُ صوم المتعة لا يصح إلا متفرقاً. قيل له : ليس كذلك، فإن صومَ السبعة قبل أيام النحر : لا يجوز؛ لأنه لم يُشرع ، لا لأن التفريق واجب، ألا يُرى أنه أُضيف إلى وقت بكلمة: إذا، فكان كالظهر لما أُضيف إلى وقتِ: لم يكن مشروعاً قبله، وذلك لمعنى ذكرناه في موضعه. وأحكام هذه الأقسام تنقسم إلى قسمين إلى العزيمة، والرخصة، وهذا باب العزيمة والرخصة، والله أعلم.
And this is analogous to what we previously said: that suspension by a condition does not necessitate negation. So a single ruling becomes both suspended and released, like the permissibility of marrying a slave woman: it is suspended by the absence of means (tawla) to marry a free woman by one text, and it remains released because release and suspension are contradictory in existence. But before the beginning of its existence, it is suspended, i.e., non-existent, its existence tied to the condition, and released from the condition, i.e., possible to exist before it. The original non-existence was possible for existence, and the non-existence did not change, so it became possible for existence through two ways. And that is permissible for every ruling before its existence through two ways and many ways. And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: the fast of the expiation of an oath is not consecutive, and he did not analogize it to zihar and killing, and this is contradictory. For if he says: the principle is conflicting because I found that the fast of tamattu' is not valid except when scattered. It is said to him: not so, for the fast of seven days before the days of sacrifice is not permissible because it has not been legislated, not because scattering is obligatory. Do you not see that it is attached to a time by the word 'when', so it is like the Zuhr prayer when attached to a time: it was not legislated before it. And that is for a meaning we mentioned in its place. And the rulings of these categories are divided into two divisions: 'azima (resolve) and rukhsa (concession). And this is the chapter of 'azima and rukhsa. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the principle that a single ruling can be both tied to a condition and left general before it comes into existence. The author uses the example of marrying a slave woman: by one text, it is permitted only when one lacks the means to marry a free woman; by another text, it is permitted absolutely. Since the ruling is not yet in effect, it can be possible through both ways without contradiction. The author then criticizes Imam al-Shafi'i for inconsistency: Shafi'i holds that the fast for kaffarat al-yamin is not consecutive, yet he does not extend this rule to zihar or manslaughter. A hypothetical objection about tamattu' fast (which must be scattered) is answered: the prohibition of fasting before the days of sacrifice is because it is not legislated at that time, not because scattering is obligatory. This is like prayer times. Finally, the passage introduces the classification of rulings into 'azima (original obligation) and rukhsa (concession).

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling's existence on a condition; the ruling is considered non-existent until the condition occurs.
إرسال (irsal): Releasing a ruling from any condition; an absolute ruling that is not tied to any condition.
عزيمة ('azima): A firm ruling that is the original obligation without concession.
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession that relaxes the original ruling due to a valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what is the status of the permissibility of marrying a slave woman before one actually marries?
A) It is absolutely prohibited until the condition of lacking means is met.
B) It is absolutely permitted without any condition.
C) It is both conditionally permitted (if lacking means) and unconditionally permitted (as a possibility) via two different texts.
D) It is only permitted if one lacks means.

Key Takeaway
A ruling before it exists can be described by multiple, even contradictory, conditions because non-existence allows for multiple possibilities; this principle reconciles apparently conflicting texts.

Naskh — Abrogation

The Fourth Type of Rukhsah: Dropped while Remaining Legislated
Defines the fourth type of rukhsah and provides three examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the fourth type of rukhsah (rukhsah majaziyyah) as an abrogation, distinguish it from true rukhsah, and apply the definition to cases such as salam, ikrah, and qasr al-salah.

Core Idea
The fourth rukhsah is an abrogation: the original ruling is entirely dropped while its legislative basis remains.

Al-Bazdawi explores the fourth division of rukhsah (license) in Hanafi usul: those concessions that are, in essence, abrogations (naskh) of the original ruling, yet they are still considered legislated in principle. He calls this a 'metaphorical license' (rukhsah majaziyyah) because it resembles a true license but is really an annulment of the original obligation. This lesson unpacks his definition and walks through three key examples: salam (forward sale), coercion to consume prohibited items, and shortening prayer while traveling. We will see how each example illustrates the concept and what evidence supports it.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definitional structure
Understand that the fourth rukhsah is defined by two aspects: it is dropped (original ruling abrogated) and it remains legislated in general terms (the principle is still on the books). Note that it is called 'majaz' (figurative) because it is not a true license but an abrogation.
Focus on the duality of being 'sāqiṭ' (dropped) and 'mashrūʿ' (legislated).
2
Analyze the first example: salam
Examine how the original requirement that a sale object must exist at the time of contract is entirely dropped in salam. This is an abrogation of that condition, but the principle of sale remains legislated overall.
The hadith of Ibn Abbas is the evidence for the license in salam.
3
Analyze the second example: coercion to drink wine or eat carrion
Understand that the prohibition is dropped entirely under coercion, but the prohibition remains legislated for those not coerced. Identify the reasoning: preserving the whole (life) over the part (prohibition).
The ruling here is based on the legal maxim: 'Necessity permits the prohibited' (al-ḍarūrāt tubīḥ al-maḥẓūrāt).
4
Analyze the third example: qasr al-salah
See how the full prayer obligation is entirely replaced by the shortened prayer for the traveler. The hadith calling it a 'charity' indicates it is a pure abrogation (isqat) that cannot be refused.
Al-Bazdawi uses the term 'ṣadaqah' to argue that qasr is obligatory and no option remains.
5
Distinguish this from true rukhsah
Compare the fourth type to true rukhsah (like wiping over socks in wudu) where the original ruling is still valid but a concession is given. The fourth type is weaker because it is an abrogation, not a genuine concession.
True rukhsah: original ruling remains as a valid option. Metaphorical rukhsah: original ruling is completely dropped.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-٤- وأما القسم الرابع: فما سقط عن العباد مع كونه مشروعاً في الجملة. فمن حيث سقط أصلاً كان مجازاً، ومن حيث بقي مشروعاً في الجملة: كان شبيهاً بحقيقة الرخصة، فكان دون القسم الثالث. مثاله: ما أن روي النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام رخص في السلم، وذلك لأن أصل البيع أن يُلاقي عيناً موجودةً، وهذا حكم باق مشروع، لكنه سقط في باب السلم أصلاً؛ تخفيفاً، حتى لم يبق تعيينه في السلم مشروعاً، ولا عزيمة، وهذا لأن دليل اليُسر متعيّن؛ لوقوع العجز عن التعيين، فوضع عنه أصلاً.
(1) حديث: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رخص في السلم: قال القرطبي شرح مسلم: ومما يدل على اشتراط الأجل في السلم الحديث الذي قال فيه: نهى: رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن بيع ما ليس عندك، وأرخص في السلم». انتهى. ولم أقف عليه هكذا، وعندي أنه مركب، فحديث النهي: هو حديث حكيم بن حزام، وحديث الرخصة: هو حديث ابن عباس قال: قَدِمَ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم المدينة وهم يُسلفون في الثمار السَّنَةَ، والسنتين، فقال: من أسلف في تمر: فليسلف في كيل معلوم، ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم». رواه الجماعة. 6
وكذلك المُكرَهُ على شرب الخمر، أو أكل الميتة، أو المضطر إليهما رخصة مجازاً؛ لأن الحرمة ساقطة، حتى إذا صبر: صار آثماً؛ لأن حرمته ما ثبتت إلا صيانةً لعقله ودينه عن فساد الخمر، ونفسه عن الميتة، فإذا خاف به فوات نفسه: لم تستقم صيانةُ البعض بفوات الكل، فسقط المحرم، وكان هذا إسقاطاً لحرمته، فإذا صبر: لم يصر مؤدياً حق الله تعالى، فكان مضيّعاً دمه، إلا أن حرمة هذه الأشياء مشروعة في الجملة.
- ومن ذلك: ما قلنا في قصر الصلاة بالسفر: إنه رخصة إسقاط، حتى لا يصح أداؤه من المسافر، وإنما جعلناها إسقاطاً؛ استدلالاً بدليل الرخصة، ومعناها. أما الدليل: فما روي «أن عمر رضي الله عنه قال: أنقصر الصلاة ونحن آمنون؟ فقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: إن هذه صدقة تصدق الله بها عليكم، فاقبلوا صدقته»(1). سماه صدقةً، والتصدق بما لا يحتمل التمليك: إسقاط محض، لا يحتمل الرد، وإن كان المتصدق مَن لا تلزم طاعته، كولي القصاص إذا عفا، فمِمَّن تلزم طاعته: أولى.
(1) حديث عمر: أنقصر الصلاة ونحن آمنون ... [بياض في الأصل]... عن يعلى بن أمية قال: قلت لعمر بن الخطاب: ﴿فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن نَقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلَوَةِ إِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَن يَفْيْنَكُمُ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا: فقد أَمِنَ الناس؟ فقال: عجبتُ مما عجبت منه، فسألتُ النبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عن ذلك، فقال: صدقة تصدق الله بها عليكم فاقبلوا صدقته». رواه الجماعة إلا البخاري.
And as for the fourth division: it is what has fallen away from the servants while being legislated in general terms. So from the perspective that it fell away entirely, it is metaphorical (majaz), and from the perspective that it remains legislated in general terms, it resembles the true license (rukhsah), so it is below the third division. Its example: what has been narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) granted a license in salam (forward sale). That is because the original principle of sale is that it should meet an existing object, and this ruling remains legislated, but it fell away entirely in the chapter of salam as a concession, so much so that its specification in salam is no longer legislated, nor is it a determination (azima). This is because the evidence of ease is specified due to the inability to specify, so it was removed entirely.
(1) Hadith: The Prophet (peace be upon him) granted a license in salam. Al-Qurtubi said in Sharh Muslim: Among the evidence for stipulating a term in salam is the hadith in which he said: 'The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) forbade selling what is not with you, and he granted a license in salam.' End. I have not come across it thus, and in my view it is composite: the hadith of prohibition is the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam, and the hadith of license is the hadith of Ibn Abbas who said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) arrived in Medina when they were paying in advance for fruits for one or two years. He said: 'Whoever pays in advance for dates, let him pay in advance for a known measure, a known weight, and a known term.' Narrated by the group.
And likewise, the one coerced to drink wine or eat carrion, or the one compelled to do these, is a metaphorical license because prohibition has fallen away, so that if he endures, he becomes sinful. This is because its prohibition was only established to safeguard his intellect and religion from the corruption of wine, and his self from carrion. So if he fears losing his life through it, preserving the part by losing the whole is not sound, so the prohibited thing falls away, and this is an abrogation of its prohibition. If he endures, he does not fulfill the right of God Most High, so he becomes negligent of his life, except that the prohibition of these things is legislated in general terms.
And from that is what we said about shortening prayer while traveling: it is a license of abrogation, such that it is not valid for the traveler to perform it (fully), and we only made it an abrogation by inference from the evidence of the license and its meaning. As for the evidence: what is narrated that Umar (may God be pleased with him) said: 'We shorten prayer while we are safe?' The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'This is a charity that God has bestowed upon you, so accept His charity.' He called it a charity, and giving charity of something that cannot be owned is pure abrogation, not subject to refusal. And if the giver is one whose obedience is not obligatory, like the guardian in retaliation if he pardons, then from one whose obedience is obligatory it is more fitting.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh discusses the fourth type of rukhsah (license/concession) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author classifies rukhsah into categories, and here he explains the fourth: concessions that are entirely dropped (the original obligation is lifted) while the ruling remains legislated in principle (i.e., it could still be followed theoretically, but that would be inappropriate). This makes it a figurative license (majaz) because in reality it is an abrogation (naskh) of the original ruling, though it resembles a true license in that the original ruling is still 'on the books'. It is considered weaker than the third type (which is a true license).

Three examples are given:
1. Salam (forward sale): In a normal sale, the object must exist at the time of contract. This is the general rule. But in salam, this condition is dropped entirely as a concession ('ala wajh al-takhfif). The hadith evidence includes the Prophet's permission for salam despite the general prohibition of selling what one does not possess.
2. Coercion (ikrah) to drink wine or eat carrion: Normally these are prohibited. But if one is coerced or compelled under threat of death, the prohibition is dropped. If the person endures and does not consume, they become sinful because they are harming themselves. The reasoning is that the prohibition was to protect one's life and religion; if life is at stake, preserving a part by losing the whole is not appropriate. So the prohibition falls entirely.
3. Shortening prayer while traveling (qasr al-salah): The author argues that this is a license of abrogation because the traveler is not permitted to perform the full prayer; the shortened prayer is the only valid option. The evidence is the hadith where the Prophet calls it a 'charity' from God, and charity is a gift that cannot be returned, indicating it is a complete dropping of the full prayer obligation.

The commentator also includes footnotes discussing the hadith texts and their authenticity.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsah): حكم شرعي ثبت على خلاف الدليل لعذر مع بقاء العزيمة مشروعة
عزيمة (azimah): الحكم الأصلي الثابت بدليل شرعي غير مرخص فيه
سلم (salam): بيع مؤجل الثمن معجل المثمن (forward sale) رخص فيه الشارع
قصر الصلاة (qasr al-salat): صلاة الرباعية ركعتين في السفر
إسقاط (isqat): إزالة الحكم بالكلية؛ الإلغاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the traveler may choose to perform the full prayer
B) Because the original obligation of full prayer is entirely dropped and the shortened prayer is the only valid option
C) Because it is a dispensation for hardship
D) Because the traveler is excused from prayer altogether

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of rukhsah is essentially an abrogation of the original ruling, with the concession being the only valid option, such as qasr for the traveler.

The Closure of the Door of Opinion: A Hanafi Usul Rule
Explaining the Hanafi rule on when hadith overrides qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of insidad bab al-ra'y and apply it to determine when a hadith overrides other proofs.

Core Idea
When the door of opinion closes, one hadith can abrogate the Quran and oppose consensus.

This passage from al-Bazdawi’s Uṣūl clarifies a cornerstone of Hanafi legal epistemology: the priority of hadith over analogical reasoning under specific conditions. Here, the author defends the school against accusations of rejecting hadith, explaining that a hadith from a narrator like Abū Hurayra is only set aside when there is room for rational opinion (ra'y). Once the door of opinion closes, the hadith gains supreme authority, even over the Quran and ijma'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Look for the phrase 'إذا انسد باب الرأي والقياس' (when the door of opinion and analogy is closed). This is the key trigger.
This condition is specific to Hanafi usul.
2
Determine the effect
When the condition is met, the hadith becomes nasikh (abrogating) for the Quran and well-known sunnah, and mu'arid (opposing) for consensus.
The hadith overrides proofs that are normally stronger.
3
Analyze the example
The hadith of al-musarra (a milked animal) is cited as a case where the door of opinion was closed, so the hadith took full effect.
No rational opinion could override this hadith.
4
Contextualize the defense
Note that the author refutes claims that Hanafis reject hadith; they conditionally apply ra'y when possible.
The madhhab respects hadith narrators like Abū Hurayra.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يذهب عليه شيء من معانيه بنقله، فتدخله شبهةً زائدة يخلو عنها القياس، فيحتاط في مثله. وإنما نعني بما قلنا : قصوراً عند المقابلة بفقه الحديث، فأما الازدراء بهم : فمعاذ الله ذلك، فإن محمداً من رحمه الله حكى عن أبي حنيفة رضي بن مالك رضي الله عنه، الله عنه في غير موضع أنه احتج بمذهب أنس وقلده، فما ظنُّك في أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه؟! حتى إن المذهب عند أصحابنا رحمهم الله في ذلك : أنه لا يُرَدُّ حديث أمثالهم إلا إذا انسدَّ باب الرأي والقياس؛ لأنه إذا انسد: صار الحديث ناسخاً للكتاب، والحديث المشهور، ومعارِضاً للإجماع. وذلك مثل حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه في المصراة): أنه انسدَّ فيه باب الرأي، فصار ناسخاً للكتاب والسنَّة المعروفة، معارضاً للإجماع.
Something goes out from its meanings by transmission, so an extra doubt enters it that analogy is free from, so caution is exercised in its like. And we mean by what we said: deficiency when compared to fiqh of hadith. As for disparaging them: God forbid! For Muhammad (ibn al-Hasan) narrated from Abu Hanifa that he used the school of Anas as proof and followed him, so what do you think about Abu Huraira? Indeed the madhhab of our companions in that is: The hadith of their likes is not rejected unless the door of opinion and analogy is closed; because when it is closed, the hadith becomes abrogating for the Book, the well-known sunnah, and opposing consensus. And that is like the hadith of Abu Huraira regarding al-musarra: that the door of opinion was closed in it, so it became abrogating for the Book and the known sunnah, opposing consensus.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Hanafi position on accepting hadith from certain narrators, particularly those who might be considered less reliable in comparison to other hadith scholars. The author defends Abu Hanifa's methodology, stating that he did not disparage hadith but rather gave priority to opinion and qiyas when the hadith was open to interpretation. However, when the door of opinion is closed—meaning there is no room for rational deduction—then the hadith takes full authority, even to the extent of abrogating clear Qur'anic verses and well-established sunnah, and contradicting consensus. The example given is the hadith of Abu Huraira regarding the musarra (a milked animal). This hadith became operative in naskh because no rational opinion could override it. Students should note that this is a nuanced position: it is not that Hanafis reject hadith of such narrators; rather, they test it against the possibility of ra'y.

Key Terms
انسداد باب الرأي (insidad bab al-ra'y): The closure of the door of opinion, meaning no rational or analogical reasoning can be applied
ناسخ (nasikh): Abrogating; a later revealed text that supersedes an earlier one
معارض (mu'arid): Opposing; a proof that contradicts another proof, here opposing ijma'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul presented, when does the hadith of a narrator like Abu Huraira become abrogating for the Quran and well-known sunnah?
A) When the narrator is considered weak
B) When the door of opinion and analogy is closed
C) When it contradicts consensus
D) When the hadith is narrated by a single chain

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule on closure of the door of opinion (insidad bab al-ra'y) determines when a hadith can override even the Quran and ijma'.

When a Khabar al-Wahid Contradicts the Well-Known Sunnah
Explaining when a khabar al-wahid can abrogate a well-known sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second type of khabar al-wahid that abrogates the well-known sunnah and apply it to the shahid wa yamin and Sa'd hadith examples.

Core Idea
A khabar al-wahid that clearly contradicts the well-known sunnah can abrogate it, provided the contradiction is explicit.

In Hanafi usul, a khabar al-wahid normally cannot abrogate a stronger sunnah (mashhur). However, the author identifies an exception: when the khabar al-wahid directly and explicitly contradicts the well-known sunnah, it can serve as an abrogator. This lesson examines the rule and two key examples from the passage: the hadith of shahid wa yamin and the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas regarding dates.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule on abrogation of mashhur
Recall that a khabar al-wahid is weaker than a mashhur sunnah, so normally it cannot abrogate it. The passage presents an exception: when the khabar al-wahid contradicts the mashhur sunnah clearly, it can abrogate.
This is the second type of khabar al-wahid that abrogates, following the first type (which abrogates based on its own strength).
2
Identify the contradiction in the shahid wa yamin example
The well-known rule is 'The evidence is upon the plaintiff, and the oath is upon the defendant.' The hadith allowing the plaintiff to swear together with a witness contradicts this by shifting the oath to the plaintiff. The author argues that the contradiction is explicit and thus the hadith abrogates the well-known rule for that specific case.
The passage also cites Quranic evidence (Al-Ma'idah 5:106) that mentions the oath of a witness, reinforcing that the shahid wa yamin hadith is clear.
3
Analyze the Sa'd hadith example
The well-known rule is that dates must be exchanged in equal quantity (riba hadith). The hadith of Sa'd permits selling fresh dates for dried dates with an increase in quality, which contradicts the equality requirement. The author states that this hadith abrogates the well-known rule because the increase is in quality, not quantity.
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad interpret the hadith differently, saying that the term 'dates' does not apply to fresh dates in common usage, so the riba rule does not apply. This is a legitimate difference in usul methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك ما خالف السُّنَّةَ المشهورة أيضاً؛ لما قلنا إنه فوقه، فلا يُنسخ به. وذلك مثل حديث الشاهد واليمين؛ لأنه خالف المشهور، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «البينة على المدعي، واليمين على مَن أنكر»، يعني على المدعى عليه. ولأنه ذكَر في ذلك يمين الشاهد بقوله : «لو فَيُقْسِمان بالله» ان المائدة / ١٠٦، ويمين الخصم في الجملة مشروع، فأما يمين الشاهد فغير مشروع، فصار النقل إلى يمين الشاهد في غاية البيان، بأن يمين المدعي ليست بحجة، وأمثال هذا كثيرة. ومثل حديث سعد بن أبي وقاص رضي الله عنه في بيع التمر بالرُّطب: مخالف لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «التمر بالتمر» : بزيادة مماثلة هي ناسخة للمشهور؛ باعتبار جودة ليست من المقدار. إلا أن أبا يوسف ومحمداً رحمهما الله عملا به على أن اسم التمر لا يتناول الرُّطَب في العادة، كما في اليمين.
And likewise, what contradicts the well-known Sunnah as well; because we said it is above it, so it is not abrogated by it. And that is like the hadith of the witness and the oath; because it contradicts the well-known (sunnah), which is his statement (peace be upon him): 'The evidence is upon the plaintiff, and the oath is upon the one who denies,' meaning upon the defendant. And because he mentioned in that the oath of the witness by his statement: 'They shall both swear by Allah' [Al-Ma'idah 5:106], and the oath of the opponent is generally legislated, but as for the oath of the witness, it is not legislated. So the transfer to the oath of the witness is extremely clear: that the oath of the plaintiff is not a proof. And many examples like this. And like the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding the sale of fresh dates for dried dates: it contradicts his statement (peace be upon him): 'Dates for dates,' with an increase similar (in quality) that is not from the quantity, so it is an abrogator of the well-known (rule); considering it is of a quality not from the quantity. Except that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) acted upon it on the basis that the name 'dates' does not apply to fresh dates in common usage, as in the case of oaths.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage explains a specific category of khabar al-wahid that can abrogate a well-known sunnah: one that clearly contradicts it. The author gives two examples: the hadith allowing the plaintiff to swear together with a witness (shahid wa yamin) which contradicts the general principle that the oath is on the defendant. He argues that the hadith is clear enough to abrogate. The second example is the hadith of Sa'd that permits selling fresh dates for dried dates in certain conditions, which appears to contradict the riba hadith requiring equality. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad interpreted this hadith differently, but the point here is that it is a valid abrogator because it contradicts the well-known rule.

Key Terms
السنة المشهورة (al-sunnah al-mashhurah): The well-known sunnah that is transmitted by many but not reaching tawatur; it is stronger than khabar al-wahid but can be abrogated by a contradictory khabar al-wahid under certain conditions.
اليمين (al-yamin): In this context, the oath taken by a witness or plaintiff; the hadith allows combining witness and oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A khabar al-wahid that supports the well-known sunnah
B) A khabar al-wahid that contradicts the well-known sunnah
C) A khabar al-wahid that is from a reliable narrator
D) A khabar al-wahid that is widely transmitted

Key Takeaway
A khabar al-wahid that clearly contradicts the well-known sunnah can abrogate it, exemplifying a nuanced approach to naskh in Hanafi usul.

Famous Incident with Obscure Hadith: An Indication of Forgetfulness
Setting a principle for reconciling practice with rare hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that the fame of a legal incident with an obscure hadith indicates forgetfulness to evaluate the strength of a practice.

Core Idea
A famous practice is not invalidated by a rare hadith—forgetfulness explains the obscurity.

In Hanafi usul, when a legal practice (incident) is widely known among the Companions but the supporting hadith is obscure, we do not reject the practice. Instead, we infer that the hadith was once known but later forgotten. This lesson unpacks that rational proof and its application, using the example of reciting Basmala aloud in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the incident and the hadith
From the passage, note that the incident is the practice that became famous among the Companions (e.g., reciting Basmala aloud). The hadith is the textual evidence that supports it but is obscure in transmission.
The incident is often a widespread practice, while the hadith is rarely narrated.
2
Contrast fame with obscurity
The passage asserts that if the incident is famous, it is impossible (istihala) that the supporting evidence (hadith) could have been unknown to the early community. Therefore, the obscurity of the hadith is attributed to forgetfulness (sahw).
The reasoning is based on the impossibility of a widely practiced ruling having no known evidence.
3
Understand the rational proof
The author uses a rational argument (dalil aqli): if the incident is famous among the Companions, they must have known the evidence. If the hadith is now obscure, it indicates that they forgot it, not that it never existed.
This principle protects authentic practices from being discarded due to later lack of textual memory.
4
Apply the example of the Basmala
The hadith about pronouncing Basmala aloud in prayer is not widely transmitted, yet the practice was common among the Companions. According to the principle, this indicates that the hadith was known to them but later forgotten, so the practice remains valid.
This example shows how the principle works in practice.
5
Evaluate the strength of the practice
The fame of the incident itself becomes a source of authority. The practice is upheld despite the hadith's obscurity.
The principle is a tool for reconciling widespread practice with weak or rare textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث: فلأن الحادثة إذا اشتهرت، وخفي الحديث: كان ذلك دلالةً على السهو؛ لأن الحادثة إذا اشتهرت: استحال أن يخفى عليهم ما يثبت به حكم الحادثة. ألا يُرى أنه كيف اشتهر في الخَلَف، فإذا شدّ الحديث مع الحادثة: كان ذلك زيافةً وانقطاعاً. وذلك مثل حديث الجهر بالتسمية.
As for the third type: because when an incident becomes famous, and the hadith is obscure, that is an indication of forgetfulness; because when the incident is famous, it is impossible that what establishes the ruling of the incident should be hidden from them. Do you not see how it became famous among the later generations, so if the hadith is joined with the incident, that would be a distortion and disconnection? And that is like the hadith of pronouncing the Basmala aloud.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage discusses a third category: when a particular legal practice (like reciting Basmala aloud in prayer) became widespread among the Companions, but the hadith supporting it is rare (khafiyya). This suggests that the hadith was known but later forgotten, and the practice persisted. The argument is that if the hadith was fabricated, it would not have been widely practiced. Thus, the obscurity of the hadith does not weaken the practice; rather, it shows that the hadith was originally known but later forgotten, and the practice remains valid based on the incident.

Key Terms
الحادثة (al-hadithah): A legal incident or practice that occurred in the time of the Companions.
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness or error; here it refers to the possibility that the Companions forgot the hadith despite the practice being well-known.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The scholars are in disagreement

Key Takeaway
The fame of a practice among the Companions is strong evidence, and if the supporting hadith is obscure, it suggests forgetfulness, not weakness.
