The Role of Custom in Determining the Scope of Legal Terms: Fresh vs. Dried Dates
Usul rule on naskh and linguistic custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how custom (ʿurf) restricts the application of a general text and evaluate the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on naskh based on linguistic scope.

Core Idea
Custom determines the scope of words; a general text does not apply where custom excludes the case.

This lesson explores a classic usul dispute: does the hadith prohibiting usury in date-for-date exchange abrogate the ruling for fresh dates? The answer hinges on whether the term 'tamr' includes 'ruttab' according to custom. Abu Hanifa upholds the literal general meaning, while the two companions rely on customary usage to restrict the term's scope.

Guided Path
1
State the well-known ruling and the hadith
Recall that the well-known ruling prohibits disparity in quantity when exchanging dates for dates of the same type. The hadith states 'al-tamr bi al-tamr... illa ma ikhtalafat alwanuhu' (dates for dates... except when colors differ).
The hadith is from Abu Hurairah, as cited in the footnote.
2
Identify the point of abrogation
Abu Hanifa argues that the hadith adds a similarity in quality (joodah) as an exception analogous to color difference, thus abrogating the earlier well-known rule.
The text says 'bi ziyada mumathala hiya nasikha li al-mashhur' (with an added similarity that is abrogating the well-known).
3
Understand the opponents' argument
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the hadith does not apply because the term 'tamr' does not customarily include 'ruttab' (fresh dates). They draw an analogy to oaths: if one swears not to eat 'tamr', eating 'ruttab' does not break the oath.
This is based on 'urf (customary usage).
4
Compare the two positions
Abu Hanifa prioritizes the literal generality of the text, while the Sahibayn prioritize the customary linguistic scope. Both are valid usul methodologies.
The text indicates the Sahibayn 'acted upon it' (wa 'amila bihi), suggesting their view was operative.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, exchanging fresh dates for dried dates with a disparity is riba. Under the Sahibayn, it is permissible because they are considered different types according to custom.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالرُّطب : مخالف لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «التمر بالتمر» (۱) : بزيادة مماثلة هي ناسخة للمشهور ؛ باعتبار جودة ليست من المقدار. إلا أن أبا يوسف ومحمداً رحمهما الله عملا به على أن اسم التمر لا يتناول الرُّطَب في العادة، كما في اليمين.
(۱) حديث التمر بالتمر عن أبي هريرة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: التمر بالتمر، والحنطة بالحنطة والشعير بالشعير، والملح بالملح، مثلاً بمثل، يداً بيد، فمن زاد أو استزاد : فقد أربى إلا ما اختلفت ألوانه. رواه مسلم. ورواه مسلم وأحمد في أثناء حديث عبادة بن الصامت، ولفظه: «الذهب بالذهب والفضة بالفضة والبُرُّ بالبُرِّ، والشعير بالشعير، والتمر بالتمر، والملح بالملح مثلاً بمثل، سواء بسواء، يداً بيد، فإذا اختلفت هذه الأصناف: فبيعوا كيف شئتم إذا كان يداً بيد». وللبخاري من حديث أبي سعيد الخدري مثل حديث عبادة، إلا أنه قال بعد: (يداً بيد»: «فمن زاد أو استزاد : فقد أربى، الآخِذُ والمعطي فيه سواء».
Regarding fresh dates: contrary to his saying, peace be upon him, 'Dried dates for dried dates' (1): an additional similarity that is abrogating the well-known [ruling] due to quality not quantity. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, acted upon it because the name 'tamr' does not apply to 'ruttab' customarily, as in oaths.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Bazdawi's Usul addresses a usul rule concerning naskh (abrogation) by a hadith that contradicts the well-known practice. The issue is the exchange of fresh dates (ruttab) for dried dates (tamr). The well-known ruling, based on the hadith 'al-tamr bi al-tamr', prohibits disparity in quantity when exchanging dates of the same type. However, the hadith cited here is considered to abrogate that ruling by adding a similarity in quality, not in measure. But Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the two companions) did not accept this abrogation because customarily the word 'tamr' does not include 'ruttab', just as in the case of oaths: if one swears not to eat tamr, eating ruttab does not break the oath. The text presents this as an example of how the precise linguistic scope of a term can affect legal rulings, and it highlights the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Key Terms
نَسْخ (naskh): Abrogation of an earlier ruling by a later textual evidence.
مُمَاثَلَة (mumāthala): Similarity in quality, not quantity, in exchange.
عُرْف (ʿurf): Customary usage of language that determines the scope of terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadith is weak in authenticity
B) Because the term 'tamr' does not customarily include 'ruttab'
C) Because Abu Hanifa changed his opinion
D) Because the hadith contradicts the Quran

Key Takeaway
The scope of a general text is determined by the customary usage of the language; ʿurf can restrict the application of a shar'i ruling.

Companions' I'rad as Evidence of Naskh
Rule: Companions' i'rāḍ indicates naskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that Companions' turning away (i'rāḍ) from a hadith indicates its abrogation, and identify the conditions under which this principle operates.

Core Idea
When Companions ignore a relevant hadith, it is abrogated.

The Companions (ṣaḥābah) are the primary transmitters of the Sharīʿah. If a ḥadīth were valid and applicable, they would not rely solely on their own opinions when discussing a legal issue. Therefore, their complete disregard for a ḥadīth in a contested matter is strong evidence that the ḥadīth is either weak or abrogated. This passage explains this fourth category of evidence for naskh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the principle: the Companions' i'rāḍ (turning away) from a ḥadīth indicates its intisākh (abrogation).
2
Understand the rationale
Why does i'rāḍ serve as evidence? Because the ṣaḥābah are reliable transmitters; they would not ignore a valid text without reason.
This is a rational (ʿaqlī) proof.
3
Examine the example
The example given is the ḥadīth 'Divorce is with men, and the waiting period is with women.' The Companions differed on rulings involving this ḥadīth but none cited it.
Refer to the source: Ibn ʿAbbās, ʿUthmān, Zayd ibn Thābit, Ibn ʿUmar.
4
Apply the rule to the example
Since the Companions did not cite the ḥadīth despite its relevance, the Hanafī school concludes the ḥadīth is abrogated or unreliable.
5
Note the scope
This principle applies only when: (1) a legal issue is being debated, (2) a relevant ḥadīth exists, (3) none of the Companions present cites it.
Not mere silence; active disregard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الأخير: فلأن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم هم الأصول في نقل الشريعة، فإعراضهم يدلُّ على انقطاعه وانتساخه، وذلك أن يختلفوا حادثة بآرائهم ، ولم يحاج بعضُهم في ذلك بحديث : كان ذلك زيافةً ؛ لأن استعمال الرأي والإعراض عن النص : غير سائغ. وذلك مثل حديث: «الطلاق بالرجال، والعدة بالنساء»(۲)؛ أن الصحابة اختلفوا، ولم يرجعوا إليه(۳).
(۳) قوله : لأن الصحابة اختلفوا، ولم يرجعوا إليه أخرج ابن أبي شيبة عن ابن عباس الطلاق» بالرجال، والعدة بالنساء». وأخرج عن عثمان بن عفان وزيد بن ثابت أنهما قالا لنفيع : طلاقك طلاق عبد ، وعدتها عدة حرة، وكان نفيع مملوكاً تحته حرة. وأخرج عن ابن عمر مثله، وكذا أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ عنه، وأخرج ابن أبي
As for the last section: because the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, are the foundations in transmitting the Shariah; so their turning away indicates its discontinuity and abrogation. And that is when they differ on an incident by their opinions, and none of them argues with a hadith; that is a rejection because using opinion and turning away from the text is not permissible. And that is like the hadith: 'Divorce is with men, and waiting period is with women'; the Companions differed and did not refer to it.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth category: when the Companions themselves disagree on a legal issue and none of them cites a relevant hadith, that is evidence that the hadith is either weak or abrogated. The reasoning is that the Companions are the primary transmitters of the Shariah, so if a hadith existed and was valid, they would not rely solely on their opinion. Their complete disregard for the hadith indicates that it was known to be unreliable or abrogated. The example given is a hadith stating that divorce depends on the husband's status and the waiting period depends on the wife's status. Despite this hadith, the Companions like Ibn Abbas, Uthman, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Ibn Umar gave different rulings without citing it, proving that the hadith was not considered binding.

Key Terms
إِعْرَاض (iʿrāḍ): Turning away from a hadith by not citing it in relevant discussions.
انْتِسَاخ (intisākh): Abrogation or discontinuation of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is likely abrogated or unreliable
C) It is the basis for consensus
D) It is only applicable to later generations

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers the Companions' i'rāḍ (active disregard) of a ḥadīth in a relevant dispute to be compelling evidence that the ḥadīth is abrogated (mansūkh).

Companions' Disregard Indicates Abrogation
Explaining the principle of i'rad al-sahaba as an indicator of naskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of i'rad al-sahaba and identify its application in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Companions' collective disregard abrogates a hadith — the Sahaba are the ultimate transmitters.

This lesson explores a uniquely Hanafi usul principle: the practice of the Companions (Sahaba) can serve as an indicator that a particular hadith has been abrogated. The author provides two classic examples where the Sahaba differed on a ruling without referring to a known hadith, thereby showing its discontinuation. Understanding this principle helps determine which narrations are operative in the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
State what i'rad al-sahaba means: the Companions' turning away from a hadith, indicating its abrogation.
It is an indicator of naskh.
2
Understand the rationale
Explain why the Sahaba's practice is authoritative: they are the primary transmitters of the Shari'a, and their disregard suggests the hadith was abrogated.
Using opinion (ra'y) over a text is not permissible unless the text is no longer binding.
3
Analyze the examples
Examine the hadith of divorce ('al-talaq bi al-rijal') and the hadith on orphan's zakat. See how the Sahaba differed without recourse to these hadiths.
This demonstrates the principle in action.
4
Apply the principle to hypothetical cases
Consider a hadith that seems to contradict Sahaba's practice; determine if it is abrogated based on this principle.
The principle requires collective disregard, not isolated opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثل حديث مَس الذكر، وما أشبه ذلك.
وأما القسم الأخير: فلأن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم هم الأصول في نقل الشريعة، فإعراضهم يدل على انقطاعه وانتساخه، وذلك أن يختلفوا حادثة بآرائهم، ولم يحاج بعضهم في ذلك بحديث: كان ذلك زيافة؛ ولأن استعمال الرأي والإعراض عن النص غير سائغ.
وذلك مثل حديث: «الطلاق بالرجال، والعدة بالنساء»؛ أن الصحابة اختلفوا، ولم يرجعوا إليه.
و، بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم. وللنسائي عن نعيم المجمر: صليت وراء أبي هريرة، فقرأ بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم، ثم قرأ بأم القرآن حتى بلغ ولا الضالين فقال: آمين، ثم يقول إذا سلم: والذي نفسي بيده، إني لأشبهكم صلاة برسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم. صححه ابن خزيمة.
وهذان أمثل أحاديث الجهر والله أعلم، والثاني لا يستلزم ذلك؛ لجواز السماع مع الإخفاء للقريب، وفيه حجة عليهم؛ لعطفه أم القرآن على التسمية، والله أعلم.
وكذلك اختلفوا في زكاة الصبي، ولم يرجعوا إلى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ابتغوا في أموال اليتامى خيرا كي لا تأكلها الزكاة.
And like the hadith of touching the penis, and similar to that. As for the last section: because the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, are the foundations in transmitting the Sharia, so their turning away indicates its discontinuation and abrogation. That is, they differed on an incident with their opinions, and none of them argued therein with a hadith: that would be an addition; and because using opinion and turning away from the text is not permissible. And that is like the hadith: 'Divorce is by men, and the waiting period by women'; the Companions differed and did not refer back to it. And, in the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And al-Nasa'i from Nu'aym al-Mujmir: I prayed behind Abu Hurayra, and he recited 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim', then he recited Umm al-Quran until he reached 'nor of those who go astray' and said 'Ameen', then he would say when he finished: 'By the One in Whose hand is my soul, I am the one among you whose prayer most resembles that of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him.' Ibn Khuzayma authenticated it. And these are two examples of the hadiths on raising the voice, and Allah knows best, and the second does not necessitate that; because hearing with concealment for the near one is possible, and in it is an argument against them; because he linked Umm al-Quran to the basmalah, and Allah knows best. And similarly they differed on the zakat of a child, and did not revert to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Seek good in the wealth of orphans so that zakat does not consume it.'
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi usul principle: the practice of the Companions (Sahaba) is a crucial indicator of the status of a hadith. If the Sahaba universally ignore a hadith and rely on their own reasoning (ra'y) in a matter, this indicates that the hadith is abrogated or otherwise not applicable. The author presents two examples. First, the hadith 'Divorce is by men, and the waiting period by women'—despite its transmission, the Sahaba differed on its application and did not refer back to it, showing it was abrogated. Second, the issue of zakat on an orphan's property: the Sahaba again differed, ignoring the hadith 'Seek good in the wealth of orphans...', further supporting the principle. Additionally, the passage briefly touches on the hadith of jahr (raising the voice) in basmalah and Ameen, using the narration of Abu Hurayra as evidence for the Hanafi position. The author notes that the second narration does not conclusively prove jahr because hearing is possible even with low voice for those near, but it contains an argument against opponents by linking Umm al-Quran to basmalah.

Key Terms
إعراض الصحابة (i'rad al-sahaba): The act of the Companions turning away from a hadith, indicating its abrogation or discontinuity.
الطلاق بالرجال (al-talaq bi al-rijal): A hadith meaning that the power of divorce is with men, and the waiting period is with women.
زكاة الصبي (zakat al-sabi): Zakat on the property of a minor, specifically orphaned children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed, what does the collective disregard of a hadith by the Companions indicate?
A) The hadith is weak in transmission
B) The hadith is abrogated or discontinued
C) The Companions forgot the hadith
D) The hadith applies only to the Companions

Key Takeaway
The collective disregard of the Companions indicates abrogation (naskh) and is a key Hanafi principle for evaluating hadith.

Sahaba's Inaction as Evidence of Abrogation: The Exile and Related Examples
Explaining the first type of evidence from Sahaba's actions for naskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a well-known hadith abandoned by knowledgeable Sahaba indicates abrogation, and distinguish between rukhsah and azimah in the context of Sahaba's practices.

Core Idea
When a widely known hadith is not acted upon by major Sahaba, it signifies the ruling was abrogated.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a key Hanafi method for identifying naskh (abrogation): the non-action of a knowledgeable Sahabi on a ruling that should have been well-known. We will explore the examples of Umar's refusal to exile fornicators, Ali's comment on exile, the temporary marriage issue, and Ibn Mas'ud's choice of tathiq over placing hands on knees. These cases illustrate the principle that Sahaba's silence or alternative practice can indicate abrogation or a non-obligatory status, while also distinguishing between a concession (rukhsah) and the stronger practice (azimah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the key hadith and its ruling
Start by noting the hadith cited: 'The virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for a year.' This is a well-known (mashhur) hadith prescribing flogging and exile for unmarried fornicators.
The hadith is from Ubadah ibn al-Samit and is considered ahad but widely accepted.
2
Observe the Sahaba's actions and their knowledge
Umar swore he would never exile anyone, and Ali said exile is a fitna. Both were knowledgeable leaders, so the ruling could not have been unknown to them. Their inaction is not due to ignorance.
The passage argues that hudud are public and known to leaders.
3
Infer abrogation from their inaction
Since the ruling was widely known and yet they abandoned it, the only plausible explanation is that the ruling was abrogated (naskh) by another divine evidence.
This is the Hanafi method: 'tark al-sahabi al-mujtahid' as proof of naskh.
4
Distinguish between rukhsah and azimah
The objection about Ibn Mas'ud not placing hands on knees (rukbah) but interlocking (tathiq) is answered: he did not deny the practice but considered it a concession (rukhsah) and chose the stronger practice (azimah).
This shows that not all differences in practice indicate naskh; sometimes it is a choice between equally valid options.
5
Apply to other examples
Similarly, Umar's refusal to divide the conquered land of Sawad shows that the Prophet's division (if any) was not obligatory. Also, Muhammad ibn Sirin's comment on temporary marriage indicates that the Sahaba witnessed it and then forbade it, again pointing to naskh.
These examples reinforce the principle across different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القسم الأول، فمثال ذلك : أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : البكر بالبكر، جلد مائة، وتغريب عام. فقد حلف عمر رضي الله عنه أن لا ينفي أحداً أبداً. وقال علي رضي الله عنه : كفى بالنفي فتنة وهذا من جنس ما لا يحتمل الخفاء عليهما ؛ لأن إقامة الحدود من أئمة حَطّ الأئمة، ومبناه على الشهرة وعمر وعلي رضي الله عنهما من الهدى، فلو صح : لما خفي، وهذا لأنا تلقينا الدين منهم، فيبعد أن يخفى عليهم، فيُحمل ذلك على الانتساخ. وكذلك لما امتنع عمر رضي الله عنه القسمة في سواد العراق: علم أن القسمة من رسول الله عليه الصلاة والسلام لم تكن حتماً. وقال محمد بن سيرين رحمه الله في متعة النساء : هم شهدوا بها، وهم نَهَوا عنها، وما عن رأيهم رغبةٌ ، ولا في نُصحهم تُهَمَةٌ. فإن قيل: إن ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه لم يعمل بأخذ الرُّكَب، بل عمل بالتطبيق، ولم يوجب جرحاً. قلنا : لأنه لم يُنكر الوضع، لكنه رآه رخصة، ورأى التطبيق عزيمة، والعزيمة أولى، إلا أن ذلك رخصة إسقاط عندنا. ومثال القسم الآخر : ما روي عن أبي موسى الأشعري رضي الله عنه لعمر : اقسمه بيننا، فإنا فتحناه عنوة، قال: فأبى، ثم أقرَّ أهلَ السَّواد على أرضهم، وضرب على رؤوسهم الجزية، وعلى أرضهم الخراج.
As for the first section, an example of that is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for a year.' Then Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) swore that he would never exile anyone. And Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Exile is sufficient as a trial.' This is of the type that cannot remain hidden from them because the implementation of hudud is the duty of the leaders, and it is based on widespread knowledge, and Umar and Ali were guided. So if it (the ruling) were sound, it would not have been hidden from them. This is because we received the religion from them, so it is unlikely that it would be hidden from them; therefore, it is attributed to abrogation. Similarly, when Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) refrained from dividing the Sawad of Iraq, it was known that division from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was not obligatory. Muhammad ibn Sirin (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding temporary marriage: 'They witnessed it, and they forbade it. We do not turn away from their opinion, nor do we suspect their sincerity.' If it is said: Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) did not practice taking the knees, but practiced interlocking (the hands), and he did not deem it a wound (on the hadith). We say: He did not deny the position, but he considered it a concession, and considered interlocking as the stronger duty, and the stronger duty is preferable, except that is a concession of omission with us. And an example of the other section: what is narrated from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari (may Allah be pleased with him) to Umar: 'Divide it among us, for we conquered it by force.' He said: He refused, then he confirmed the people of Sawad on their land, and imposed jizya on their heads and kharaj on their land.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents two types of evidence from the actions of the Sahaba for inferring abrogation (naskh) or the non-obligatory nature of a ruling. The first type: when a hadith is well-known and widespread (mutawatir in fame) yet major Sahaba like Umar and Ali do not act upon it, it indicates the ruling was abrogated. The example is the hadith about flogging and exiling unmarried fornicators. Umar swore never to exile, and Ali called exile a fitna; their knowledge and status make it impossible that the ruling was unknown—thus, abrogation is inferred. Another example: Umar's refusal to divide the conquered land of Sawad shows that the Prophet's division (if any) was not obligatory. Muhammad ibn Sirin's comment on temporary marriage (mut'ah) shows that the Sahaba witnessed it and then forbade it, again indicating abrogation. The objection regarding Ibn Mas'ud's practice of tathiq (interlocking fingers) instead of placing hands on knees is answered by distinguishing between a concession (rukhsah) and the stronger practice (azimah); he chose the latter but did not deny the former. The second type: an example where a Sahabi (Umar) abandons a practice that was common among earlier Sahaba (like dividing conquered land), showing that the ruling was not absolute but subject to ijtihad or later abrogation. This passage illustrates the Hanafi use of Sahaba's actions as a source of law, particularly in the context of naskh.

Key Terms
الانتساخ (al-intisākh): The abrogation (naskh) of a legal ruling, inferred here when a known ruling is not acted upon by knowledgeable Sahaba.
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession or lenient ruling, as opposed to azimah (stronger duty). In the context, Ibn Mas'ud considered taking the knees a concession.
العزيمة (al-'azīmah): The stronger, more stringent ruling that is preferred. Ibn Mas'ud practiced tathiq as azimah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main inference from Umar's refusal to exile fornicators despite the hadith on flogging and exile?
A) Umar ignored the hadith due to personal opinion.
B) The ruling of exile was abrogated (naskh).
C) Umar was unaware of the hadith.
D) Exile is not part of Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
The deliberate inaction of a knowledgeable Sahabi on a well-known ruling is strong evidence of its abrogation in Hanafi usul.

Sahaba's Abandonment of a Common Practice: The Sawad Land Example
Explaining the second type: Sahaba's abandonment of a common practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second type of Sahaba's action as evidence for non-obligation, specifically when a later Sahabi abandons a practice that was common among earlier Sahaba.

Core Idea
When a later Sahabi does not follow a practice that was common among earlier Sahaba, it indicates the practice was not obligatory.

This second part of the passage from al-Bazdawi presents another scenario: a Sahabi (Umar) abandons a practice that was followed by earlier Sahaba (dividing conquered land). The passage explains that this shows the practice was not compulsory but subject to ijtihad. We will analyze this example and understand the underlying methodological principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the practice and its earlier application
The practice is the division of conquered land among the conquerors. Earlier Sahaba, as narrated by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, asked Umar to divide the land of Sawad because they had conquered it by force (anwatan).
This indicates that earlier conquering parties received land shares.
2
Observe the later Sahabi's (Umar) action
Umar refused to divide the land. Instead, he left the land in the hands of the original owners, imposing jizya and kharaj.
Umar's decision was based on his ijtihad, not ignorance of the earlier practice.
3
Infer the legal implication
Since Umar was knowledgeable and the earlier practice was known, his abandonment indicates that the earlier practice (dividing land) was not obligatory (not hattman). It was a permissible option that he chose not to follow.
This is the second type: non-obligation, not necessarily naskh.
4
Distinguish from the first type
The first type involved a well-known hadith being abandoned, leading to naskh. Here, the practice is based on previous Sahaba's actions, not a direct hadith. The inference is that the practice was not mandatory.
The passage classifies this as the second section (al-qism al-akhar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ta'āruḍ: Definition, Conditions, and Resolution Hierarchy
Definition and resolution of ta'āruḍ between evidences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ta'āruḍ, identify its pillar and condition, and apply the hierarchy for resolving apparent contradictions between textual evidences.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not an original principle; the default is to seek resolution before declaring ta'āruḍ.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi introduces the concept of ta'āruḍ (contradiction) between legal evidences. It clarifies that contradiction is not a foundational state but a secondary phenomenon that requires active resolution. The author divides the discussion into four parts: linguistic meaning, pillar, condition, and ruling. Understanding these components is essential for any mujtahid dealing with apparently conflicting sources.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the overarching principle
The passage begins by establishing that contradiction is not an original principle (laysa bi aṣl). Hence, the default is to seek what removes contradiction (ṭalab mā yadfaʿ al-taʿāruḍ). Only when inability (ʿajz) occurs is the ruling of contradiction established.
This principle governs the entire chapter: one must first attempt reconciliation.
2
Examine the linguistic definition
The linguistic meaning of muʿāraḍah is 'prevention in a confrontational manner' (al-mumānaʿah ʿalā sabīl al-muqābalah). The term relates to obstacles (ʿawāriḍ).
Understanding the linguistic root helps grasp the technical nuance.
3
Identify the pillar (rukn)
The pillar of taʿāruḍ is the confrontation of two proofs equally (taqābul al-ḥujjatayn ʿalā al-sawāʾ), with no superiority for one over the other, and with opposing rulings (ḥukmayn mutaḍādayn). Without equality, one proof would be preferred and no contradiction exists.
Equality in strength and opposition in ruling are essential.
4
Understand the condition (shart)
The condition is unity of subject (ittiḥād al-maḥall) and time (ittiḥād al-waqt) with opposing rulings. If the subject or time differs, both rulings can apply simultaneously (e.g., marriage gives permissibility in one relationship and prohibition in another; wine was permissible before revelation but later prohibited).
This condition ensures genuine contradiction; otherwise, it is only apparent.
5
Memorize the hierarchy for resolving contradictions
The passage gives a two‑tier hierarchy: between two Quranic verses, resort to the Sunnah. Between two hadiths, the order is: first resort to qiyas (analogy), then to the sayings of the Companions (aqwāl al‑ṣaḥābah). This is because ignorance of the abrogator (nāsikh) prevents acting on both.
The hierarchy follows the general order of proofs in Hanafi usul after the Quran and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المعارضة
وإذا ثبت أن التعارض ليس بأصل: كان الأصل في الباب: طلب ما يدفع التعارض، وإذا جاء العجز وجب إثبات حكم التعارض.
وهذا الفصل أربعة أقسام في الأصل:
وهو معرفة التعارض لغةً، وشرطه، وركنه، وحكمه شريعة.
أما معنى المعارضة لغةً: فالممانعة على سبيل المقابلة، يقال: عَرَضَ لي كذا: أي استقبلني بصد، ومنع وسميت الموانع عوارض.
وركن المعارضة: تقابل الحجتين على السواء، لا مزية لإحداهما، في حكمين متضادين، فركنُ كل شيء ما يقوم به.
وأما الشرط: فاتحاد المحل والوقت، مع تضاد الحكم، مثل التحليل والتحريم، وذلك لأن التضاد لا يقع في محلين؛ لجواز اجتماعهما، مثل النكاح: يوجب الحل في محل والحرمة في غيره وكذلك في وقتين؛ لجواز اجتماعهما في محل واحد، في وقتين مثل حرمة الخمر بعد حلها.
وحكم المعارضة بين آيتين: المصير إلى السنة.
وبين السنتين نوعان: المصير إلى القياس وأقوال الصحابة رضي الله عنهم على الترتيب في الحجج إن أمكن؛ لأن الجهل بالناسخ: يمنع العمل بهما.
Chapter of Contradiction. And when it is established that contradiction is not an original principle: the principle in this chapter is: seeking what removes contradiction, and when inability occurs, the ruling of contradiction must be established. And this section has four divisions in principle: knowledge of contradiction linguistically, its condition, its pillar, and its legal ruling. As for the linguistic meaning of mu'āraḍah: it is prevention in the manner of confrontation. It is said: 'araḍa lī kadhā: it faced me with obstruction and prevention, and preventive factors are called 'awāriḍ. The pillar of contradiction: the confrontation of two proofs equally, no superiority for one over the other, in two opposing rulings, for the pillar of everything is that by which it stands. As for the condition: unity of subject and time, with opposition of ruling, like permissibility and prohibition, and that is because opposition does not occur in two subjects; because of the possibility of their combination, like marriage: it necessitates permissibility in one subject and prohibition in another, and likewise in two times; because of the possibility of their combination in one subject at two times, like the prohibition of wine after its permissibility. And the ruling of contradiction between two verses: resort to the Sunnah. And between two hadiths: two types: resort to analogy and the sayings of the Companions, may God be pleased with them, in order of the proofs if possible; because ignorance of the abrogator prevents acting upon both.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on contradiction (ta'āruḍ) in Usul al-Bazdawi. The author establishes that contradiction is not an original principle; rather, the default is to seek resolution. The discussion is divided into four parts: (1) linguistic definition: mu'āraḍah means prevention or obstruction in a confrontational manner; (2) pillar (rukn): two evidences facing each other equally with opposing rulings, with no superiority; (3) condition (shart): the two evidences must relate to the same subject and time, and their rulings must be opposite (e.g., halal vs. haram). If the subject or time differs, there is no true contradiction because both rulings can apply simultaneously; (4) ruling (hukm): between two verses of the Quran, one resorts to the Sunnah to resolve the contradiction. Between two hadiths, the order is: first resort to qiyas (analogy), then to the sayings of the Companions (aqwāl al-ṣaḥābah), following the hierarchy of evidences. The reason for this is that ignorance of the abrogating (nāsikh) text prevents acting on both. The passage emphasizes that the mujtahid must seek to reconcile conflicting evidences before declaring a contradiction.

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-mu'āraḍah): Linguistically: prevention in a confrontational manner. Technically: the confrontation of two proofs equally with opposing rulings.
الركن (al-rukn): The pillar: the confrontation of two proofs equally with no preference for either, in two opposing rulings.
الناسخ (al-nāsikh): The abrogator: ignorance of which prevents acting on the conflicting evidences and necessitates following the prescribed order of resolution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling when two Quranic verses appear to contradict each other?
A) Resort to the Sunnah
B) Resort to Qiyas
C) Resort to Ijma'
D) Both verses are abandoned

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, ta'āruḍ is not a default state; one must first seek resolution via the hierarchy of proofs, and the order depends on the type of evidence involved.

Naskh — Abrogation

Five Methods of Resolving Apparent Contradiction between Evidences
Enumerates five ways to resolve ta'arud between conflicting proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five methods of resolving ta'arud and apply each method to a given pair of contradictory evidences, citing the appropriate Hanafi usul principle.

Core Idea
Ta'arud is resolved by hierarchy, reconciliation, situational difference, chronology, or implied abrogation—never by discarding both proofs.

When two primary sources appear to contradict, the Hanafi usul scholar does not jump to preference or rejection. Instead, a systematic method is applied to resolve the apparent conflict. Al-Bazdawi presents five distinct approaches, from the simplest (superiority in authority) to the more interpretive (implied abrogation). This lesson walks you through each method, equipping you to handle apparent contradictions with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contradictory evidences
First, clearly state the two texts (or rulings) that appear to conflict. For each, note the source and its apparent ruling.
The conflict must be genuine; if one evidence is inherently weak, no contradiction exists.
2
Check if the evidences are equal in strength (i'tidal)
If one evidence is muhkam and the other mujmal, or Qur'an vs. khabar al-wahid, the weaker cannot stand. Method 1: no true ta'arud.
This is the first filter: hierarchy (muhkam > mujmal, mashhur > khabar al-wahid).
3
Examine if the rulings can be reconciled by context
Sometimes the rulings apply to different spheres (e.g., Hereafter vs. this world) or different aspects. Method 2: reconcile by difference in ruling.
Example: verse on yamin ghamus vs. verse on laghw.
4
Consider if the conflict is due to different situations (hal)
The same text may have multiple valid interpretations based on circumstances. Method 3: assign each meaning to a distinct situation.
Example: the two readings of 'arjulakum/arjulikum' in wudu verse.
5
Determine if chronological knowledge is available
If we know which revelation came later, the later abrogates the earlier (Method 4). If not, and there is a prohibition vs. permission, the prohibition is considered later by implication (Method 5).
Method 4 requires explicit date; Method 5 is a presumption in the absence of date.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١- أما من قبل نفس الحجة فأن لا يعتدل الدليلان، فلا تقوم المعارضة، مثل المحكم يعارضه المجمل أو المتشابه. ومثل الكتاب أو المشهور من السنة يعارضهما خبر الواحد ؛ لأن ركنها اعتدال الدليلين، وأمثلة هذا كثيرةٌ لا تُحصى.
۲- وأما الحكم فإن الثابت بهما إذا اختلف عند التحقيق: سقط التعارض مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِن يُؤَاخِذُكُم بِمَا كَسَبَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ ﴾ البقرة / ٢٢٥ ، والمراد به الغموس. وقال جلَّ ذكره: لَا يُؤَاخِذُكُمُ اللهُ بِاللَّغْوِ فِي أَيْمَنِكُمْ وَلَكِن يُؤَاخِذُكُم بِمَا عَقَدتُّمُ الْأَيْمَنَ المائدة /۸۹ ، والغَموس داخل في هذا اللغو؛ لأن المؤاخذة المثبتة مطلقة، وهي في دار الجزاء، والمؤاخذة المنفية مقيدة بدار الابتلاء، فصح الجمع، وبطل التدافع، فلا يصح أن يُحمل البعض على البعض. وأمثاله كثيرة.
وأما الحال فمثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا نَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ ﴾ البقرة / ۲۲۲ : بالتخفيف، ومعناه : انقطاع الدم وبالتشديد قرىء، ومعناه: الاغتسال، وهما معنيان متضادان ظاهراً. ألا يُرى أن الحيض لا يجوز أن يمتد إلى الاغتسال مع امتداده إلى انقطاع الدم؛ لأن امتداد الشيء إلى غاية واقتصاره دونها معاً: متضادان، لكن التعارض يرتفع باختلاف الحالين بأن يُحمل الانقطاع على العشرة، فهو الانقطاع التام الذي لا تردُّد فيه، ولا يستقيم التراخي إلى الاغتسال ؛ لما فيه من بطلان التقدير، ويُحمل الاغتسال على ما دون مدة الانقطاع والتناهي ؛ لأن ذلك هو المفتقر إلى الاغتسال، فينعدم به التعارض. وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ وَأَرْجُلَكُمْ إِلَى الْكَعْبَينِ ﴾ المائدة / ٦ : بالخفض والنصب : متعارضان ظاهراً، فإذا حملنا النصب على ظهور القدمين، والخفض على حال الاستتار بالخفين: لم يثبت التعارض، فصح ذلك لأن الجلدَ أُقيم مُقامَ بشرة القدم، فصار مسحه بمنزلة غسل القدم. ٤- وأما صريح اختلاف الزمان فبأن يُعرف التاريخ، فيسقط التعارض، ويكون آخرهما ناسخاً له. وذلك مثل قول ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه في المتوفى عنها زوجها إذا كانت حاملاً إنها تعتد بوضع الحمل، وقال مَن شاء باهلته أن سورة النساء القُصرى : ﴿وَأَوَلَتُ الْأَحْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ ﴾ الآية، الطلاق/٤: نزلت بعد التي في سورة البقرة، وأراد به قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَاجًا ﴾ الآية ، البقرة / ٢٣٤ ، وكان ذلك رداً على من قال بأبعد الأجلين. ٥- وأما الذي ثبت دلالة فمثل النصين تعارضا في الحظر والإباحة: أن الحاظر يُجعل آخراً ناسخاً دلالةً ؛ لأنا نعلم أنهما وُجدا في زمانين. ولو كان الحاظرُ أولاً : كان ناسخاً للمبيح، ثم كان المبيح ناسخاً له، فيتكرر النسخ.
1. As for (resolution) from the side of the proof itself: that the two evidences are not equal, so no contradiction arises, such as a muhkam (clear) text being contradicted by a mujmal (ambiguous) or mutashabih (unclear) text. And such as the Book or the well-known (mashhur) Sunnah being contradicted by khabar al-wahid (isolated report); because the pillar of contradiction is equality of the two proofs, and examples of this are numerous without count. 2. As for the ruling: if what is established by them (i.e., two apparently contradictory texts) differs upon investigation, the contradiction falls away. Example: Allah's saying, 'But He will take you to account for what your hearts have earned' (al-Baqarah 2:225), meaning the yamin ghamus (false oath). And Allah, exalted, said, 'Allah will not take you to account for the unconsidered oaths you swear' (al-Ma'idah 5:89), and the yamin ghamus is included in this 'laghw' (unconsidered) oath? Because the affirmed accountability is absolute and is in the abode of recompense, while the negated accountability is restricted to the abode of trial; thus reconciliation is correct and conflict is invalidated, so it is not correct to interpret one part as overriding another. And similar examples are many. 3. As for the situation: such as Allah's saying, 'Do not approach them until they become pure' (al-Baqarah 2:222) with takhfif (reading yat-hurna), meaning cessation of blood; and it is also recited with tashdid (yattahharna), meaning the ritual bath (ghusl). These two meanings are apparently contradictory. Do you not see that menstruation cannot extend to ghusl while it extends to cessation of blood? Because the extension of something to a limit and its termination before it are contradictory. But the contradiction is removed by considering the difference in situations: cessation is interpreted as ten days (i.e., the maximum duration of menstruation) which is complete cessation with no doubt, and delaying to ghusl is not valid because that would invalidate the estimation; and ghusl is interpreted as (occurring) before the period of cessation and termination, because that (i.e., the lesser duration) is what requires ghusl. Thus contradiction disappears. Similarly, Allah's saying, 'And wipe your heads and your feet to the ankles' (al-Ma'idah 5:6) with both jarr (genitive) and nasb (accusative) are apparently contradictory. When we interpret nasb as washing the feet when they are exposed, and jarr as wiping the feet when they are covered by khuffs (leather socks), then no contradiction exists. That is correct because the skin of the foot has been placed in the position of the skin of the foot (i.e., the foot itself), so wiping becomes equivalent to washing the foot. 4. As for explicit chronological difference: that the date is known, so contradiction is removed, and the later one abrogates the earlier. Example: the saying of Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding a woman whose husband dies while she is pregnant: that she observes her iddah by giving birth. And he said, 'Whoever wishes, I will imprecate that Surah al-Nisa' al-Qusra (the shorter Surah of Women, i.e., al-Talaq) came after the one in Surah al-Baqarah,' referring to Allah's saying, 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave behind wives' (al-Baqarah 2:234). This was a refutation of those who said (she must observe) the longer of the two periods. 5. As for that which is established by implication: such as two texts contradicting in prohibition and permissibility: that the prohibiting one is made later (as an abrogator) by implication, because we know that they existed in two times. And if the prohibiting one were first, it would abrogate the permitting one, then the permitting one would abrogate it, leading to repeated abrogation.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's usul text enumerates five distinct methods to resolve apparent contradiction (ta'arud) between two legal evidences. The passage is enumerative and structured as a series of rules. The author begins by stating that contradiction only arises when the two evidences are equal in strength (i'tidal). Therefore, if one evidence is superior in authority—e.g., a muhkam (clear) text versus a mujmal (ambiguous) text, or the Qur'an/mashhur Sunnah versus khabar al-wahid—no true contradiction exists; the weaker evidence cannot genuinely oppose the stronger. The second method examines the actual rulings established by the texts. If upon careful investigation the rulings are found to apply in different contexts or aspects, they can be reconciled. The example of the two Qur'anic verses on oaths is given: one verse threatens accountability for deliberate (ghamus) oaths, while another exempts laghw (unintentional) oaths. The apparent contradiction is resolved by noting that the accountability is for the Hereafter (absolute), while exemption is in this world (specific to legal liability). Thus, both verses operate in different spheres. The third method involves different situations (hal). The example is the reading of 'tat-hurna' (with tashdid or takhfif) in the verse on menstruation. The two readings imply different meanings—cessation of blood versus ritual purity through ghusl. By interpreting the cessation as the maximum menstrual period (10 days) and ghusl as valid before that period (since ghusl is needed when bleeding stops earlier), the contradiction is resolved. Similarly, the reading of 'arjulakum' (accusative) and 'arjulikum' (genitive) in the verse on wudu is explained: the accusative indicates washing the feet when exposed, the genitive indicates wiping over khuffs when worn. The fourth method is explicit knowledge of chronology: if one text is known to have been revealed later, it abrogates the earlier. The example is the iddah of a pregnant widow: the verse in Surah al-Talaq (full term until delivery) abrogates the earlier verse in al-Baqarah (four months and ten days) because the later verse is chronologically later. The fifth method is implied chronology: when a prohibition (hazr) contradicts a permission (ibaha), the prohibition is considered later as a presumptive abrogator. This is because we know the texts came in different times; if the prohibition were first, then the permission would have to abrogate the prohibition and then be abrogated again, leading to multiple abrogations which is unlikely unless proven. Therefore, the prohibition is taken as the later revelation.

Key Terms
تعارض (ta'arud): Apparent contradiction between two evidences that appear to lead to conflicting rulings, requiring resolution.
محكم (muhkam): A clear, unambiguous text that is decisive in meaning and not subject to abrogation.
ناسخ (nasikh): A later revelation that abrogates an earlier rule (mansukh), used to resolve chronological contradiction.
ريفعالعارض (raf' al-ta'arud): Resolution of apparent contradiction through one of the five methods described in the passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the apparent contradiction between the two readings of 'arjulakum/arjulikum' in the wudu verse resolved?
A) By declaring one reading corrupt and using the other.
B) By applying the rule of naskh (abrogation) and taking the later reading.
C) By interpreting each reading as applying to a different situation (exposed feet vs. feet covered by khuffs).
D) By considering both readings as equally valid and leaving the choice to the individual.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for resolving ta'arud follows a strict order: check strength, reconcile by context or situation, then resort to explicit or implied abrogation—never discard a proof without attempting reconciliation.

Naskh — Abrogation

The Prohibiting Text as Abrogating (Al-Haziru Nasikh)
Rule statement with examples and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the prohibiting text is deemed abrogating in case of conflict, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding affirmative vs negative texts.

Core Idea
When a prohibiting text conflicts with a permissive one, prohibition abrogates permission – a default Hanafi rule.

Revelation sometimes brings apparently contradictory rulings: one text permits an action, another prohibits it. Hanafi usul al-fiqh resolves this by presuming the prohibiting text is later and thus abrogating (nasikh). This lesson unpacks that rule and notes a disagreement among Hanafi scholars when one text affirms a ruling and another negates it while remaining on the original state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting texts
Recognise that the source passage gives examples: permissive narrations for eating dhabb (hyena) and domesticated donkey meat, versus prohibitive narrations for the same items. The conflict is between an earlier permission and a later prohibition.
The rule applies specifically when one text prohibits and another permits; not all textual conflicts imply naskh.
2
Apply the rule: prohibiting is abrogating
The Hanafi rule states: 'We make the prohibiting text abrogating' (نجعل الحاظر ناسخاً). This means the prohibition takes precedence and the permission is considered abrogated (mansukh). The burden of proof is on the prohibition to be from a reliable source.
This is a default; evidence may shift the presumption (e.g., if the permissive text is qat'i and the prohibition is zanni).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on muthbit vs nafi
The passage mentions a disagreement among Hanafi scholars (مشايخنا) about cases where one text is affirmative (muthbit) and the other negative (nafi) while remaining on the original state. Some give precedence to the affirmative, others to the negative, depending on contextual clues.
This ikhtilaf does not overturn the main rule but applies to a specific subcategory.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
For example, if a hadith says 'The Prophet permitted the meat of dhabb' and another says 'He prohibited it', the Hanafi ruling is prohibition. Other schools might harmonise or prefer the permissive text, leading to different fatwas.
The ikhtilaf on muthbit vs nafi affects rulings when one text affirms a general permission and another negates it without specifying a later time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروي عنه صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه أباحه، وحرم لحوم الحمر الأهلية، وروي أنه أباحها. وكذلك الضبع، وما يجري مجرى ذلك : أنا نجعل الحاظر ناسخاً. واختلف مشايخنا رحمهم الله فيما إذا تعارض نصان أحدهما مثبت، والآخر ناف مُبَقٌ على الأمر الأول.
And it has been narrated from him (peace be upon him) that he permitted it, and he prohibited the meat of domesticated donkeys, and it is narrated that he permitted them. And likewise the hyena, and whatever runs along that line: we make the prohibitive [text] abrogating. And our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) differed regarding the case when two texts conflict, one being affirmative and the other negative while remaining upon the first matter.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage presents a key Hanafi usul principle for resolving apparent contradictions between primary texts. The rule is that when there is a narration permitting something (like eating dhabb or domesticated donkey meat) and another narration prohibiting it, we consider the prohibiting text as nasikh (abrogating), and the permissible text as mansukh (abrogated). This is based on the premise that prohibition indicates a legal change. The author then notes an ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars regarding the case where one text establishes a ruling (muthbit) and another denies it (nafi) while explicitly remaining on the original state. The disagreement touches on the methodology of determining which text is later and how to reconcile apparently contradictory evidence.

Key Terms
الحاظر (al-ḥāẓir): The text that prohibits an action or thing.
الناسخ (al-nāsikh): The abrogating text that replaces the ruling of a previous text.
مثبت (muthbit): A text that establishes a ruling (affirmation).
ناف (nāfī): A text that negates or denies a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle stated in this passage, when one text permits eating dhabb and another prohibits it, which text takes precedence?
A) The permissive text because permission is the default
B) The prohibiting text is considered abrogating and takes precedence
C) Both texts are reconciled by ruling that dhabb is disliked (makruh)
D) The later text is always preferred regardless of content

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a prohibiting text conflicts with a permissive one, the prohibiting text is defaulted as nasikh, establishing prohibition as the operative ruling.

Preferring the Affirming Text (Muthbit) in Conflicting Narrations
Usul dispute on preferring muthbit over nafi mubaqqa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a muthbit and nafi mubaqqa text, apply the Hanafi rule that the muthbit is preferred (awla), and evaluate the opposing view of Isa ibn Aban.

Core Idea
When two texts conflict, the one affirming a new ruling (muthbit) prevails over the one merely upholding the original (nafi mubaqqa).

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses a key Hanafi rule for resolving conflicting textual evidence when one text affirms a new ruling and the other negates it while maintaining the original. The disagreement between Al-Karkhi and Isa ibn Aban is settled by the early practice of the Hanafi school. By mastering this rule, you will understand how the Hanafi school prioritises the affirming text in cases where abrogation is not clearly determined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting reports
In a conflict scenario, there are two reports: one that affirms a new ruling (muthbit) and one that negates that new ruling while maintaining the original ruling (nafi mubaqqa). The rule applies when each report claims to be the correct account.
2
Classify each report
Determine which report is muthbit (affirming a change) and which is nafi mubaqqa (upholding the original status quo). The nafi mubaqqa does not claim a new ruling but simply confirms the pre-existing ruling.
The nafi mubaqqa is distinguished from a mere contradictory text because it specifically maintains the earlier ruling.
3
State the Hanafi preference
According to Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, the muthbit is preferable (awla) over the nafi mubaqqa. This means the affirming text is given precedence in deriving the ruling.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position of the Hanafi school.
4
Apply to the examples
In the case of Burayrah, the muthbit report states her husband was free; the nafi mubaqqa says he was a slave. The Hanafi school took the muthbit, granting Burayrah the right of choice (khiyar) upon emancipation. In the case of Maymunah, the muthbit report says the Prophet married her while non-muhrim (halal); the nafi mubaqqa would imply he was in ihram. The Hanafis took the muthbit, ruling that marriage in ihram is invalid.
5
Note the opposing view and outcome
Isa ibn Aban argued that the two texts are merely contradictory without preference. However, the early Hanafi practice and later school adopted Al-Karkhi's view, making the muthbit the relied-upon position.
The passage indicates that the early scholars acted upon the muthbit in the two examples, establishing the tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروي عنه صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه أباحه، وحرم لحوم الحمر الأهلية، وروي أنه أباحها. وكذلك الضبع، وما يجري مجرى ذلك: أنا نجعل الحاظر ناسخاً. واختلف مشايخنا رحمهم الله فيما إذا تعارض نصان أحدهما مثبت، والآخر ناف مُبَقٌ على الأمر الأول. فقال الكرخي رحمه الله: المثبت أولى. وقال عيسى بن أبان رحمه الله: يتعارضان. وقد اختلف عمل أصحابنا المتقدمين في هذا الباب، فقد بريرة أُعتقت وزوجها حر وهذا مثبت، وروي أنها أُعتقت وزوجها عبد وهذا ناف مُبَقٌ على الأمر الأول، وأصحابنا أخذوا بالمثبت. وروي أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام تزوج ميمونة وهو حلال.
And it is reported from him (the Prophet) that he permitted it, and he forbade the meat of domesticated donkeys, and it is reported that he permitted them. And likewise the hyena, and what runs in the same vein: we make the prohibitior abrogating. And our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) differed regarding when two texts conflict, one affirming, and the other negating while maintaining the original ruling. Al-Karkhi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The affirming is preferable. And Isa ibn Aban (may Allah have mercy on him) said: They are in conflict. And the practice of our early scholars differed on this matter: Indeed, Burayrah was freed and her husband was free—this is affirming, and it is reported that she was freed and her husband was a slave—this is negating while maintaining the original ruling, and our scholars adopted the affirming. And it is reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) married Maymunah while he was non-muhrim (halal).
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a usul rule concerning the preference between two conflicting texts (nass) when one affirms a ruling (muthbit) and the other negates (nafi) while maintaining the original ruling (mubaqqa 'ala al-amr al-awwal). The discussion is framed within the context of abrogation (naskh): earlier it mentions that a prohibiting text is considered abrogating (nasikh). Then the author introduces a disagreement among Hanafi scholars: Al-Karkhi holds that the affirming text (muthbit) is preferable (awla), while Isa ibn Aban argues that both texts are in mutual conflict (tata'aradan) and no inherent preference exists. The author then notes that the actual practice (amal) of the early Hanafi masters varied but they ultimately adopted the muthbit in two examples: the case of Burayrah (whether her husband was free or slave when she was freed) and the case of the Prophet's marriage to Maymunah (whether he married her while in ihram or in a state of halal). In both, the affirming version (muthbit) was taken: Burayrah's husband was free, and the Prophet was halal (non-muhrim). This illustrates the Hanafi preference for the muthbit when a nafi upholds the original ruling. The passage does not provide a detailed proof or istidlal; it merely states the positions and the outcome of practice.

Key Terms
مثبت (muthbit): A text or report that affirms (proves) a new ruling that contravenes the original ruling.
ناف مبق على الأمر الأول (nafi mubaqqa 'ala al-amr al-awwal): A text or report that negates the new ruling and upholds the original ruling (the ruling that was in effect before the alleged abrogation).
ناسخ (nasikh): Abrogating; a text that supersedes a previous ruling. In this passage, the prohibiting (hazir) text is considered nasikh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view did the early Hanafi scholars adopt when faced with two conflicting texts—one affirming (muthbit) and one negating while maintaining the original ruling (nafi mubaqqa)?
A) They considered both texts equally and suspended judgment.
B) They preferred the negating text (nafi) because it maintains the original ruling.
C) They preferred the affirming text (muthbit) as stated by Al-Karkhi.
D) They rejected both texts as unreliable.

Key Takeaway
In a conflict between a muthbit and a nafi mubaqqa, the Hanafi school gives preference to the muthbit, as practiced by the early scholars.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on the Nature of Exception (Istithna)
Usul dispute: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on istithna

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i views on legal exception, state the Hanafi definition of exception as bayan taghyir, and evaluate the Shafi'i proofs (linguistic consensus and tawhid).

Core Idea
Exception prevents speech; it modifies, not abrogates.

Exception (istithna) is a common tool in legal speech: 'I owe you 1000 dirhams except 100.' But how does it work? Does it change what the speaker actually said, or does it oppose the original ruling? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions as debated in usul al-fiqh, showing how a seemingly small linguistic difference leads to major practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is about how exception operates: does it prevent speech on the excepted part (Hanafi) or does it provide a contradictory proof that opposes the initial ruling (Shafi'i)?
This is a dispute about the nature of legal reasoning, not just grammar.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafis, exception prevents the speaker from having spoken about the excepted part from the start. It is a form of 'bayan taghyir' (explanatory modification) that adjusts the scope of the utterance.
Example: 'I owe 1000 dirhams except 100' means the speaker is understood to have said 'I owe 900 dirhams.' The 100 is simply not part of the original statement.
3
State the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i holds that exception is a 'dalil mu'arada' (proof of opposition) that opposes the general ruling. The excepted part is negated due to a separate, contradictory proof.
Example: In the same debt statement, the Shafi'i view is that the 100 dirhams are not owed because the exception acts as a counter-proof.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i proofs
Al-Bazdawi presents two Shafi'i proofs: linguistic consensus (exception from affirmation is negation) and the rational proof from 'La ilaha illa Allah' (the exception provides the affirmative meaning).
These proofs aim to show that exception has a force independent of the original speech.
5
Compare practical outcomes
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) appears in debt cases: Hanafi says 900 owed; Shafi'i says nothing owed because exception negates the whole. Also when exception is of a different genus (e.g., 'except a cloth'), Shafi'i deducts value; Hanafi rejects unless same genus.
These differences affect fatwa in financial transactions.

بالشرط والاستثناء لو صح كل واحد منهما متراخياً كان ناسخاً، ولكنه إذا اتصل : مَنَعَ بعض التكلم، لا أنه يرفع بعد الوجود، فكان بياناً، فسمي بيان تغییر. ومنزلة الاستثناء : مثل منزلة التعليق بالشرط، لأن الاستثناء التكلم إيجاباً في بعض الجملة أصلاً، والتعليق يمنع الانعقاد لأحد الحكمين أصلاً، وهو الإيجاب، ويُبقي الثاني، وهو الاحتمال، فلذلك كانا من قسم واحد، فكانا من باب التغيير، دون التبديل. * واختلفوا في كيفية عمل كل واحد منهما : فقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : الاستثناء يمنع التكلم بحكمه بقدر المستثنى، فيجعل تكلماً بالباقي بعده. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : إن الاستثناء يمنع الحكم بطريق المعارضة، بمنزلة دليل الخصوص. كما اختلفوا في التعليق على ما سبق ذكره، وقد دل على هذا الأصل مسائلهم. فصار عندنا تقدير قول الرجل: لفلان علي ألف درهم إلا مائةً : لفلان علي تسعمائة. وعنده إلا مائة : فإنها ليست علي. وبيان ذلك : أنه جعل قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوا ﴾ البقرة / ١٦٠ : بمعنى قوله إلا الذين تابوا فلا تجلدوهم واقبلوا شهادتهم، وأولئك هم الصالحون ، غير فاسقين. وكذلك قال في قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء» (۱) : إن معناه : بيعوا الطعام بالطعام سواء بسواء، فبقي صدر الكلام عاماً في القليل والكثير ؛ لأن الاستثناء عارضه في المكيل خاصةً، وخصوص دليل المعارضة لا يتعدى مثل دليل الخصوص في العام. وذلك مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَّا أَن يَعْفُونَ أَوْ يَعْفُواْ الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ، عُقْدَةُ النكاح البقرة / ۲۳۷ : هذا دليل معارض لبعض الصدر، وهو في حق من يصح منه العفو، فبقي فيما لا معارضة فيه. وقال في رجل قال: لفلان عليَّ ألف درهم إلا ثوباً: إنه يسقط من الألف قدر قيمته ؛ لأن دليل المعارضة يجب العمل به على قدر الإمكان، وذلك ممكن في القيمة. واحتج في المسألة بالإجماع ودلالته، وبالدليل المعقول. أما الأول : فإن أهل اللغة أجمعوا على أن الاستثناء من الإثبات نفي، ومن النفي: إثبات، وهذا إجماع على أن للاستثناء حكماً وضع له يعارض به حكم المستثنى منه. وأما الثاني : فلأن كلمة التوحيد: لا إله إلا الله، وهي كلمة وضعت للتوحيد، ومعناه النفي والإثبات، فلو كان تكلماً بالباقي: لكان نفياً لغيره، لا إثباتا له ، فصح لما كانت كلمة التوحيد أن معناها إلا الله، فإنه إله.
By condition and exception: if each of them were valid when delayed, it would be abrogating. But when it is attached, it prevents some of the speech, not that it lifts after existence, so it was explanation, thus called 'explanation of modification'. The status of exception is like the status of suspension by condition, because exception makes the speech affirmative in part of the sentence originally, and suspension prevents the establishment of one of the two rulings originally—which is affirmation—and retains the second, which is probability. Therefore both are of one category, thus belonging to the chapter of modification, not substitution. And they differed on the manner of operation of each. Our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said: Exception prevents the speech from its ruling to the extent of the excepted part, thus it makes speech concerning the remainder after it. And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Exception prevents the ruling by way of opposition, like a specific proof. As they also differed on suspension as previously mentioned, and their cases indicate this principle. Thus with us the estimation of a man's statement: 'I owe Zaid one thousand dirhams except one hundred' is: 'I owe Zaid nine hundred.' In his view, it is 'except one hundred' and it is not owed. The explanation: He made the verse {Except those who repent} (al-Baqara 160) to mean 'except those who repent, do not flog them, accept their testimony, and they are righteous, not sinners.' Likewise he said concerning the Prophet's statement (peace be upon him): 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal' (1): Its meaning is: 'Sell food for food equal for equal'. The beginning of the statement remained general for little and much, because the exception opposed it specifically regarding measurables, and the specificity of the proof of opposition does not extend like the specific proof in the general. That is like His statement {Except if they pardon or the one in whose hand is the marriage knot pardons} (al-Baqara 237): This is a proof opposing part of the beginning, regarding the one from whom pardon is valid, so it remains where there is no opposition. And he said concerning a man who said: 'I owe Zaid one thousand dirhams except a cloth': that the value is deducted from the thousand, because the proof of opposition must be applied as much as possible, and that is possible in value. And he argued in the issue by consensus and its indication, and by rational proof. As for the first: The linguists are unanimous that exception from affirmation is negation, and from negation is affirmation. This is consensus that exception has a ruling established for it by which it opposes the ruling of the thing from which exception is made. As for the second: Because the word of tawhid 'La ilaha illa Allah' is a word established for monotheism, and its meaning is negation and affirmation. If it were speech about the remainder, it would be a negation of others, not an affirmation of Him. So it is established that the meaning of the word of tawhid is 'except Allah', for He is God.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage examines the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the nature of legal exception (istithna) and its relationship to conditional suspension (ta'liq bi al-shart). The author, al-Bazdawi, begins by establishing that both exception and condition, when attached to speech, are forms of 'bayan taghyir' (explanatory modification) rather than naskh (abrogation), because they prevent the speech from applying to the excepted part from the outset, not after it has taken effect. He then contrasts the two schools: The Hanafis hold that an exception such as 'except one hundred' in a debt statement effectively makes the speaker's utterance apply only to the remainder (i.e., nine hundred), so the excepted part is simply not included in the original speech. By contrast, al-Shafi'i views exception as a standalone 'dalil mu'arada' (proof of opposition) that counteracts the general ruling, so that the excepted part is negated entirely. To illustrate, al-Bazdawi gives examples from the Quran and Hadith. The verse 'except those who repent' is interpreted by al-Shafi'i as a contradictory proof that reverses the ruling for those who repent. Similarly, the hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal' is understood as actually commanding sale with equality, leaving the prohibition only for unequal amounts. The Shafi'i view even extends to cases where the exception is of a different genus, as with 'except a cloth' where the value is subtracted. Al-Bazdawi then presents the main proofs for the Shafi'i position: the linguistic consensus that exception reverses the ruling, and the rational argument from the declaration of faith 'La ilaha illa Allah', which would lose its affirmative meaning if it were interpreted as mere speech about the remainder. The passage does not include the Hanafi response, leaving the dispute open at this point.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā’): The exclusion of part of a statement by using the particle 'illa' or its equivalent; it modifies the original speech by preventing its application to the excepted part.
دليل المعارضة (dalīl al-mu‘āraḍa): A proof that opposes the general ruling of a statement, used by Shafi'i to explain how exception works.
بيان التغيير (bayān al-taghyīr): An explanatory modification that adjusts the scope of speech without abrogating it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what happens when a person says 'I owe Zaid 1000 dirhams except 100 dirhams'?
A) The entire debt is void because the exception contradicts the affirmation.
B) The speaker is considered to have only spoken of 900 dirhams.
C) The exception is ignored because it is not attached.
D) The debt is reduced by the value of the excepted item if it is of the same genus.

Key Takeaway
Exception (istithna) in Hanafi usul is a modification of speech (bayan taghyir), not a contradictory proof, leading to different legal outcomes compared to the Shafi'i school.

Naskh — Abrogation
beginner
6 min
Abrogation (Naskh): Definition and Validity in Hanafi Usul
Defining naskh linguistically and technically, and stating its validity by consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definition of naskh, identify its Quranic evidence, and explain its status as a pure declaration (bayan mahd) according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Naskh is a pure declaration of a ruling's duration, not a change in God's knowledge.

This lesson introduces the concept of abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law. We begin with its linguistic meaning of substitution, supported by a Quranic verse, then examine its technical definition as a pure declaration (bayan mahd) from the Lawgiver. Finally, we confirm its validity by consensus of the Muslim community, distinguishing it from the rejected notion of badā'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic meaning
The word naskh literally means substitution (tabdīl). The Quran uses this term in verse 16:101. Grasp that substitution implies one thing ceasing and another taking its place.
The verse uses 'tabdīl' (بدّلنا) to describe replacing one verse with another.
2
Examine the technical definition
In Sharīʿah, naskh is a pure declaration (bayān mahḍ) of the duration of a ruling that was already known to Allah but revealed without an apparent time limit. From our perspective it appears as change, but from Allah's knowledge it is simply the expiration of the ruling's term.
This definition refutes the idea of badā' (change in divine knowledge).
3
Recognize the analogy to death
Just as a person's death is predetermined by Allah but appears as a change caused by the killer, similarly naskh is a declaration of the end of a ruling's applicability.
The author uses this analogy to clarify the Lawgiver's perspective.
4
Confirm validity by consensus
The passage states that naskh is permissible and valid by the consensus of all Muslims. Note that the Jews rejected it, but their view is dismissed.
Ijma' is a primary source of law; the rejection by Jews is not considered valid.

Source Text

والناسخ والمنسوخ
أما النسخ: فإنه في اللغة: عبارة عن التبديل.
قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِذَا بَدَّلْنَا ءَايَةً مَكَانَ عَوَايَةٍ وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِمَا
ينزل ﴾ النحل / 101، فسمى النسخ تبديلاً.
ومعنى التبديل : أن يزول شيء، ويَخلُفَه غيره.
ويقال : نسخت الشمس الظل؛ لأنها تَخلُفُه شيئاً فشيئاً.
هذا أصل هذه الكلمة ،وحقيقتها حتى صارت تشبه الإبطال؛ من حيث كان وجوداً يَخلُف الزوال.
وهو في حق صاحب الشرع : بيان محض لمدة الحكم المطلق الذي كان معلوماً عند الله تعالى، إلا أنه أطلقه فصار ظاهره البقاء في حق البشر، فكان تبديلاً في حقنا، بياناً محضاً في حق صاحب الشرع.
وهو كالقتل: بيان محض للأجل ؛ لأنه ميت بأجله بلا شبهة في حق
صاحب الشرع، وفي حق القاتل: تغيير وتبديل.

وقالت اليهود لَعَنَهُمُ الله بفساده، وهم في ذلك فريقان:
Chapter on Explanation of Substitution, which is Abrogation. The discussion in this chapter is about the interpretation of abrogation itself, its locus, its condition, the abrogator, and the abrogated. As for abrogation: it is in language an expression for substitution. Allah Most High said: 'And when We substitute a verse in place of another verse, and Allah knows best what He sends down' (al-Nahl 16:101), thus He called abrogation substitution. And the meaning of substitution is that something ceases and something else replaces it. It is said: the sun abrogated the shade, because it replaces it little by little. This is the origin of this word and its reality, until it came to resemble nullification, in that it is an existence that replaces the cessation. And it, in relation to the Lawgiver, is a pure statement of the duration of the absolute ruling which was known to Allah Most High, except that He expressed it absolutely so its apparent meaning is permanence for humans, thus it is substitution in relation to us, and a pure statement in relation to the Lawgiver. And it is like killing: a pure statement of the term, because he dies at his appointed time without doubt in relation to the Lawgiver, but in relation to the killer, it is a change and substitution. And abrogation in the rulings of the Sharia is permissible and valid by the consensus of all Muslims. And the Jews, may Allah curse them for their corruption, said otherwise, and they in this are two groups:
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on abrogation (naskh). It states the plan: to discuss the definition, locus, conditions, abrogator and abrogated. Then it provides the linguistic definition: naskh means tabdeel (substitution), citing the Quranic verse where Allah says 'When We substitute a verse in place of another verse.' The linguistic meaning is that something ceases and another takes its place, like the sun replacing the shade gradually. In legal terminology, from the perspective of the Lawgiver, naskh is merely a declaration (bayan) of the time limit of a ruling that was already known in divine knowledge but was revealed without a specified term, thus appearing to humans as permanent. So from our perspective it is a change, but from Allah's knowledge it is simply the expiration of the ruling's term. This is analogous to a person's appointed death: from Allah's perspective, it is the predetermined end, but from the killer's perspective, it is a change and substitution. The passage then affirms the validity of naskh in Islamic rulings by the consensus of the Muslim community, and notes that the Jews reject it, and they are divided into two groups, but the text cuts off here. This is a foundational usul definition stressing that naskh does not imply badā' (change in God's knowledge) but is a manifestation of the intended duration.

Key Terms
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation; a ruling of Shari'ah that ceases another ruling with a later revealed text, indicating the end of its applicability.
التبديل (al-tabdīl): Substitution; one thing ceasing and another taking its place, the linguistic basis for naskh.
بيان محض (bayān mahḍ): Pure declaration; a statement that merely clarifies something already known to the Lawgiver, with no element of change in His knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is naskh from the perspective of the Lawgiver (Allah)?
A) A change in God's knowledge due to new circumstances.
B) A mere declaration of the ruling's duration (bayan mahd).
C) Complete nullification of the ruling without replacement.
D) A ruling that adapts to evolving human needs.

Key Takeaway
Naskh is a valid, divinely ordained mechanism for the cessation of rulings, functioning as a pure declaration of their intended durations.

Naskh — Abrogation
beginner
8 min
Defining Naskh: Abrogation as Clarification
Definition of naskh and proof of its permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of naskh as a pure clarification (bayan) of the duration of a ruling, and explain why naskh is permissible and valid in Shari'ah.

Core Idea
Naskh is not a divine change of mind but a clarification of when a ruling always was to end.

Naskh (abrogation) is a fundamental concept in usul al-fiqh that explains how a later revelation can supersede an earlier ruling. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between the apparent change for humans and the immutable divine knowledge behind it. In this passage, Imam al-Bazdawi defines naskh both linguistically and technically, addresses objections from Jews and rationalists, and provides a clear proof from the law of Adam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic meaning of naskh
Recognize that naskh linguistically means 'substitution' (tabdil) – something ceases and another takes its place, as the sun 'abrogates' the shadow. This is the foundation for its technical use.
The Quran itself uses 'tabdil' for replacing one verse with another.
2
Distinguish the Lawgiver's perspective from the human perspective
From Allah's side, naskh is pure clarification (bayan mahd) of a ruling's duration because He knew from eternity that the ruling would be temporary. Humans see change; Allah sees disclosure.
The analogy of killing: the killer changes the victim's life, but from Allah's knowledge, the term was fixed.
3
Identify the opponents and their arguments
The Jews rejected naskh either rationally or scripturally (Sabbath eternal). Some rationalists claimed naskh implies badā' (change in divine knowledge) or that commands/prohibitions indicate intrinsic good/evil.
These objections are refuted by the Hanafi position that naskh is merely bayan.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
The historical proof: in Adam's Shari'ah, marriage of siblings was initially allowed then abrogated – no one denies this. The rational proof: naskh is bayan of duration, involving no contradiction in divine attributes.
These proofs establish naskh as permissible (ja'iz) and actually occurring (waqi').
5
Conclude with the Hanafi ruling
Naskh in Shari'ah is permissible and sound by consensus of Muslims. Rejecting it is inconsistent with Islamic belief because it denies the abrogation of previous laws by the Qur'an and Sunnah.
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
