Chapter on Explanation of Al-Tabdil (Substitution), which is Al-Naskh (Abrogation). The discussion in this chapter is on the interpretation of naskh itself, its subject, its condition, the abrogator and the abrogated. As for naskh: in language, it is expression for substitution. Allah Most High said: "And when We substitute a verse in place of a verse, and Allah knows best what He sends down" (Surah An-Nahl 16:101), so He called naskh substitution. The meaning of substitution is that something disappears and something else takes its place. It is said: the sun naskh-ed the shadow because it replaces it bit by bit. This is the origin of this word and its reality until it came to resemble nullification, in the sense that existence replaces disappearance. And it, with respect to the Lawgiver, is pure clarification of the duration of the absolute ruling that was known to Allah Most High, except that He issued it so its apparent meaning became permanence for humans; thus it is substitution with respect to us, pure clarification with respect to the Lawgiver. And it is like killing: pure clarification of the term, because he dies by his term without doubt with respect to the Lawgiver, but with respect to the killer it is change and substitution. And naskh in the rulings of Shari'ah is permissible and sound according to all Muslims. The Jews—may Allah curse them for their corruption—said it, and they are two groups in that: One of them said: it is rationally invalid. And some of them said: it is invalid by revelation and tradition. And some Muslims have denied naskh, but this statement from a Muslim having sound Islamic belief is inconceivable. As for the one who rejected it by tradition: he argued that Moses, peace be upon him, said to his people: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath as long as the heavens and the earth endure,' for that is written in the Torah. And that it reached them through a way of knowledge from Moses, peace be upon him, that there is no abrogation for his law. And the proponents of the other view argued: that a command indicates the goodness of the commanded thing, and prohibition of a thing indicates its repulsiveness, and abrogation indicates its opposite, and in that there is something that necessitates badā' (change in divine knowledge) and ignorance of the outcomes of affairs. And our proof for its permissibility and occurrence by revelation and tradition: that no one denied the permissibility of [marrying] sisters in the law of Adam, peace be upon him, and the permissibility of the part for Adam, peace be upon him—that is Eve who was created from him—and that that was abrogated by other laws. And the rational proof: that naskh is clarification of the duration of the ruling for the servants, and indeed... (1) [Chapter on Explanation of Al-Tabdil, his statement: the statement of Moses, peace be upon him: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath...' blank in the manuscript).
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is an introductory definition of naskh (abrogation) from the classical Hanafi usul work of al-Bazdawi. It begins by stating the chapter topic: 'Explanation of al-Tabdil (substitution), which is al-Naskh.' The author then defines naskh linguistically as substitution, citing Quran 16:101 where Allah uses 'tabdil' for replacing one verse with another. The literal meaning is that something ceases and is succeeded by something else, illustrated by the sun replacing the shadow. In the context of Shari'ah, naskh from the Lawgiver's perspective is not a change in divine knowledge but a pure clarification of the duration of a ruling that was already known to Allah. For humans, it appears as a change because the ruling was revealed with a general applicability that seemed permanent. The author analogizes this to killing: from the killer's perspective it is a change, but from the divine perspective it is merely the appointed time. The passage then affirms the permissibility of naskh by consensus of Muslims and refutes the Jews who rejected it, either rationally or by claiming their scriptures forbid abrogation. Some Muslims also denied naskh, but that is considered inconsistent with sound Islamic belief. The Hanafi proof for its occurrence is the historical example of the Shari'ah of Adam, where marrying one's sister (Eve) was initially permissible and later abrogated. The rational proof is that naskh is simply a clarification (bayan) of the duration of a ruling for the servants, implying no contradiction in divine wisdom.

Key Terms
النسخ (al-naskh): Linguistically substitution; technically, the removal of a Shari'ah ruling by a later revelation, which in reality is a clarification of the duration of that ruling.
التبديل (al-tabdil): Substitution: something ceases and is replaced by another.
البيان (al-bayan): Clarification or disclosure; in this context, naskh is considered a pure bayan from the Lawgiver.
البداء (al-badā'): A change in divine knowledge or will; rejected by Ahl al-Sunnah as impossible for Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of naskh with respect to the Lawgiver (صاحب الشرع)?
A) It is a change in divine decree due to new knowledge.
B) It is a pure clarification of the duration of a ruling.
C) It is the abrogation of a ruling without replacement.
D) It is the abrogation of a ruling by a similar ruling.

Key Takeaway
Naskh is permissible and real, functioning as a divine clarification of the temporary duration of a ruling, not a revision of divine knowledge.

Naskh — Abrogation

Proof of the Validity of Naskh and Refutation of Its Deniers
Proving naskh is possible and occurred in Islamic law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof for the validity of naskh and refute the opposing arguments by distinguishing naskh from badā'.

Core Idea
Naskh is not a change in God's will but a clarification of the preordained term of a ruling.

The validity of naskh (abrogation) is a foundational principle in Usul al-Fiqh. This passage from al-Bazdawi presents the Hanafi defense against those who deny naskh on rational or scriptural grounds. It distinguishes naskh from badā' and provides both historical and rational proofs. By understanding this argument, you will grasp how Hanafi usul reconciles the apparent contradiction of a later ruling superseding an earlier one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing views and their evidence
Two groups deny naskh: one on rational grounds (claiming it implies badā'), the other on scriptural grounds (citing the Torah). Note their specific arguments as presented in the passage.
2
Examine the historical proof for naskh
The Hanafi proof uses the universally accepted example of Adam's law: the permissibility of marrying sisters was later abrogated. This establishes that naskh occurred across different sharias.
This is a historical fact accepted by all Muslims.
3
Analyze the rational proof: analogy with life and death
The passage analogizes naskh to the announcement of a creature's preordained lifespan. Just as death is the clarification of the term of life, naskh is the clarification of the term of a ruling. The command itself does not linguistically imply continuation.
Continuation is by istishāb al-ḥāl, not by the command.
4
Distinguish naskh from badā'
The rational deniers argue that naskh implies badā' (change in God's knowledge). The Hanafi response: naskh is merely the disclosure of a preexisting term, not a change in the divine will. Badā' is impossible for God.
This is the core conceptual distinction.
5
Refute the scriptural proof
The deniers cite a statement from Moses that the Sabbath is eternal. The Hanafi response: the Torah has been distorted, so it is not authoritative (tawqīf). Therefore, their scriptural evidence is invalid.
Distortion of previous scriptures is a well-established Islamic belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أحدهما : إنه باطل عقلاً.
وقال بعضهم : هو باطل سمعاً وتوقيفاً.
وقد أنكر بعض المسلمين النسخ، لكنه لا يُتصور هذا القول من مسلم
صحة اعتقاد الإسلام.
أما مَن ردَّه توقيفاً : فقد احتج بأن موسى صلوات الله عليه قال لقومه: تمسكوا بالسبت مادامت السموات والأرض، فإن ذلك مكتوب في التوراة.
وأنه بلغهم بما هو طريق العلم عن موسى صلوات الله عليه أن لا نسخ لشريعته.
واحتج أصحاب القول الآخر: أن الأمر يدل على حسن المأمور به، والنهي عن الشيء : يدل على قُبحه، والنسخ يدل على ضده، وفي ذلك ما يوجب البداء والجهل بعواقب الأمور.
ودليلنا على جوازه، ووجوده سمعاً وتوقيفاً: أن أحداً لم يُنكر استحلال الأخوات في شريعة آدم صلوات الله عليه، واستحلال الجزء لآدم صلوات الله عليه، وهي حواء التي خُلقت منه، وأن ذلك نُسخ بغيره من الشرائع. والدليل المعقول : أن النسخ هو بيان مدة الحكم للعباد، وقد كان ذلك غيباً عنهم.
وبيان ذلك : أنا إنما نجوز النسخ في حكم مطلق عن ذكر الوقت يحتمل أن يكون مؤقتاً، ويحتمل البقاء والعدم على السواء؛ لأن النسخ إنما يكون في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، والأمر المطلق في حياته للإيجاب، لا للبقاء، بل البقاء باستصحاب الحال على احتمال العدم بدليله، لا أن البقاء بدليل يوجبه ؛ لأن الأمر لم يتناول البقاء لغةً، فلم يكن دليلُ النسخ متعرّضاً لحكم الدليل الأول بوجه إلا ظاهراً، بل كان بياناً غيب عنا، وهي الحكمة البالغة بلا شبهة.
للمدة التي هي بمنزلة الإحياء والإيجاد أن حكمه الحياة والوجود، لا البقاء، بل البقاء لعدم أسباب الفناء، أو بإبقاء هو غيرُ الإيجاد، وله أجل معلوم عند الله تعالى، فكان الإفناء والإماتة بياناً محضاً، فهذا مثله.
هذا حكم بقاء المشروع في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، فإذا رسول الله عليه الصلاة والسلام من غير نسخ: صار البقاء من بعد ثابتاً بدليل يوجبه، فصار يقيناً، لا يحتمل النسخ بحال، فإذا غاب الحي: بقيت حياته ؛ لعدم الدليل على موته فكذلك المشروع المطلق في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام.
وأما دعواهم التوقيف فباطل عندنا ؛ لأنه ثبت عندنا تحريف كتابهم، فلم يبق حجة، والله أعلم.
One of them said: It is rationally invalid. And some said: It is invalid according to revelation and scripture. And some Muslims have denied abrogation, but this statement is not conceivable from a Muslim with sound Islamic belief. As for the one who rejected it on scriptural grounds: he argued that Moses, peace be upon him, said to his people: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath as long as the heavens and the earth endure,' for that is written in the Torah. And that it reached them through a reliable path from Moses, peace be upon him, that there is no abrogation for his law. And the proponents of the other view argued: that a command indicates the goodness of the commanded thing, and a prohibition indicates its evil, and abrogation indicates its opposite, and in that is what necessitates badā' (change of divine will) and ignorance of the consequences of matters. And our proof for its permissibility and occurrence according to revelation and scripture is: that no one has denied the permissibility of marrying sisters in the law of Adam, peace be upon him, and the permissibility of a part for Adam, peace be upon him, that is, Eve who was created from him, and that that was abrogated by other laws. And the rational proof: that abrogation is the clarification of the duration of a ruling for the servants, and that was hidden from them. And the explanation: that we only permit abrogation in a ruling that is unrestricted regarding the mention of time, which may be temporary or may continue or cease equally; because abrogation only occurs during the lifetime of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the absolute command during his lifetime is for obligation, not for continuation; rather, continuation is by way of istishāb al-ḥāl (presumption of continuity) with the possibility of cessation by its evidence, not that continuation is required by an evidence; because the command does not linguistically necessitate continuation, so the evidence of abrogation does not oppose the first ruling in any way except apparently; rather, it is a clarification hidden from us, and it is perfect wisdom without doubt. For the period is like giving life and bringing into existence; its ruling is life and existence, not continuation; rather, continuation is due to the absence of causes of extinction, or by preservation that is not the same as origination, and it has a known term with Allah, so annihilation and death are pure clarification; this is its analog. This is the ruling of the continuation of legislation during the lifetime of the Prophet, peace be upon him. When the Messenger, peace be upon him, passed away without abrogation, continuation thereafter becomes established by an evidence that necessitates it, so it becomes certain, not subject to abrogation at all. Just as when a living person is absent, his life continues due to the absence of evidence of his death, so too is the unrestricted legislation during the lifetime of the Prophet, peace be upon him. As for their claim of scripture, it is invalid in our view, because we have established the distortion of their book, so it remains no proof. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the validity of naskh (abrogation) in Islamic law. The author first presents the arguments of those who deny naskh. Two groups are mentioned: one denies it rationally ('aqlan), the other denies it on scriptural grounds (sam'an wa tawqīfan). The author notes that denial of naskh is not from sound Islamic belief. The scriptural denier cites a statement attributed to Musa (AS) that the Sabbath is eternal, and a report that Musa's law has no abrogation. The rational denier argues that commands imply goodness and prohibitions imply evil, so abrogation would contradict that, implying badā' (change in God's knowledge). The author then provides the Hanafi proof for naskh: historically, the permissibility of marrying sisters in Adam's law was later abrogated — this establishes occurrence. Rationally, naskh is simply the announcement of the pre‑known duration of a ruling. During the Prophet's lifetime, an unrestricted command is for immediate obligation, not perpetual continuation; continuation is presumed by istishāb al-ḥāl, not by the command itself. Therefore, naskh does not overturn a permanent ruling but clarifies that the ruling's term has expired. After the Prophet's death, if a ruling was not abrogated, it becomes certain and unchangeable. The author rejects the scriptural proof of the deniers because the Torah is acknowledged to have been altered.

Key Terms
النسخ (al-naskh): Lifting a shar'i ruling by a later shar'i evidence, indicating the expiration of its term.
البداء (al-badā'): The appearance of a new opinion or knowledge after ignorance, attributed to God by some deniers of naskh.
توقيف (tawqīf): Revelation-based scriptural evidence; something known only by prophetic warrant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The command implies continuation, so naskh is necessary.
B) Naskh is the clarification of the pre-determined duration of a ruling, like announcing someone's death.
C) Naskh is permissible because the Torah says so.
D) Naskh is impossible because it would imply ignorance in God.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, naskh is a clarification of the preordained term of a ruling, not a change in God's command.

Naskh — Abrogation

The Permissibility of Naskh and Refutation of Tawqif
Rational argument for naskh with analogy and refutation of tawqif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rationale for naskh in absolute commands and refute the Jewish claim of tawqif by identifying the logical structure of the argument.

Core Idea
Naskh is a clarification of a hidden time limit, not a contradiction of divine will.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul addresses a critical usul question: can a divinely revealed ruling be abrogated? The Hanafi school argues yes, and here the author provides a rational defense against the claim that prior scriptures are permanently unchangeable (tawqif). By understanding the distinction between the initial obligation of a command and its continuation, you will see why naskh is a form of bayan (clarification) and why the objection based on earlier scriptures fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
The Jewish claim of tawqif asserts that their scripture is permanently unchangeable and cannot be abrogated. Recognize this as the target of the Hanafi rebuttal.
2
State the Hanafi position on naskh
The Hanafi school permits naskh, particularly in rulings expressed in absolute terms (hukm mutlaq) without a specified duration. An absolute command indicates obligation, not perpetual continuation.
The passage argues that the command itself does not linguistically include continuation.
3
Analyze the rationale: linguistic and istishab-based
The absolute command during the Prophet's life is for wujub (obligation). Continuation (baqa) is not part of the command; it is only presumed by istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) until evidence of change appears. Thus naskh does not contradict the original command.
Istishab is used here to show that continuation is not inherent but a provisional presumption.
4
Follow the analogy to life and death
The author draws an analogy: just as life and death clarify a created being's appointed term, so naskh clarifies the appointed term of a ruling. Abrogation is a pure clarification (bayan), not an arbitrary change.
The analogy strengthens the claim that naskh is consistent with divine wisdom.
5
Evaluate the rebuttal of tawqif
The Jewish claim is rejected because their scripture is proven to have been distorted (tahrif) and thus is no longer a valid proof. Even if intact, the rational argument for naskh would still stand.
This underscores that the Hanafi position relies on sound evidence (Qur'an, Sunnah, aqli) rather than a compromised source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك غيباً عنهم. وبيان ذلك : أنا إنما نجوز النسخ في حكم مطلق عن ذكر الوقت يحتمل أن يكون مؤقتاً، ويحتمل البقاء والعدم على السواء؛ لأن النسخ إنما يكون في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، والأمر المطلق في حياته للإيجاب، لا للبقاء، بل البقاء باستصحاب الحال على احتمال العدم بدليله، لا أن البقاء بدليل يوجبه ؛ لأن الأمر لم يتناول البقاء لغةً، فلم يكن دليلُ النسخ متعرّضاً لحكم الدليل الأول بوجه إلا ظاهراً، بل كان بياناً غيب عنا، وهي الحكمة البالغة بلا شبهة. و للمدة التي هي بمنزلة الإحياء والإيجاد أن حكمه الحياة والوجود، لا البقاء، بل البقاء لعدم أسباب الفناء، أو بإبقاء هو غيرُ الإيجاد، وله أجل معلوم عند الله تعالى، فكان الإفناء والإماتة بياناً محضاً، فهذا مثله. قبض و هذا حكم بقاء المشروع في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، فإذا الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام من غير نسخ: صار البقاء من بعد ثابتاً بدليل يوجبه، فصار يقيناً، لا يحتمل النسخ بحال، فإذا غاب الحي: بقيت حياته ؛ لعدم الدليل على موته فكذلك المشروع المطلق في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام. وأما دعواهم التوقيف فباطل عندنا ؛ لأنه ثبت عندنا تحريف كتابهم، فلم يبق حجة، والله أعلم.
That was hidden from them. Explanation: We permit naskh in a ruling that is absolute regarding the mention of time, which can be time-bound, and equally can remain or cease; because naskh only occurs during the life of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the absolute command during his life is for obligation, not for continuation; rather, continuation is by istishab al-hal with the possibility of non-existence due to its evidence, not that continuation is by evidence that requires it; because the command does not linguistically include continuation, so the evidence of naskh does not oppose the ruling of the first evidence except apparently, but rather it was a clarification hidden from us, which is the manifest wisdom without doubt. And for the period which is like bringing to life and creation, its ruling is life and existence, not continuation; rather, continuation is due to the absence of causes of annihilation, or by preservation which is different from creation, and it has an appointed term known to Allah, so annihilation and causing death were pure clarification; similarly for this. [Death?] And this is the ruling on the continuation of the legislated during the life of the Prophet (peace be upon him). So when the Prophet (peace be upon him) [died?] without abrogation, the continuation thereafter became established by evidence requiring it, so it became certain, not subject to abrogation at all. Just as when a living person disappears, his life continues due to the absence of evidence of his death; similarly for the absolute legislated during the life of the Prophet (peace be upon him). As for their claim of tawqif (immutability), it is false in our view, because we have established that their scripture was distorted, so it is no longer a proof. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a dispute with the Jews who claim that their scripture cannot be abrogated (tawqif). The Hanafi author begins by explaining the rationale for naskh: we permit abrogation in a ruling that is mentioned in absolute terms without a specified time, because such a ruling could be time-bound and could equally continue or cease. The reason is that naskh only occurred during the Prophet's lifetime. An absolute command during his life is for obligation, not for continuation—continuation is merely by istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) with the possibility of abrogation. The evidence for naskh does not directly oppose the first ruling except in appearance; rather it is a clarification of a hidden time limit, which is divine wisdom. The author then draws an analogy: just as creation and life are not the same as continuation (since continuance requires preservation or absence of causes of destruction), similarly, a ruling's existence is like life, and abrogation is like death—a pure clarification of its appointed end. After the Prophet's death, if a ruling was not abrogated during his life, its continuation becomes certain (qat'i) and cannot be abrogated afterwards. Finally, the author dismisses the Jewish claim of tawqif (that their scripture is unchangeable) by noting that their scripture has been distorted, so it is no longer a valid proof. This aligns with the Hanafi position that naskh is possible and has occurred, and that the Torah and Gospel have been abrogated by the Qur'an.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): The abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revelation during the Prophet's lifetime.
حكم مطلق (hukm mutlaq): A legal ruling that is absolute and not qualified by a time specification.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity of a state or ruling until evidence of change appears.
توقيف (tawqif): The claim by some that certain scriptures are permanently unchangeable and not subject to abrogation.
بَيان (bayan): Clarification; here, naskh is described as a bayan of the hidden term of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the Prophet's life is necessary for continuation
D) Because abrogation negates continuation

Key Takeaway
Naskh is permissible for absolute commands because their continuation is not linguistically implied but presumed by istishab, and abrogation is a clarification of their hidden term.

The Locus of Naskh: Conditions and Exceptions
Defining where naskh can occur and listing excluded categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a ruling to be a valid locus of naskh and list the three categories that cannot be abrogated.

Core Idea
Only rulings that are temporally indeterminate and not fixed as eternal or time-bound can be abrogated.

This passage from Hanafi usul defines the specific types of rulings that are subject to abrogation (naskh). It sets two essential conditions and then clarifies three categories that, though generally possible, are excluded due to explicit or implicit eternity or fixed time limits. Understanding this helps prevent erroneous claims of abrogation for foundational beliefs like divine attributes or eternal rewards and punishments.

Guided Path
1
Define the locus of naskh
Start by extracting the definition: 'mahall an-naskh' is a ruling that admits clarification of its duration and time. This sets the basic scope.
The term 'mahall' here means the place or subject where abrogation operates.
2
Analyze the first condition
The ruling must inherently be possible of existence and non-existence. If it is eternal (like the attributes of Allah), it cannot be abrogated.
This condition excludes the divine essence, names, and attributes.
3
Analyze the second condition
Nothing antithetical to time/duration may be attached to the ruling. This introduces the three excluding factors: explicit eternity, eternity by implication (dalala), and explicit time-binding (tawqit).
These factors make the ruling fixed and thus non-abrogable.
4
List the three excluding categories
Identify each: (1) ta'bid bi-nass (eternity by explicit text), e.g., 'abiding forever' (Quran 4:57) for Hell; (2) ta'bid bi-dalala (eternity by implication), e.g., Paradise; (3) tawqit (time-bound), e.g., the victory of believers until the Day of Judgment.
The passage cuts off mid-verse but the classification is clear.
5
Apply the rule
Given a specific ruling, check if it meets both conditions. If it does, it is a valid locus of naskh; otherwise, it is not.
For example, a command without time limit is abrogable; a statement of eternal punishment is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان محل النسخ. مَحَلَّ النسخ: حكم يحتمل بيان المدة والوقت، وذلك بوصفين: أحدهما: أن يكون في نفسه محتملاً للوجود والعدم، فإذا كان بخلافه : لم يحتمل النسخ. والثاني : أن لا يكون مُلْحَقاً به ما ينافي المدة والوقت. أما الأول : فبيانُه : أن الصانع بأسمائه وصفاته قديم لا يحتمل الزوال والعدم، فلا يحتمل شيء من أسمائه وصفاته النسخ بحال. وأما الذي ينافي النسخ من الأحكام التي هي في الأصل محتملة للوجود والعدم: فثلاثة : - تأبيد ثبت نصاً. وتأبيد ثبت دلالة. وتوقيت. أما التأبيد صريحاً فمثل قول الله تعالى : ﴿ وَخَالِدِينَ فِيهَا أَبَدًا ﴾ النساء / ٥٧ . ومثل قوله جل وعلا: ﴿ وَجَاعِلُ الَّذِينَ اتَّبَعُوكَ فَوْقَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا إِلَى يَوْمِ
Chapter: The Locus of Naskh. The locus of naskh is a ruling that admits the clarification of duration and time, and this has two descriptions: First, that it in itself is possible of existence and non-existence; if it is otherwise, it does not admit naskh. Second, that nothing antithetical to duration and time is attached to it. As for the first, its explanation: The Creator with His names and attributes is eternal, not subject to passing away or non-existence, so none of His names or attributes admit naskh at all. As for what precludes naskh among rulings that are originally possible of existence and non-existence: there are three: (1) Eternity established by explicit text (nass). (2) Eternity established by dalala (implication). (3) Time-bound (tawqit). As for explicit eternity, like the saying of Allah Most High: 'They will abide therein forever' (an-Nisa 4:57). And like His saying, Exalted: 'And [Allah] will make those who follow you above those who disbelieve until the Day of...' (incomplete).
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule defining the locus of naskh—the types of rulings that can be abrogated. The author (likely the commentator) states that the locus is a ruling that can admit clarification of its time duration. This requires two conditions: (1) the ruling must be inherently possible of existence and non-existence; if it is not (like the attributes of Allah), it cannot be abrogated. (2) The ruling must not have something attached that negates the mention of duration/time, such as an explicit statement of eternity. Then the author lists three specific categories of rulings that, although originally possible, are not subject to naskh because of a factor that precludes it: (a) eternity explicitly stated in nass (like the punishment of Hell), (b) eternity established by dalala (like the reward of Paradise), and (c) time-bound rulings (like the victory of believers until the Day of Judgment). These are given as examples. The passage cuts off mid-verse, but the classification is clear. This is typical Hanafi usul: naskh is only possible in rulings that are temporally indeterminate and not explicitly eternal or time-bound. The ruling is that the attributes of Allah cannot be abrogated, and rulings with eternal or fixed time limits are also excluded.

Key Terms
محل النسخ (mahall an-naskh): The specific type of ruling that can be subject to abrogation: one that admits clarification of its time duration.
تأبيد (ta'bid): A ruling established as eternal, either by explicit text or by implication.
توقيت (tawqit): A ruling that is explicitly time-bound to a specific period or event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two conditions for a ruling to be a valid locus of naskh according to this passage?
A) The ruling must be eternal and time-bound
B) The ruling must be possible of existence/non-existence and not have anything antithetical to time attached
C) The ruling must be from the Qur'an and explicit
D) The ruling must be about worldly matters and not about the afterlife

Key Takeaway
The locus of naskh is limited to rulings that are temporally indeterminate and not fixed by explicit or implicit eternity or time-binding.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
10 min
The Locus of Abrogation: What Can and Cannot Be Abrogated
Defines what can and cannot be abrogated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of mahall al-naskh to classify rulings as abrogable or non-abrogable based on the two conditions.

Core Idea
A ruling is abrogable only if it is contingent and not tied to perpetuity; Allah's attributes and perpetual rulings cannot be abrogated.

Abrogation (naskh) is a key concept in usul al-fiqh, but not every ruling can be abrogated. This passage from Imam al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul defines the precise criteria for a ruling to be a candidate for abrogation, and lists those that are permanently excluded. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for correctly applying the principle of naskh in legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two defining attributes
A ruling must be contingent (possible of existence and non-existence) and must have no element contradicting temporal limitation. Write down these two conditions.
These are the necessary and sufficient conditions for a ruling to be mahall al-naskh.
2
List the four non-abrogable categories
From the passage, extract the four types of rulings that cannot be abrogated: Allah's attributes, explicitly perpetual rulings, implicitly perpetual rulings (laws of the final Prophet), and temporally fixed rulings before expiry.
The first category is intrinsic; the other three are rulings that appear contingent but have perpetuity attached.
3
Distinguish explicit vs. implicit perpetuity
Identify the Quranic verses provided as examples of explicit perpetuity (ta'bid nass). Then understand implicit perpetuity: the finality of prophethood makes the entire Shariah perpetual.
Implicit perpetuity arises from the seal of prophethood, as no new revelation can occur.
4
Analyze the Abraham example
Read the passage's response to the objection: the command to sacrifice was not abrogated but its obligation was replaced by ransom. Trace the reasoning that this is not naskh but fida' (ransom).
This clarifies that naskh is termination, not substitution.
5
Apply the framework to a new case
Consider a ruling like 'Cut off the hand of a thief' (Quran 5:38). Is it subject to abrogation? Apply the two conditions: it is contingent, but is there any attached perpetuity? The verse does not contain 'forever', so it is abrogable in principle.
The actual applicability may depend on other usul principles (e.g., conditions of punishment), but the locus check is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان محل النسخ
مَحَلَّ النسخ: حكم يحتمل بيان المدة والوقت، وذلك بوصفين: أحدهما: أن يكون في نفسه محتملاً للوجود والعدم، فإذا كان بخلافه لم يحتمل النسخ. والثاني: أن لا يكون مُلْحَقاً به ما ينافي المدة والوقت. أما الأول: فبيانُه: أن الصانع بأسمائه وصفاته قديم لا يحتمل الزوال والعدم، فلا يحتمل شيء من أسمائه وصفاته النسخ بحال. وأما الذي ينافي النسخ من الأحكام التي هي في الأصل محتملة للوجود والعدم: فثلاثة: تأبيد ثبت نصاً. وتأبيد ثبت دلالة. وتوقيت. أما التأبيد صريحاً فمثل قول الله تعالى: ﴿ وخَلِدِينَ فِيهَا أبدا﴾ النساء/٥٧. ومثل قوله جل وعلا: ﴿ وَجَاعِلُ الَّذِينَ اتَّبَعُوكَ فَوْقَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا إِلَى يَوْمِ الْقِيَامَةِ﴾ آل عمران/٥٥: يريد بهم الذين صدقوا بمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. والقسم الثاني: مثل سائر شرائع النبي محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام التي قبض على قرارها فإنها مؤيَّدة لا تحتمل النسخ، بدلالة أن محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلم خاتم النبيين، ولا نبي بعده، ولا نسخ إلا بوحي على لسان نبي. والثالث: واضح، والنسخ فيه قبل الانتهاء باطل؛ لأن النسخ هذا كله بَداء وظهور الغلط، لا بيان للمدة، والله تعالى يتعالى عن ذلك. فصار الذي لا يحتمل النسخ أربعة أقسام في هذا الباب، والذي محل النسخ قسم واحد، وهو حكم مطلق يحتمل التوقيت، لم يجب بقاؤه بدليل يوجب البقاء، كالشراء يثبت به الملك دون البقاء، فينعدم الحكم لانعدام سببه لا بالناسخ بعينه، فلا يؤدي إلى التضاد والبداء، ولا يصير الشيء الواحد حسناً وقبيحاً في حال واحدة، بل في حالين. فإن قيل: إن الأمر بذبح الولد في قصة إبراهيم عليه السلام نُسخ، وصار الذبح بعينه حسناً بالأمر، قبيحاً بالنسخ. قيل له: لم يكن ذلك بنسخ للحكم، بل ذلك الحكم بعينه كان ثابتاً، والنسخ هو انتهاء الحكم، ولم يكن بل كان ثابتاً، إلا أن المحل الذي أُضيف إليه لم يحلَّه الحكم على طريق الفداء دون النسخ، وكان ذلك ابتلاء استقرَّ حكم الأمر عند المخاطب، وهو إبراهيم صلوات الله عليه في آخر الحال.
Chapter: Clarifying the locus of abrogation. The locus of abrogation: a ruling that admits the clarification of duration and time. This is by two attributes: one: that it is in itself possible of existence and non-existence. If it is otherwise, it does not admit abrogation. The second: that nothing contradictory to duration and time is attached to it. As for the first: its explanation is that the Creator, in His names and attributes, is eternal, not admitting cessation and non-existence, so none of His names and attributes admit abrogation at all. As for what contradicts abrogation from among rulings that are originally possible of existence and non-existence: they are three: perpetual established by explicit text; perpetual established by indication; and temporally fixed. As for explicit perpetual: like Allah's saying: 'and they will dwell therein forever' (al-Nisa 4:57). And like His saying: 'and He will make those who follow you above those who disbelieve until the Day of Resurrection' (Al Imran 3:55) — meaning those who believed in Muhammad, peace be upon him. The second type: like all the laws of Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, which he passed away upon their established form — they are confirmed, admitting no abrogation, because Muhammad is the seal of the prophets, and there is no prophet after him, and there is no abrogation except by revelation upon the tongue of a prophet. The third: clear, and abrogation before its expiry is invalid, because all this is [like] changing opinion and manifest error, not clarification of duration, and Allah is exalted above that. So that which cannot be abrogated becomes four divisions in this chapter, and that which is the locus of abrogation is one division: an absolute ruling that admits temporal conditioning, whose continuance is not necessitated by a proof that necessitates continuance — like purchase: ownership is established by it but not continuance. So the ruling ceases due to the cessation of its cause, not by a particular abrogator. Thus it does not lead to contradiction and change of opinion, nor does one and the same thing become good and bad in a single state, but rather in two states. If it is said: the command to slaughter the son in the story of Abraham, peace be upon him, was abrogated, and the very slaughter became good by the command and bad by the abrogation. He is told: that was not an abrogation of the ruling; rather, that same ruling was established, and abrogation is the termination of the ruling — it was not [terminated] but remained established, except that the locus to which it was added did not receive the ruling by way of ransom, not by abrogation. And that was a trial; the ruling of the command settled with the addressee, namely Abraham, peace be upon him, in the final state.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage from Imam al-Bazdawi's 'Kanz al-Wusul' (Usul al-Bazdawi) establishes the foundational rule for what can and cannot be abrogated in Islamic law. The author defines the locus of abrogation (mahall al-naskh) as a ruling that can be temporally conditioned. Two conditions must hold: (1) the ruling must be contingent—possible to exist or not exist—because eternal matters like Allah's attributes cannot be abrogated; (2) the ruling must not have an element attached that negates temporality, such as an explicit or implicit indication of perpetuity. The author then categorizes rulings that are not subject to abrogation: (a) Allah's names and attributes (eternal), (b) rulings explicitly made perpetual (e.g., eternal paradise in verse 4:57), (c) rulings implicitly perpetual (e.g., all laws of Prophet Muhammad because he is the last prophet), and (d) temporally fixed rulings before their expiry. Only one type can be abrogated: an absolute ruling that admits temporal conditioning and is not proven to continue. Using the example of purchase, the ruling (ownership) ceases when its cause is removed, not by a direct abrogator. The passage then addresses a potential objection from the story of Abraham and the command to sacrifice his son. The author clarifies that this was not abrogation: the command remained, but the original obligation was replaced by a ransom, and the test was to see whether Abraham would submit. Thus, the same act (slaughter) was not both good and bad; it was good when commanded and not performed later because the command was lifted. This preserves the integrity of legal rulings and avoids contradiction.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): The termination of the legal effect of a ruling by a later revelation, signifying the end of its applicability.
محل النسخ (mahall al-naskh): The locus of abrogation: a ruling that can be temporally conditioned (muqayyad bi-waqt) and is not proven to continue forever.
تأبيد (ta'bid): Perpetuity: a ruling established as eternal either by explicit text (nass) or by indication (dalala), such as the everlasting punishment of disbelief or the finality of the Shariah of Prophet Muhammad.
بداء (bada'): A change of opinion or manifestation of error, which is impossible for Allah; used to argue that abrogation is not a change of divine mind but a clarification of duration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following cannot be abrogated?
A) A ruling that is explicitly stated as eternal in the Quran
B) A ruling that is absolute and has no indication of perpetuity
C) A ruling that is temporally fixed, but its time has not yet ended
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi locus of abrogation is a contingent ruling with no attached perpetuity; Allah's attributes, explicitly perpetual rulings, the final Shariah, and fixed-term rulings before expiry are non-abrogable.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
8 min
Rulings Abrogable and Non-Abrogable in Hanafi Usul
Classifies rulings by abrogability and refutes an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Hanafi rulings by their capacity for naskh and apply the criteria to distinguish abrogation from trial (ibtilā').

Core Idea
Only absolute time-bound rulings without permanence evidence can be abrogated; Ibrahim's sacrifice was a trial, not naskh.

This passage from al-Pazdawi's Usul classifies rulings into those that can and cannot be abrogated. It then addresses a common objection: the command to Ibrahim to sacrifice his son is often cited as naskh, but the Hanafi school demonstrates it was a trial (ibtilā') and ransom (fidā'). Understanding this classification is essential for any usuli to protect divine transcendence (badā').

Guided Path
1
Identify the four non-abrogable categories
From the text, list: (1) rulings from previous prophets confirmed by the Prophet Muhammad, (2) the final sharia of the Prophet Muhammad due to his seal, (3) rulings that have already completed their term, (4) rulings that are time-bound and have expired. Each is permanently settled.
The common thread is that these rulings have evidence for permanence (baqā') or have already been fulfilled.
2
Identify the sole abrogable category
The only rulings that can accept naskh are those that are absolute (muṭlaq) and admit being time-bound (muwaqqat), and for which no evidence necessitates permanence. Example: purchase establishes ownership without permanence; the ruling 'ownership' exists only as long as the cause persists.
This category avoids the implication of badā' because the ruling ends by its own nature, not by a new divine decision.
3
Analyze the objection regarding Ibrahim
The objection states that the command to slaughter was given then removed, which fits the definition of naskh. The Hanafi response: the command was never intended to be executed as a final duty; its purpose was to test obedience. The ruling 'command' was established for Ibrahim, but the act was never performed; instead, a ransom replaced the slaughter.
The Qur'an calls it 'fidā'' (ransom), not 'naskh'.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The response hinges on two points: (1) naskh occurs only after the ruling's purpose is settled, which was not the case here; (2) the ruling remained intact, but the locus (the son) was substituted by a ransom. Thus, no termination of a settled ruling occurred.
This preserves the principle that naskh does not imply imperfect knowledge (badā') in Allah.
5
Explain the conceptual safeguard
By restricting naskh to time-bound rulings without permanence evidence, and by reinterpreting problematic cases as trials, the Hanafi school avoids attributing badā' to Allah and prevents a single act from being considered both good and evil in the same state.
The categories ensure that naskh is a rational, structured process, not a change of divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقِيَامَةِ آل عمران / ٥٥ : يريد بهم الذين صدقوا بمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. والقسم الثاني : مثل سائر شرائع النبي محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام التي قبض على قرارها فإنها مؤيَّدة لا تحتمل النسخ، بدلالة أن محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلم خاتم النبيين، ولا نبي بعده، ولا نسخ إلا بوحي على لسان نبي. والثالث : واضح، والنسخ فيه قبل الانتهاء باطل؛ لأن النسخ هذا كله بَداء وظهور الغلط، لا بيان المدة والله تعالى يتعالى عن ذلك. فصار الذي لا يحتمل النسخ أربعة أقسام في هذا الباب، والذي محل النسخ قسم واحد، وهو حكم مطلق يحتمل التوقيت، لم يجب بقاؤه بدليل يوجب البقاء، كالشراء يثبت به الملك دون البقاء، فينعدم الحكم لانعدام سببه لا بالناسخ بعينه، فلا يؤدي إلى التضاد والبداء، ولا يصير الشيء الواحد حسناً وقبيحاً في حال واحدة، بل في حالين. فإن قيل: إن الأمر بذبح الولد في قصة إبراهيم عليه السلام نُسخ، وصار الذبح بعينه حسناً بالأمر، قبيحاً بالنسخ. قيل له: لم يكن ذلك بنسخ للحكم، بل ذلك الحكم بعينه كان ثابتاً، والنسخ هو انتهاء الحكم، ولم يكن بل كان ثابتاً، إلا أن المحل الذي أُضيف إليه لم يحلَّه الحكم على طريق الفداء دون النسخ، وكان ذلك ابتلاء استقرَّ حكم الأمر عند المخاطب، وهو إبراهيم صلوات الله عليه في آخر الحال. على أن المتبغَى منه في حق الولد أن يصير قُرباناً بنسبة الحكم إليه، مُكرَماً بالفداء الحاصل لمعرَّة الذبح، مبتلى بالصبر والمجاهدة إلى حال المكاشفة، وإنما النسخ بعد استقرار المراد بالأمر، لا قبله، وقد سمي فداءً في الكتاب، لا نسخاً، فثبت أن النسخ لم يكن؛ لعدم ركنه، والله أعلم.
[...] refers to those who believed in Muhammad, peace be upon him. The second type: like the rest of the laws of Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, which were settled upon their final form, for they are confirmed and cannot accept abrogation, by the evidence that Muhammad, peace be upon him, is the seal of the prophets, and there is no prophet after him, and abrogation only occurs through revelation upon the tongue of a prophet. The third type is clear, and abrogation within it before completion is invalid, because all this abrogation would be badā' (appearance of error) and not a statement of duration, and Allah is exalted above that. Thus, what cannot accept abrogation has become four divisions in this chapter, and what accepts abrogation is one division: it is an absolute ruling that allows for being time-bound, whose permanence is not necessitated by evidence requiring permanence, like purchase establishes ownership without permanence, so the ruling ceases due to the cessation of its cause, not by an abrogator specifically, so it does not lead to contradiction or badā', nor does a single thing become good and bad in one state, but in two states. If it is said: The command to slaughter the son in the story of Ibrāhīm, peace be upon him, was abrogated, and the slaughter itself became good by the command and bad by abrogation. It is said to him: That was not an abrogation of the ruling, but that very ruling was established, and abrogation is the termination of the ruling, and it was not terminated but rather established, except that the locus to which it was added did not receive the ruling by way of ransom without abrogation, and that was a trial; the ruling of the command was established for the addressee, Ibrāhīm, peace be upon him, in the final state. Moreover, the intended goal regarding the son was that he become a sacrifice by the attribution of the ruling to him, honored by the ransom that occurred due to the dire consequences of slaughter, tested with patience and struggle until the state of unveiling. Abrogation only occurs after the intention of the command is established, not before it, and it was called ransom in the Book, not abrogation. So it is established that there was no abrogation due to the absence of its pillar. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a rule-based elaboration on the types of rulings that can and cannot be abrogated (naskh) in the Hanafi usul tradition. The author first categorizes rulings into those that cannot accept abrogation: (1) rulings from previous prophets that were confirmed by the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ as part of his sharia, (2) the established sharia of the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ itself because he is the last prophet and revelation has ceased, (3) rulings that have completed their term (like the direction of prayer changed after it was established), and (4) rulings that are time-bound and have already expired. The only type that can accept abrogation is a ruling that is absolute (muṭlaq) and admits the possibility of being time-bound (muwaqqat), and for which no evidence necessitates its permanence. The author then addresses a common objection: the command to Ibrāhīm to sacrifice his son is often cited as an example of naskh. The Hanafi response is that this was not naskh at all. The command was never intended to be carried out; it was a trial (ibtilā') to test Ibrāhīm's obedience. The ruling remained established for Ibrāhīm, but the actual slaughter was replaced by a ransom—this is fida' (ransom), not naskh. The author emphasizes that naskh is the termination of a ruling after its intended duration, and here the command's purpose was to test, not to mandate actual slaughter. Therefore, the core principle is preserved: abrogation does not imply badā' (change of divine will) and does not make an act both good and bad in the same state.

Key Terms
النسخ (al-naskh): رفع الحكم الشرعي بدليل شرعي متأخر
البداء (al-badā'): ظهور الرأي بعد خفائه، وهو محال على الله
التوقيت (al-tawqīt): كون الحكم مقدراً بمدة محددة تنتهي بانتهائها

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, naskh is strictly limited to absolute time-bound rulings without permanence evidence, and the story of Ibrahim is a trial, not a case of naskh.

The Condition for Naskh: Mental Commitment vs. Ability to Act
Dispute on the condition for naskh: Hanafi vs Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi condition for naskh and EVALUATE the Mu'tazila counterargument using the Mi'raj hadith.

Core Idea
Naskh only requires mental commitment, not physical ability — the heart's resolve is the true object of obligation.

In this passage, Imam al-Bazdawi clarifies a foundational rule in Hanafi usul: what must be present for abrogation (naskh) to be valid. The Mu'tazila argued that naskh before the commanded act is possible implies a change in divine knowledge (badā'), which is impossible. The Hanafi response draws on the famous Mi'raj hadith to show that only the heart's commitment matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested condition
What is the precise point of dispute? Both schools agree naskh requires a condition; they differ on what that condition is.
The condition is called shart in the text.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafis hold that tamakkun min 'aqd al-qalb (ability to mentally commit) is the condition. Their proof is the Mi'raj hadith.
Note the Arabic term: عقد القلب.
3
State the Mu'tazila position and its proof
The Mu'tazila argue that physical ability (tamakkun min al-fi'l) is the condition, otherwise naskh would imply badā' and error.
Badā' means a change in divine will, impossible for God.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal uses the Mi'raj hadith: fifty prayers were commanded then reduced to five before any prayer was performed. This proves naskh can occur without physical ability.
The hadith is the central dalil naqli.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this difference? For any command abrogated before anyone could act, Hanafis accept it as valid naskh; Mu'tazila reject it.
This shows that the difference affects the scope of acceptable naskh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن المتبغَى منه في حق الولد أن يصير قُرباناً بنسبة الحكم إليه، : مُكرَماً بالفداء الحاصل لمعرَّة الذبح، مبتلى بالصبر والمجاهدة إلى حال المكاشفة، وإنما النسخ بعد استقرار المراد بالأمر، لا قبله، وقد سمي فداءً في الكتاب، لا نسخاً، فثبت أن النسخ لم يكن؛ لعدم ركنه، والله أعلم.

وقالت المعتزلة : إنه شرط.

And naskh occurs only after the intended purpose of the command is settled, not before. And it is called 'ransom' in the Book, not 'abrogation'. So it is established that naskh did not occur due to the absence of its pillar. And Allah knows best.

In our view:
Section on the Explanation of the Condition
It is: the ability to contract the heart. As for the ability to act, it is not a condition.
And the Mu'tazila said: It is a condition.
The summary is that the ruling of naskh is the clarification of the duration for the action of the heart and the body together, or for the action of the heart alone. And the action of the heart is the decisive one in this according to us, and the other is from the extras.
And according to them, it is the clarification of the duration of bodily action. They said: Because bodily action is the intended purpose of every prohibition and every command explicitly. It is said: 'Do such and such, and do not do such and such.' So it necessitates its goodness by the command necessarily, and its evil by the prohibition. And if naskh occurs before the act and its possibility, it becomes in the sense of badā' and error.
And the proof for us: That the Prophet ﷺ was commanded with fifty prayers on the night of the Mi'raj, then what exceeded five was abrogated, and that was after the commitment before
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi school and the Mu'tazila regarding the condition (shart) for naskh (abrogation). The author first explains the Hanafi position: the condition for naskh is the ability to mentally commit (tamakkun min 'aqd al-qalb) to obey the command, not the physical ability to perform the act (tamakkun min al-fi'l). This is based on the principle that the primary object of taklif (obligation) is the heart's submission. The Mu'tazila argue that physical ability is a condition because the purpose of commands and prohibitions is bodily action; otherwise, abrogation before the possibility of acting would imply badā' (change of divine mind) and error. The Hanafi counterproof is the well-known hadith of the Mi'raj: fifty daily prayers were commanded but then abrogated to five before any prayer was actually performed. This demonstrates that naskh can occur after mental commitment but before physical ability. Thus, the condition is only mental commitment, not physical ability.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): The condition for the validity of naskh: the ability to mentally commit to obey the command.
عقد القلب ('aqd al-qalb): Mental commitment or intention to obey the command; the heart's resolve.
البداء (al-badā'): A change in divine will or knowledge, which is impossible for God. The Mu'tazila argue that abrogation before possibility of action implies badā'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the condition (shart) for naskh?
A) The ability to physically perform the commanded act.
B) The ability to mentally commit to the command.
C) The actual performance of the act.
D) The expiration of a fixed time period.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that naskh is valid as long as the heart can commit to obey; physical performance is not a condition, as shown by the Mi'raj hadith.

The Condition (Shart) for Naskh: Intellectual Assent vs. Action
Defining the shart for naskh and refuting the Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid naskh and explain why the ability to act is not required, using the hadith of Isra' and Mi'rāj as proof.

Core Idea
Intellectual assent, not action, is the only condition for naskh in Hanafi usul.

This lesson addresses a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: what must have occurred for an abrogated ruling to be validly removed? The Hanafi school argues that only the intellectual acceptance of the ruling is necessary, while the Mu'tazila insist that one must have been able to perform the act. Understanding this disagreement clarifies the nature of divine commands and the role of human intention.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the shart for naskh
Identify the definition given at the beginning of the passage: 'It is: the ability to intellectually assent; as for the ability to act, it is not a condition.'
The key term is 'tamakkun min 'aqd al-qalb'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argue that the ability to act is a condition; otherwise naskh before action would imply badā' (change in God's will) and error.
Their reasoning: commands explicitly target bodily action.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof from the Isra' and Mi'rāj hadith
The Prophet was commanded fifty prayers, then abrogated to five after he had assented but before he could have performed any. This proves intellectual assent suffices.
The hadith is cited as dalīl naqlī.
4
Analyze the additional Hanafi arguments
The passage also appeals to mutashābihāt (requiring only belief) and to the consensus that naskh after partial action is valid, showing the heart's intention can be the sole object.
These strengthen the proof by analogy and induction.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
For Hanafis, a command can be abrogated before anyone acts upon it; for Mu'tazila, such abrogation would be invalid unless the command was never intended to be acted upon.
Example: the reduction of prayers from fifty to five.

Chapter on explaining the condition. It is: the ability to intellectually assent [to the ruling]. As for the ability to act, it is not a condition. The Mu'tazila said: it is a condition. The summary is that the ruling of abrogation is specifying the duration for the action of the heart and the body together, or for the action of the heart alone, and the action of the heart is the decisive one according to us, and the other is from the additions. According to them, it is specifying the duration for the action of the body. They said: because the action of the body is the intended object of every prohibition and every command by way of explicit text. It is said: 'Do such-and-such' and 'Do not do such-and-such', which necessarily entails its goodness by the command and its repugnance by the prohibition. And if abrogation occurs before the act and its possibility, it becomes a matter of badā' (change of mind) and error. And the proof for us is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was commanded with fifty prayers on the night of the Mi'rāj, then what exceeded five was abrogated. That was after intellectual assent but before the ability to act, because he is the original of this ummah. Thus abrogation was valid after his intellectual assent existed, and there was no ability to act. Also because abrogation is valid by consensus after part of the act has been performed, or after a period sufficient for part of it, even if the apparent command encompasses all of it. Because the lesser can be intended as a trial. Similarly, intellectual assent to the goodness of the commanded thing and its truth can be intended separately from the act. Do you not see that Allah tests us with what is mutashābih (unclear) wherein we are only required to believe in its truth? That indicates that intellectual assent is valid as a basis. And because an act is not an act of worship except with determination of the heart, and determination of the heart can become an act of worship without an act. The act is more prone to being dropped than determination. If that is so, then it is valid for it to be intended apart from the act. Do you not see that the essence of goodness is not established by the ability to act. And the statement of the speaker: 'Do [something] by way of obedience' is a command for intellectual assent, necessarily. So it is possible that one of the two matters is intended necessarily, and the other is ambiguous between the two. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi school and the Mu'tazila regarding the condition (shart) for the validity of naskh (abrogation). The author, Imam al-Bazdawi, begins by defining the shart as the ability to intellectually assent ('tamakkun min 'aqd al-qalb') to the ruling being abrogated. He denies that the ability to act upon the ruling is a condition; the Mu'tazila affirm it as a condition. The summary of the matter is that naskh is the specification of the duration for which either both the heart's action (i.e., belief) and the body's action are required, or only the heart's action. According to Hanafis, the heart's action (assent) is the decisive element, and the body's action is additional. The Mu'tazila argue that the body's action is the intended object of all commands and prohibitions, and if naskh occurs before the act is possible, it would imply badā' (a change in God's will) and error, which is impossible. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of the Isra' and Mi'rāj, where the Prophet was commanded with fifty prayers but later abrogated to five. This abrogation occurred after the Prophet had assented to the command but before he could have performed any of the fifty prayers. Since the Prophet is the exemplar for the ummah, this proves that intellectual assent alone suffices for the ruling to be abrogated. Further evidence is that naskh is valid by consensus after partial performance of the act, which shows that the duration can be specified after some action, and by analogy, the heart's assent can be the intended object separate from action. Additionally, Allah tests believers with unclear verses (mutashābihāt) that only require belief in their truth, not action. Thus the heart's determination can be a valid object of worship independent of action.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): The condition for the validity of naskh: according to Hanafis, it is the ability to intellectually assent to the ruling (tamakkun min 'aqd al-qalb), not the ability to act.
عقد القلب ('aqd al-qalb): Intellectual assent or conviction of the heart regarding the ruling being commanded or prohibited.
التمكن من الفعل (al-tamakkun min al-fi'l): The ability to physically perform the commanded act within the designated time frame.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the condition (shart) for the validity of naskh (abrogation)?
A) The ability to physically perform the commanded act (al-tamakkun min al-fi'l).
B) The intellectual assent to the ruling ('aqd al-qalb).
C) Both intellectual assent and the ability to act.
D) The presence of a new revelation abrogating the previous ruling.

Key Takeaway
The ability to intellectually assent to a ruling is the only condition for its valid abrogation in Hanafi usul.

Naskh Before Action: The Hanafi Proof from Intention
Rational proof that naskh is valid before action is possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of naskh before action and analyze the proof that intention (aqd al-qalb) can be the sole objective of a command.

Core Idea
Naskh is valid immediately upon command because intention itself can be the objective of the command.

A common assumption is that abrogation (naskh) requires the commanded act to be at least partially performed. The Hanafi school disagrees, presenting a rational proof based on the nature of intention. This lesson unpacks that proof, showing that the command itself, even without action, is a sufficient trial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The passage argues that naskh is valid before the commanded action is possible. Note the key term 'تمكن من الفعل' (ability to act).
The passage uses the Prophet as the origin of the ummah to establish that his command alone creates obligation.
2
Understand the analogy with partial action
The author compares naskh before action to naskh after partial action, which is accepted by consensus. If naskh is valid after part of the act, why not before any action?
The lesser (before action) is included in the greater (after partial action) because the trial is already present.
3
Grasp the concept of 'عقد القلب' (intention)
The passage argues that intention itself can be a separate objective. It cites the example of mutashabih (ambiguous) verses, where only belief (intention) is required, not action.
This shows that a command can be about intention alone, independent of action.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith of Musa (AS) on the night journey shows that Allah abrogated the command of fifty prayers before the ummah performed them. This is direct evidence of naskh before action.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for this proof in Hanafi usul.
5
Synthesize the argument
Combine: intention can be the objective; the command itself is a trial; naskh after partial action is valid; therefore naskh before action is also valid.
The passage concludes that either intention or action may be the necessary objective, and naskh can target the command alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And because abrogation is valid by consensus after the existence of part of the act, or a period suitable for ability to perform part of it, even though the apparent command encompasses all of it; because the lesser is suitable as an intended objective for trial, similarly the intention of the heart regarding the goodness of the commanded thing, and regarding its truth, is suitable to be a distinct objective from the act. Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the validity of naskh (abrogation) before the commanded action is possible. The author argues that just as naskh is valid after partial performance of an act or after a period sufficient for partial performance, it is also valid after the command itself is issued but before any ability to act. The basis is that the command itself is a trial, and the intention of the heart (عقد القلب) to obey is a separable objective. The proof from the Prophet (peace be upon him) being the origin of the ummah means that his command alone creates obligation, and naskh can occur immediately after the command, as happened with the abrogation of facing Jerusalem. The hadith of Musa (peace be upon him) about the reduction of prayers is cited as evidence that Allah abrogated the command of fifty prayers before the community actually performed them, proving that naskh does not require prior action. The author uses the analogy of Allah's test with ambiguous verses (متشابهات) which require only belief, not action, to show that intention can be the sole purpose of a command. Thus, the commanded act is not the only objective; the command itself may be intended to elicit intention.

Key Terms
عقد القلب (aqd al-qalb): The intention or determination of the heart to obey a command, considered as a separate legal objective from the physical act.
تمكن من الفعل (tamakkun min al-fi'l): Ability to perform the act; a condition for the obligation to be fully binding in some views.

Key Takeaway
Naskh before action is valid because the command itself, and the intention it elicits, constitutes a sufficient trial.

Types of Naskh: Sources and their Validity
Definition and classification of naskh types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of naskh according to the Hanafi school and identify which sources (Quran, Sunnah, ijma, qiyas) are valid for abrogation and why.

Core Idea
Only Quran and Sunnah can abrogate; ijma and qiyas cannot.

In usul al-fiqh, understanding which sources can abrogate is fundamental. This passage from al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul defines the four proofs and then limits valid naskh to the Quran and Sunnah, with a key disagreement with al-Shafi'i. Let's break it down step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Proofs
The passage opens by naming the four sources (hujaj) of Islamic law: the Book (Quran), Sunnah, Ijma (consensus), and Qiyas (analogy). Recognize that each can theoretically serve as a source of rulings.
These are the primary sources in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand why Qiyas is Invalid for Naskh
The author states that qiyas is not valid as an abrogator, with a promise to explain later. For now, note that analogy, being a derived source, cannot overrule a primary revelation.
The full reason involves the nature of qiyas as ijtihad.
3
Understand why Ijma is Invalid for Naskh
The passage argues that ijma cannot abrogate because naskh only occurs during the Prophet's lifetime, and ijma is not a proof (hujja) while the Prophet is alive—his opinion is authoritative and there is no consensus without him.
This is a key Hanafi point: naskh is a legislative act that ceased with the Prophet's death.
4
List the Four Valid Divisions
Valid naskh is limited to the Quran and Sunnah, yielding four combinations: (1) Quran by Quran, (2) Sunnah by Sunnah, (3) Sunnah by Quran, and (4) Quran by Sunnah. Memorize these four types.
The Hanafi school accepts all four.
5
Note the Shafi'i Objection
Imam al-Shafi'i rejects the last two types (where the abrogating source differs in kind from the abrogated source). Understand that this ikhtilaf has practical consequences.
Example: The hadith 'No will for an heir' abrogating Quran 2:180 is accepted by Hanafis but rejected by Shafi'is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان تقسيم الناسخ الحجج أربع : الكتاب والسنة، والإجماع، والقياس. أما القياس: فلا يصلح ناسخاً؛ لما نبين إن شاء الله تعالى. وأما الإجماع: فقد ذكر بعض المتأخرين أنه يصح النسخ به، والصحيح أن النسخ به لا يكون لأن النسخ لا يكون إلا في حياة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. والإجماع ليس بحجة في حياته صلى الله عليه وسلم؛ لأنه لا إجماع دون رأيه، والرجوع إليه فرض، وإذا وجد منه البيان: كان منفرداً بذلك، لا محالة، وإذا صار الإجماع واجب العمل به: لم يبق النسخ مشروعاً بعده. وإنما يجوز النسخ بالكتاب والسنة، وذلك أربعة أقسام: ١ - نسخ الكتاب بالكتاب. ٢- والسنة بالسنة. ٣- ونَسْخُ السنة بالكتاب. -٤ - ونَسْخُ الكتاب بالسنة. وذلك كله جائز عندنا. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله بفساد القسمين الأخيرين.
Chapter on clarifying the division of the abrogator. The proofs are four: the Book, the Sunnah, consensus, and analogy. As for analogy, it is not valid as an abrogator, as we will explain if Allah wills. As for consensus, some later scholars have mentioned that abrogation by it is valid, but the correct view is that abrogation by it does not occur, because abrogation only occurs during the lifetime of the Prophet (peace be upon him). Consensus is not a proof during his lifetime, because there is no consensus without his opinion, and referring to him is obligatory. When his clarification is found, he is unique in that, inevitably. And when consensus becomes obligatory to act upon, abrogation is no longer legislated after it. However, abrogation is permissible by the Book and the Sunnah, and that is four divisions: 1. Abrogation of the Book by the Book. 2. Abrogation of the Sunnah by the Sunnah. 3. Abrogation of the Sunnah by the Book. 4. Abrogation of the Book by the Sunnah. All of that is permissible in our view. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said that the last two divisions are invalid.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This is a chapter heading that defines the types of naskh (abrogation) and the sources that can serve as abrogators. The author identifies four proofs (حجج) in Islamic law: the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and qiyas. He states that qiyas is not valid as an abrogator, and consensus is also not valid because naskh can only occur during the Prophet's lifetime, and consensus does not exist when the Prophet is alive as his opinion is authoritative. Thus, valid abrogation is only from the Quran and Sunnah, yielding four combinations: (1) Quran abrogating Quran, (2) Sunnah abrogating Sunnah, (3) Sunnah abrogated by Quran, and (4) Quran abrogated by Sunnah. The Hanafi school accepts all four. The author notes that Imam al-Shafi'i rejects the last two (abrogation of the Quran by the Sunnah and abrogation of the Sunnah by the Quran). This is a classic ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
ناسخ (nasikh): The abrogating evidence or text that removes or replaces a previous ruling.
حجج (hujaj): Proofs or sources of Islamic law; in this passage, the four primary sources.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the ummah after the Prophet's death on a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which types of abrogation does Imam al-Shafi'i consider invalid?
A) Abrogation of the Book by the Book and Sunnah by Sunnah
B) Abrogation of the Sunnah by the Book and the Book by the Sunnah
C) Abrogation by consensus and qiyas
D) All abrogation is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, naskh is valid only from Quran and Sunnah, and all four cross-combinations are accepted, contrary to al-Shafi'i who rejects cross-abrogation.

Inter-Abrogation of Quran and Sunnah: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Dividing naskh into types and presenting Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of naskh according to the Hanafi school and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument against the last two types.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits abrogation of Quran by Sunnah and vice versa; Shafi'i restricts naskh to like-for-like only.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces the classification of abrogation (naskh) and immediately confronts the central dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. While both agree that naskh can occur between Quran and Quran or Sunnah and Sunnah, they differ sharply on cross-type abrogation. The Hanafi school defends the permissibility of all four combinations, while Imam al-Shafi'i restricts naskh to parallel textual categories. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for reconciling seemingly conflicting primary texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proofs and which can abrogate
The author lists the four sources: Quran, Sunnah, ijma', and qiyas. He immediately excludes qiyas and ijma' as abrogators, leaving only Quran and Sunnah. Verify the reasoning for each exclusion.
Qiyas cannot abrogate because it derives rulings; ijma' cannot because naskh ended at the Prophet's death.
2
Examine the four types of naskh
The permissible types are: 1) Quran by Quran, 2) Sunnah by Sunnah, 3) Sunnah by Quran, 4) Quran by Sunnah. Note that the Hanafi school accepts all four.
This is the Hanafi position, stated as 'jā'iz 'indanā'.
3
State the Shafi'i objection and its proofs
Imam al-Shafi'i rejects the last two types (cross-abrogation). His proofs include: Quran 2:106 (implies like-for-like), Quran 10:15 (Prophet cannot change revelation), the hadith to present to Quran, and a rational argument to prevent criticism.
List each proof and its textual basis.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response using the bequest example
The Hanafi response cites Quran 2:180 (bequest to parents/relatives) abrogated by the Prophet's hadith 'No bequest to an heir'. This is presented as clear evidence of Sunnah abrogating Quran. Understand how this counters the Shafi'i arguments.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but the Hanafi school accepts it as abrogating.
5
Determine the practical impact (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: for Hanafis, a bequest to an heir is invalid due to the hadith abrogating the verse; for Shafi'is, the verse remains operative, so such a bequest is valid.
This example illustrates how the dispute affects actual fiqh rulings.
