Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
12 min
Types of Naskh and Defending Heavier Abrogation
Explains types of naskh and defends heavier abrogation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of naskh mentioned in the text and evaluate the Hanafi response to the objection that abrogation must be lighter or equal.

Core Idea
Naskh can bring a heavier ruling because 'better' refers to worldly benefit, not the weight of the obligation.

This passage from a Hanafi usul work classifies different types of naskh (abrogation): the Quran abrogating the Sunnah, the Sunnah abrogating the Sunnah, and a solitary report abrogating another. It then addresses a key dispute: is it permissible for the abrogating ruling to be more burdensome than the original? The opponent cites Quran 2:106 ('We bring a better one or its like') to argue that abrogation must be to an equal or lighter ruling. The Hanafi school defends the permissibility of heavier abrogation by reinterpreting 'better' as referring to worldly benefits, not the weight of the ruling itself.

Guided Path
1
List the types of naskh
Identify the three categories of abrogation explicitly mentioned: (1) the Book (Quran) abrogating the Sunnah, (2) the Sunnah abrogating the Sunnah, (3) a solitary report (khabar wahid) abrogating another like it.
The text also implies but does not discuss Quran abrogating Quran; it focuses on cross-category abrogation.
2
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent argues from Quran 2:106 that the abrogating ruling must be 'better' or 'like' the abrogated one, which they interpret as lighter or equal in burden. Note the apparent meaning that supports them.
The opponent is referred to as 'ba'dhuhum' (some scholars), likely Mu'tazila or early usulis.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the objection
The Hanafi response reinterprets 'better' (khayr) as referring to worldly benefits (masalih al-'ibad), not the level of obligation. They also argue that the heavier ruling brings greater otherworldly reward, so it is still 'better' in a different sense.
The text cites examples: abrogation of option in Ramadan fasting to obligation, and the stages of jihad from forgiveness to fighting all polytheists.
4
Apply the distinction to the examples
For each Hanafi example, confirm that the abrogating ruling is indeed more burdensome than the original, and explain how the 'better' still applies (e.g., the fasting obligation leads to more reward).
Examine the fasting example: from takhyir (choice) to 'azimat (determination/obligation).
5
State the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: Hanafis accept abrogation where a light obligation is replaced by a heavy one (e.g., fasting from optional to obligatory); opponents would reject such abrogation, considering it invalid.
This affects rulings derived from earlier and later revealed texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلامة وأما الحديث : فدليل على أن الكتاب يجوز أن يَنسخ السُّنَّةَ. وتأويل الحديث أن العرض على الكتاب: إنما يجب فيما أشكل و تاريخه، أو لم يكن في الصحة، بحيث يُنسخ به الكتاب، فكان تقديم الكتاب أولى. فأما قوله جل وعلا: نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا ﴾ البقرة / ١٠٦: فإن المراد بالخيرية : فيما يرجع إلى مرافق العباد دون النظم بمعناه، فكذلك المماثلة. على أنا قد بينا أن نسخ حكم الكتاب بالسنة خارج عن هذه الجملة. ونَسْخُ السنَّة بالسنة : مثل قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إني كنتُ نهيتكم عن زيارة القبور ألا فزوروها ، فقد أذن لمحمد في زيارة قبر أمه وكنت نهيتكم عن لحوم الأضاحي أن تُمسكوها فوق ثلاثة أيام: فأمسكوها ما بدا لكم، وكنتُ نهيتكم عن النبيذ في الدباء والحَنْتَم والنقير والمُرَفَّت، فاشربوا في كل ظرف فإن الظرف لا يُحِلَّ شيئاً ، ولا يُحرمه) . ونَسْخُ خبر الواحد بمثله : جائز أيضاً.
ويجوز أن يكون حكم الناسخ أشقَّ من حكم المنسوخ عندنا؛ لأن الله تبارك وتعالى نَسَخ التخيير في صوم رمضان بعزيمة الصيام. ونَسَخَ الصفحَ والعفو عن الكفار بقتال الذين يقاتلون، فقال: وَقَتَتِلُوا فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ الَّذِينَ يُقَتِلُونَكُمْ ﴾ البقرة / ١٩٠، ثم نسخه بقتالهم كافةً :بقوله: ﴿وَقَئِلُواْ الْمُشْرِكِينَ كَافَةَ التوبة/٣٦، والناسخ أشقُ ههنا. وقال بعضهم : لا يصح إلا بمثله أو بأخف منه ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا نَنسَحْ مِنْ ءَايَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا ﴾ البقرة / ١٠٦ . والجواب : أن ذلك فيما يرجع إلى مرافق العباد، وفي الأشق: فضلُ ثواب الآخرة، والله أعلم.
Peace. As for the hadith: it is evidence that the Book may abrogate the Sunnah. The interpretation of the hadith is that presenting to the Book is only obligatory concerning what is ambiguous and its date, or it was not in authenticity such that the Book would be abrogated by it. So giving precedence to the Book is more appropriate. As for His statement, Exalted and Glorified: 'We bring a better one or its like' (al-Baqarah 2:106): the intended meaning of 'better' is with regard to what pertains to the benefits of the servants, not the wording in its meaning, and similarly for 'like'. However, we have already explained that abrogation of the ruling of the Book by the Sunnah is excluded from this statement. And the abrogation of the Sunnah by the Sunnah: such as the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'I used to forbid you from visiting graves; now visit them, for Muhammad has been permitted to visit his mother's grave. And I used to forbid you from keeping the meat of sacrificial animals for more than three days; now keep it as long as you wish. And I used to forbid you from drinking from [vessels made of] gourds, green jars, hollowed palm stumps, and varnished containers; now drink from any container, for the container does not make anything lawful or unlawful.' And the abrogation of a solitary report by one like it is also permissible. And it is permissible according to us that the abrogating ruling be more burdensome than the abrogated ruling; because Allah, Blessed and Exalted, abrogated the option in the fast of Ramadan by the determination of fasting. And He abrogated turning away and forgiving the disbelievers by fighting those who fight you, saying: 'Fight in the way of Allah those who fight you' (al-Baqarah 2:190), then He abrogated it by fighting them all in His saying: 'Fight the polytheists all together' (al-Tawbah 9:36), and the abrogating ruling is more burdensome here. And some said: it is not valid except with one like it or lighter; because of His statement, Exalted: 'Whatever verse We abrogate or cause to be forgotten, We bring a better one or its like' (al-Baqarah 2:106). The answer: that pertains to the benefits of the servants, and in the more burdensome one there is the merit of the reward of the Hereafter. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Fiqh (Hanafi) discusses types of naskh (abrogation) and a related dispute. It begins by stating that the Quran can abrogate the Sunnah, supported by a hadith that is interpreted to mean that only when a hadith is ambiguous or its date unclear should it be presented to the Quran; otherwise the Book takes precedence. The Quranic verse 2:106 is explained: 'better' refers to worldly benefits, not to the wording, so abrogation can bring a ruling that is more beneficial or equal in benefit. Hanafis hold that abrogation of a Quranic ruling by Sunnah is separate from this verse. Then, examples are given of Sunnah abrogating Sunnah: the Prophet (peace be upon him) abrogated his earlier prohibition of visiting graves, keeping sacrificial meat beyond three days, and drinking from certain vessels. Abrogation of a solitary report (khabar wahid) by another like it is also allowed. The passage then affirms the Hanafi position that an abrogating ruling can be more burdensome than the abrogated one, citing examples: the abrogation of the option (takhyir) to fast or not by the obligation of fasting in Ramadan, and the stages of jihad from forgiveness to fighting aggressors to fighting all polytheists. An opponent (some scholars) argues that abrogation must be to an equal or lighter ruling, based on 'better or like'. The Hanafi response: the verse refers to worldly benefits, and the more burdensome ruling carries greater otherworldly reward, so it is still 'better' in that sense. This reasoning preserves the principle that Allah's abrogation can increase obligation as a test and for greater reward.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation: lifting a pre-existing ruling by a later Shar'i evidence.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A solitary report (a hadith not reaching the level of tawatur).
التخيير (al-takhyir): Option: the original ruling in Ramadan where the Companions had the choice to fast or feed the poor.
المماثلة (al-mumathalah): Likeness: the abrogating ruling can be similar in weight to the abrogated one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following best explains why an abrogating ruling can be more burdensome?
A) Because Allah's desire to test His servants is the sole purpose.
B) Because 'better' in the Quranic verse refers to worldly benefits, while heavier rulings have greater otherworldly reward.
C) Because the Prophet's hadiths explicitly command heavier rulings.
D) Because the majority of scholars agree on this.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the permissibility of heavier abrogation is established by interpreting 'better' in Quran 2:106 as referring to worldly benefits, and by examples from the Quran itself.

Naskh — Abrogation

Permissibility of Abrogation by a More Severe Ruling
Author argues that the abrogating ruling can be more severe, citing Quranic examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the abrogating ruling can be more severe than the abrogated ruling, and evaluate the opposing argument from Quran 2:106.

Core Idea
Abrogation can be more severe; the 'better' in Quran 2:106 refers to worldly ease, while difficulty brings greater spiritual reward.

In this passage, the author defends the Hanafi view that an abrogating ruling (nasikh) can be more severe than the one it replaces. This is a point of dispute with some scholars who argue from Quran 2:106 that the replacement must be 'better or similar.' The Hanafi response distinguishes between worldly benefits and otherworldly rewards. We will examine the evidence and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school permits the abrogating ruling to be more severe than the abrogated one.
This is the central claim being defended.
2
Identify the opponent's position and their dalil
Recognize that the opponent argues naskh must be to an equal or lighter ruling, citing Quran 2:106.
The verse 'We bring better or similar to it' is their proof.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand that the Hanafi school interprets 'better' as referring to worldly ease (marafi' al-ibad), while a more severe ruling is 'better' due to greater reward in the Hereafter.
This response neutralizes the opponent's dalil.
4
Examine the Quranic examples
Look at the two examples: abrogation of the option in fasting to obligatory fasting, and progression from fighting only those who fight to fighting all polytheists.
Both show increasing severity.
5
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference is that Hanafis allow more severe naskh, while opponents restrict it to equal or lighter rulings.
This affects how one understands the progression of rulings in the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز أن يكون حكم الناسخ أشقَّ من حكم المنسوخ عندنا؛ لأن الله تبارك وتعالى نَسَخ التخيير في صوم رمضان بعزيمة الصيام. ونَسَخَ الصفحَ والعفو عن الكفار بقتال الذين يقاتلون، فقال: ﴿وَقَاتِلُوا فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ الَّذِينَ يُقَاتِلُونَكُمْ﴾ سورة البقرة / ١٩٠، ثم نسخه بقتالهم كافةً بقوله: ﴿وَقَاتِلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ كَافَّةً﴾ سورة التوبة/٣٦، والناسخ أشقُّ هنا. وقال بعضهم : لا يصح إلا بمثله أو بأخف منه ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا نَنسَخْ مِنْ ءَايَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا ﴾ سورة البقرة / ١٠٦ . والجواب : أن ذلك فيما يرجع إلى مرافق العباد، وفي الأشق: فضلُ ثواب الآخرة، والله أعلم.
And it is permissible for the abrogating ruling to be more severe than the abrogated ruling according to us (Hanafis), because Allah the Blessed and Exalted abrogated the option in fasting Ramadan with the determination of fasting. And He abrogated pardoning and forgiving the disbelievers with fighting those who fight you, so He said: 'And fight in the way of Allah those who fight you' (Al-Baqarah 2:190), then He abrogated it by fighting them all together with His statement: 'And fight the polytheists all together' (At-Tawbah 9:36), and the abrogating (ruling) is more severe here. And some (opponents) said: It is not valid except with a similar or lighter (ruling), because of His statement: 'Whatever verse We abrogate or cause to be forgotten, We bring better or similar to it' (Al-Baqarah 2:106). The answer: That (the verse) pertains to what concerns the benefits of the servants, and in the more severe (ruling) there is the superiority of reward in the Hereafter, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute in Usul al-Fiqh regarding whether the abrogating ruling (nasikh) can be more severe than the abrogated one (mansukh). The Hanafi position (عندنا) affirms this possibility. The author provides two Quranic examples: the abrogation of the optional choice in fasting (between fasting or feeding) during Ramadan to the obligatory fasting, and the progression from forgiving disbelievers to fighting only those who fight, then to fighting all polytheists. Both show increasing severity. The opponent (likely some Shafi'is or others) argues from Quran 2:106 that the replacement must be better or similar, implying equal or lighter. The Hanafi response distinguishes: 'better' here refers to worldly benefits and ease for people, while the more severe ruling carries greater spiritual reward, thus it is 'better' for the afterlife. The phrase 'فضل ثواب الآخرة' indicates that the superiority in reward compensates for the increased difficulty.

Key Terms
ناسخ (nasikh): The abrogating ruling or verse that replaces a previous one.
منسوخ (mansukh): The abrogated ruling or verse that is replaced.
نسخ (naskh): The process of abrogation, where a later ruling supersedes an earlier one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, can the abrogating ruling be more severe than the abrogated one?
A) No, it must be equal or lighter.
B) Yes, it can be more severe because of greater reward.
C) Yes, but only if it is exactly similar.
D) No, abrogation is only possible for lighter rulings.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits naskh by a more severe ruling because the 'better' in Quran 2:106 refers to worldly ease, while severity brings greater reward in the afterlife.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
6 min
The Four Types of Abrogation (Naskh) in Hanafi Usul
Author lists the four types of abrogation based on what is abrogated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of abrogation (naskh) in the Hanafi classification and provide an example for each.

Core Idea
Abrogation can affect the text, the ruling, or both—and even a description within a ruling.

This lesson encapsulates the Hanafi classification of naskh. While you may have encountered the concept of abrogation in earlier studies, this passage presents the precise categories: naskh of tilawa and hukm together, naskh of hukm alone, naskh of tilawa alone, and naskh of a wasf within a hukm. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly interpreting abrogated verses.

Guided Path
1
List the four categories
From the passage, extract the four types: (1) tilawa wa al‑hukm, (2) al‑hukm duna al‑tilawa, (3) al‑tilawa bi‑la hukm, (4) naskh wasf fi al‑hukm.
The author uses dashes for enumeration.
2
Define each type
For each type, state what is abrogated and what remains: Type 1 – both text and ruling are removed; Type 2 – only the ruling is removed, the text remains; Type 3 – only the text is removed, the ruling remains; Type 4 – a description (e.g., condition) within the ruling is removed.
3
Recall known examples from Hanafi works
For Type 3, the classic example is the verse of stoning (rajm) – its recitation is abrogated but the ruling of stoning remains. For Type 2, the verse of the sword (ayat al‑sayf) is said to abrogate many earlier peaceful verses while remaining in the Quran. For Type 1, the verse of secret counsel (Surah 58:12) is sometimes cited. For Type 4, consider the abrogation of the condition of 'other than believers' in a ruling.
The author notes that details have preceded in earlier chapters.
4
Appreciate the unique Hanafi type
Type 4 (naskh al‑wasf) is specific to the Hanafi school; it shows that abrogation can target a single attribute or condition without changing the ruling’s core.
This type is not recognized by all schools.
5
Summarize the classification system
Write a short summary of the four types in Arabic and English, verifying that you can reproduce them from memory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنسوخ أنواع: ١ - التلاوة والحكم. ٢ - الحكم دون التلاوة. ٣ - التلاوة بلا حكم. ٤ - نسخ وصف في الحكم. باب تفصيل المنسوخ : تقدم جميع ما فيه في الأبواب قبله.
The abrogated (naskh) is of types: 1 - The recitation and the ruling. 2 - The ruling without the recitation. 3 - The recitation without ruling. 4 - Abrogation of a description in the ruling. Chapter on detailing the abrogated: all that is in it has preceded in the previous chapters.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of abrogation (naskh) based on what is abrogated. The first type is abrogation of both the Quranic verse (tilawa) and its legal ruling (hukm), meaning the verse is removed from memory and the ruling no longer applies. The second type abrogates only the ruling while the verse remains in the Quran. The third type abrogates the recitation of the verse but the ruling remains (e.g., the verse of stoning). The fourth type abrogates a description (wasf) within a ruling, such as abrogating the condition of 'other than believers' in a ruling. The author notes that this classification has already been covered in previous chapters, implying this is a summary.

Key Terms
تلاوة (tilawa): The recited Quranic text (verse) itself.
حكم (hukm): The legal ruling derived from a verse.
وصف (wasf): A description or condition within a ruling that is abrogated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the classification of abrogation, which type involves abrogating the Quranic verse but keeping its ruling?
A) Abrogation of recitation and ruling
B) Abrogation of ruling without recitation
C) Abrogation of recitation without ruling
D) Abrogation of a description

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes four distinct types of naskh, which affect the text (tilawa), the ruling (hukm), or a description (wasf) within the ruling.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
10 min
The Four Types of Naskh: Classification and Examples
Classifies four types of naskh and provides examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of naskh according to al-Bazdawi and provide an example for each.

Core Idea
Naskh can abrogate recitation, ruling, or both, and an addition to a text is also a form of abrogation.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul classifies abrogation into four distinct categories. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly interpreting which Quranic rulings are abrogated and which texts remain operative parts of the Mushaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
The author lists four types: (1) both recitation and ruling abrogated, (2) ruling abrogated but recitation remains, (3) recitation abrogated but ruling remains, (4) abrogation of a description in the ruling (addition to the text).
Types 2 and 3 are validated by the majority; type 4 is a distinctive Hanafi view.
2
Understand the first type
Abrogation of both recitation and ruling occurred only during the Prophet's lifetime, e.g., the scriptures of Ibrahim. After the Prophet, the Quran is protected from any such abrogation (al-Hijr 15:9).
The Quranic verses (al-A'la 87:6-7, al-Baqarah 2:106) prove permissibility during prophetic times.
3
Understand the second and third types
These are accepted by the majority. Examples: the verse on keeping adulteresses (ruling abrogated) and the reading of Ibn Mas'ud (recitation abrogated). The text retains inherent rulings: permissibility of recitation in prayer and miraculous nature.
The objection that a text cannot exist without its ruling is refuted by these inherent rulings.
4
Understand the fourth type
Addition to the text (ziyada) is considered naskh because it changes the ruling, e.g., adding a condition. This is a distinctively Hanafi position.
Shafi'is consider such addition as clarification (bayan) unless it is separate and contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنسوخ () أنواع:
١ - التلاوة والحكم.
ـ والحكم دون التلاوة.
و
والتلاوة بلا حكم.
باب
تفصيل المنسوخ
(۱) باب تفصيل المنسوخ : تقدم جميع ما فيه في الأبواب قبله.
٤- ونَسْخُ وصف في الحكم.
أما نسخ التلاوة والحكم جميعاً فمثل: صحف إبراهيم عليه السلام، فإنها نُسخت أصلاً ، إما بصرفها عن القلوب، أو بموت العلماء، وكان هذا جائزاً في القرآن في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، قال الله تبارك وتعالى: سَنُقْرِئُكَ فَلَا تَنسَى إِلَّا مَا شَاءَ الله ... الأعلى /٦ - ٧ .
وقال جل جلاله: مَا نَنْسَخْ مِنْ ءَايَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا ﴾ البقرة / ١٠٦ .
فأما بعد وفاته صلى الله عليه وسلم : فلا ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِّكْرَ وَإِنَّا لَهُ لَحَفِظُونَ ) الحجر / ٩ : أي نحفظه منزلاً، لا يلحقه تبديل؛ صيانةً للدين إلى آخر الدهر.
وأما القسم الثاني والثالث: فصحيحان عند عامة العلماء. ومن الناس مَن أنكر ذلك، فقال: لأن النص لحكمه، فلا يبقى بدونه، والحكم بالنص ثبت، فلا يبقى بدونه.
ولعامة العلماء: أن الإيذاء باللسان، وإمساك الزواني في البيوت نُسخ حكمه، وبقيت تلاوته، وكذلك الاعتداد بالحول، ومثله كثير. ولأن للنظم حكمين جواز الصلاة وما هو قائم بمعنى صيغته، وجواز الصلاة حكم مقصود بنفسه.
وكذلك الإعجاز الثابت بنظمه: حكم مقصود بنفسه، فيبقى النص لهذين الحكمين.
ودلالة أنهما يصلحان مقصودين: ما ذكرنا أن من النصوص ما متشابة لا يثبت به إلا ما ذكرنا من الإعجاز وجواز الصلاة، فلذلك استقام البقاء بهما، وانتهى الآخر.
وأما نسخ التلاوة، وبقاء الحكم فمثل قراءة ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه في كفارة اليمين : فصيام ثلاثة أيام متتابعات»؛ لأنه لما صح إلحاقه عنده بالمصحف، ولا تهمة في روايته وجب الحمل على أنه نُسخ نظمه ، وبقى حكمه.
وهذا لأن للنظم حكماً يتفرد به، به، وهو ما ذكرنا فيصلح أن يكون هذا الحكم متناهياً أيضاً، ويبقى الحكم بلا نظم، وذلك صحيح في أجناس الوحي.
وأما القسم الرابع : فمثل الزيادة على النص، فإنها نسخ عندنا.
The abrogated (types): 1 - recitation and ruling. And ruling without recitation. And recitation without ruling. Chapter detailing the abrogated. (1) Chapter detailing the abrogated: everything in it has preceded in the chapters before it. 4- And abrogation of a description in the ruling. As for abrogation of both recitation and ruling together, an example is the scriptures of Abraham, peace be upon him, for they were abrogated entirely, either by being removed from hearts or by the death of scholars, and this was permissible in the Quran during the lifetime of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him. Allah, blessed and exalted, said: 'We shall make you recite, so you shall not forget, except what Allah wills' (al-A'la 87:6-7). And He, majesty His, said: 'Whatever verse We abrogate or cause to be forgotten' (al-Baqarah 2:106). As for after his death, peace be upon him: no, because of His statement: 'Indeed, it is We who sent down the Reminder and indeed, We will be its guardian' (al-Hijr 15:9): meaning We guard it as revealed, no alteration reaches it, to preserve the religion until the end of time. As for the second and third types: they are valid according to the general body of scholars. But some people denied that, saying: because the text is for its ruling, so it cannot remain without it, and the ruling is established by the text, so it cannot remain without it. And for the general body of scholars: that the harming by the tongue and the keeping of adulteresses in houses were abrogated in their ruling, and their recitation remained, and likewise the waiting period of a year, and many similar examples. And because the text has two rulings: the permissibility of prayer and what is established in the meaning of its wording, and the permissibility of prayer is a ruling intended in itself. Likewise the miraculousness established by its wording: a ruling intended in itself, so the text remains for these two rulings. And the indication that they are suitable as intended: what we mentioned that among the texts are ambiguous ones that establish only what we mentioned of miraculousness and permissibility of prayer, therefore the continuation through them was sound, and the other ended. And as for abrogation of the recitation while the ruling remains, an example is the reading of Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding the expiation of an oath: 'Fasting three consecutive days'; because when its attachment to the Mushaf according to him was deemed correct, and there is no suspicion in his narration, it became necessary to conclude that its wording was abrogated and its ruling remained. And this is because the wording has a ruling unique to it, which is what we mentioned, so it is fit that this ruling be also terminable, and the ruling remains without wording, and that is valid in the genres of revelation. And as for the fourth type: an example is the addition to the text, for it is abrogation according to us.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents the classification of abrogation (naskh) into four categories. The author first lists the types: (1) abrogation of both recitation and ruling, (2) abrogation of ruling only, (3) abrogation of recitation only, and (4) abrogation of a description in the ruling (which is essentially an addition to the text). He then elaborates each type with examples and proofs. For the first type, he gives the example of the scriptures of Abraham (a.s.) which were completely abrogated, and argues that such abrogation was permissible during the Prophet's lifetime, citing Quranic verses (al-A'la 87:6-7 and al-Baqarah 2:106). After the Prophet's death, however, the Quran is protected from any such abrogation. For the second and third types (abrogation of ruling only and recitation only), he presents a dispute: the majority of scholars affirm their validity, while some deny it, arguing that a text cannot exist without its ruling. The author refutes this by showing that the text has two inherent rulings: the permissibility of reciting it in prayer and its miraculous nature (i'jaz). He gives examples such as the verse about keeping adulteresses (4:15) and the waiting period of a year (2:240), which were abrogated in ruling but remain recited. For abrogation of recitation only, he gives the example of Ibn Mas'ud's reading of the verse on expiation of an oath, which required three consecutive fasts. The recitation was abrogated but the ruling remains. Finally, the fourth type is defined as an addition to the text (ziyada 'ala al-nass), which in Hanafi usul is considered naskh because it changes the ruling.

Key Terms
نَسْخ (naskh): Abrogation: removal of a legal ruling by a later revealed text, or removal of the recitation itself.
تلاوة (tilāwah): The recitation of a Quranic verse; the wording/text as part of the Quran.
حكم (ḥukm): The legal ruling derived from the verse, e.g., obligation, prohibition, etc.
الزيادة على النص (al-ziyādah ʿalā al-naṣṣ): Adding a condition or restriction to the explicit ruling of a text, which according to Hanafis constitutes naskh (abrogation) because it changes the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, which type of naskh is exemplified by the verse about keeping adulteresses in homes (إمساك الزواني في البيوت)?
A) Naskh of both recitation and ruling
B) Naskh of ruling only
C) Naskh of recitation only
D) Naskh of description (increase upon the text)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of naskh into four types, with the recognition that a text can retain inherent rulings (recitation in prayer and i'jaz) even after its specific legal ruling is abrogated, is essential for understanding the integrity of the Quranic text.

Naskh — Abrogation
advanced
12 min
The Hanafi Defense of Naskh of Ruling Only: Textual and Rational Proofs
Addresses dispute over naskh of ruling only, provides proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that abrogation of ruling only is valid and refute the objection that a text cannot exist without its ruling.

Core Idea
A text has independent value through its recitation in prayer and miraculous nature, allowing it to remain after its specific ruling is abrogated.

Some scholars argued that if a ruling is abrogated, the text becomes meaningless and must also be abrogated. The Hanafi school, however, provides a robust defense by identifying two inherent rulings in every Quranic text: the permissibility of reciting it in prayer and its miraculous nature. These intrinsic values ensure the text remains even when its specific legal ruling is lifted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Some people (possibly Zahiri) argued that the text (nass) exists solely for its ruling; thus, if the ruling is abrogated, the text cannot remain.
This objection is based on the principle 'al-nass li-hukmihi' (the text is for its ruling).
2
State the Hanafi/Majority position
The general body of scholars affirms the validity of abrogating ruling alone while preserving recitation. This is supported by numerous examples.
Examples include verses 4:15 and 2:240.
3
Follow the Hanafi evidence
(a) Textual examples: verses like 4:15 and 2:240 have abrogated rulings but remain recited. (b) Rational proof: The text has two inherent rulings: permissibility of recitation in prayer and miraculous nature (i'jaz). These are self-sufficient purposes, as seen in ambiguous (mutashabih) verses that only serve these purposes.
The fact that mutashabih exists with no legal ruling proves that a text can exist solely for these inherent rulings.
4
Compare the opposing view
The objectors rely on the principle that a text's existence depends on its ruling. But this ignores the other inherent rulings.
The Hanafi response shows that the objector's principle is not absolute.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
Accepting or rejecting this type of naskh determines whether verses like 4:15 are considered part of the Quran with no operative ruling or considered removed entirely.
The majority view preserves the integrity of the Quranic text.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents the classification of abrogation (naskh) into four categories. The author first lists the types: (1) abrogation of both recitation and ruling, (2) abrogation of ruling only, (3) abrogation of recitation only, and (4) abrogation of a description in the ruling (which is essentially an addition to the text). He then elaborates each type with examples and proofs. For the first type, he gives the example of the scriptures of Abraham (a.s.) which were completely abrogated, and argues that such abrogation was permissible during the Prophet's lifetime, citing Quranic verses (al-A'la 87:6-7 and al-Baqarah 2:106). After the Prophet's death, however, the Quran is protected from any such abrogation. For the second and third types (abrogation of ruling only and recitation only), he presents a dispute: the majority of scholars affirm their validity, while some deny it, arguing that a text cannot exist without its ruling. The author refutes this by showing that the text has two inherent rulings: the permissibility of reciting it in prayer and its miraculous nature (i'jaz). He gives examples such as the verse about keeping adulteresses (4:15) and the waiting period of a year (2:240), which were abrogated in ruling but remain recited. For abrogation of recitation only, he gives the example of Ibn Mas'ud's reading of the verse on expiation of an oath, which required three consecutive fasts. The recitation was abrogated but the ruling remains. Finally, the fourth type is defined as an addition to the text (ziyada 'ala al-nass), which in Hanafi usul is considered naskh because it changes the ruling.

Key Terms
نَسْخ (naskh): Abrogation: removal of a legal ruling by a later revealed text, or removal of the recitation itself.
تلاوة (tilāwah): The recitation of a Quranic verse; the wording/text as part of the Quran.
حكم (ḥukm): The legal ruling derived from the verse, e.g., obligation, prohibition, etc.
إعجاز (i'jāz): The miraculous nature of the Quran's composition and content.
الزيادة على النص (al-ziyādah ʿalā al-naṣṣ): Adding a condition or restriction to the explicit ruling of a text, which according to Hanafis constitutes naskh (abrogation) because it changes the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends the validity of naskh of ruling only by showing that the Quranic text possesses independent inherent rulings (recitation in prayer and miraculous nature) that justify its retention in the Mushaf even after the specific legal ruling is abrogated.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate

Types of Naskh: Ruling Abrogated, Recitation Preserved and Vice Versa
Defending the validity of two types of naskh and providing proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of naskh (ruling abrogated/recitation preserved and vice versa) and explain the Hanafi proof for each using the examples given.

Core Idea
A text can survive its ruling’s abrogation for its own sake; a ruling can survive its text’s abrogation by reliable report.

Abrogation (naskh) is not always total. The Hanafi school, with the majority, recognizes that sometimes only the ruling is lifted while the Qur'anic wording remains, and sometimes only the wording is lifted while the ruling stays in effect. This lesson unpacks the rationale and evidence for both types, addressing a common objection and providing concrete examples from revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of naskh under discussion
Distinguish between abrogation of the ruling while the text remains, and abrogation of the text while the ruling remains. Note that the author presents them together as defended by the majority.
These are the second and third types in a classical classification.
2
Understand the objection against these types
Some scholars argue that a text exists solely for its ruling, so if the ruling is gone, the text is pointless. Identify this premise.
The objectors see the text and ruling as inseparable.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response for type 2 (ruling abrogated, text remains)
The text serves two independent rulings: the permissibility of reciting it in prayer and its inimitability (i'jaz). These remain even after the specific legal ruling is abrogated. List the examples given: harming by tongue, confining adulteresses, waiting period of a year.
Recitation in prayer is a ruling intended for itself, separate from the verse's legal content.
4
Examine the proof for type 3 (text abrogated, ruling remains)
The example is Ibn Mas'ud's reading 'fasting three consecutive days' for expiation of an oath. Since this reading is reliably transmitted but not in the standard Mushaf, it shows the wording was abrogated while the ruling remained.
Hanafis accept ahad reports as evidence for such a type of naskh.
5
Recognize the usul principle: texts have multiple functions
The author concludes that a text has a unique ruling (its wording/qira'a) that can be abrogated independently from the legal ruling, and vice versa. This is valid in all genres of revelation.
This principle allows the coexistence of preserved texts and abrogated rulings in the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني والثالث: فصحيحان عند عامة العلماء. ومن الناس مَن أنكر ذلك، فقال: لأن النص لحكمه، فلا يبقى بدونه، والحكم بالنص ثبت، فلا يبقى بدونه. ولعامة العلماء: أن الإيذاء باللسان، وإمساك الزواني في البيوت نُسخ حكمه، وبقيت تلاوته، وكذلك الاعتداد بالحول، ومثله كثير. ولأن للنظم حكمين جواز الصلاة وما هو قائم بمعنى صيغته، وجواز الصلاة حكم مقصود بنفسه. وكذلك الإعجاز الثابت بنظمه: حكم مقصود بنفسه، فيبقى النص لهذين الحكمين. ودلالة أنهما يصلحان مقصودين: ما ذكرنا أن من النصوص ما متشابة لا يثبت به إلا ما ذكرنا من الإعجاز وجواز الصلاة، فلذلك استقام البقاء بهما، وانتهى الآخر. وأما نسخ التلاوة، وبقاء الحكم فمثل قراءة ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه في كفارة اليمين: «فصيام ثلاثة أيام متتابعات»؛ لأنه لما صح إلحاقه عنده بالمصحف، ولا تهمة في روايته وجب الحمل على أنه نُسخ نظمه، وبقي حكمه. وهذا لأن للنظم حكماً يتفرد به، وهو ما ذكرنا فيصلح أن يكون هذا الحكم متناهياً أيضاً، ويبقى الحكم بلا نظم، وذلك صحيح في أجناس الوحي.
As for the second and third types: they are valid according to the majority of scholars. Some people denied that, saying: because the text is for its ruling, so it does not remain without it, and the ruling is established by the text, so it does not remain without it. And according to the majority of scholars: that harming by the tongue, and imprisoning adulteresses in houses are abrogated in their ruling, while their recitation remained, and likewise the waiting period of a year, and many similar examples. And because the text has two rulings: the permissibility of prayer and what subsists by the meaning of its wording, and the permissibility of prayer is a ruling intended for itself. Likewise, the inimitability established by its wording is a ruling intended for itself, so the text remains for these two rulings. And the evidence that both are suitable as intended purposes: what we mentioned that among the texts are some that are ambiguous and establish only what we mentioned of inimitability and permissibility of prayer, therefore the survival by them is sound, and the other ends. As for abrogation of recitation while the ruling remains, an example is the reading of Ibn Mas‘ud (may Allah be pleased with him) concerning the expiation for an oath: 'Fasting three consecutive days'; because when its inclusion in the Mushaf was established for him, and there is no suspicion in his narration, it is necessary to conclude that its wording was abrogated while its ruling remained. This is because the wording has a ruling that is unique to it, which is what we mentioned, so it is possible that this ruling also terminates, and the ruling remains without wording, and that is valid in the genres of revelation.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage discusses the second and third types of naskh (abrogation) according to the Hanafi school and the majority of scholars. The second type is abrogation of the ruling while the textual recitation remains, and the third is abrogation of the recitation while the ruling remains. The author first presents the objection of some who deny these types, arguing that a text is only for its ruling, so if the ruling is abrogated the text becomes pointless. The Hanafi response, representing the majority, provides examples: the verse about harming with the tongue and confining adulteresses in houses had their rulings abrogated, but the verses remain in the Qur'an. Similarly, the waiting period for widows was originally one year, but later abrogated to four months and ten days, while the verse remains. The author then argues that the text has two intrinsic rulings: the permissibility of reciting it in prayer (which is a ruling intended for itself) and the inimitability (i'jaz) of its wording. Therefore, even after a ruling derived from the text is abrogated, the text remains for these two purposes. For the third type, the example of Ibn Mas'ud's reading of the verse on expiation for an oath with 'three consecutive days' is given. Since this reading is reported reliably and is considered part of the Qur'an, but not included in the standard Mushaf, the conclusion is that its wording was abrogated but its ruling remained. This shows that a ruling can remain without its original textual wording, as is valid in other forms of revelation. The author emphasizes that the wording has a specific ruling (e.g., its unique wording) that can be abrogated while the legal ruling persists.

Key Terms
النسخ (an-naskh): Abrogation: the lifting of a Shariah ruling by a later Shariah evidence.
تلاوة (tilawa): The recitation of the Qur'anic text itself, as opposed to its ruling.
إعجاز (i‘jāz): The inimitability of the Qur'an's wording, which is a ruling intended for itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two rulings does the author say a text carries even after its specific legal ruling is abrogated?
A) Permissibility of prayer and inimitability.
B) Permissibility of prayer and guidance.
C) Inimitability and reward.
D) Guidance and wisdom.

Key Takeaway
A Qur'anic text can survive the abrogation of its ruling because it serves the independent purposes of recitation in prayer and inimitability, and a ruling can survive the abrogation of its text as demonstrated by the reading of Ibn Mas'ud.

Naskh — Abrogation
advanced

Addition to the Text: Naskh or Takhsis? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Presenting the Hanafi view that addition to a text is naskh and the Shafi'i view that it is takhsis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that addition to a text (al-ziyada 'ala al-nass) constitutes naskh and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument that it is takhsis.

Core Idea
Adding a new condition to an absolute ruling abrogates the absolute – that is naskh, not mere specification.

The fourth type of naskh according to Hanafi usul is addition to the text. This has been disputed by Imam al-Shafi'i, who views it as takhsis. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly deriving rulings when new conditions are introduced. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi argument, the Shafi'i response, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and definition of naskh
Identify how the Hanafis define naskh: 'bayān muddat al-hukm wa ibtidā’ hukm ākhar'. Note that they consider addition to a text as abrogation because it terminates the absolute ruling and initiates a restricted one.
The absolute and restricted cannot coexist, so the first must end.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and definition
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that naskh is total repeal (tabdil), whereas addition confirms the original and specifies its application. He treats addition as takhsis, not naskh.
For Shafi'i, specification leaves the original ruling valid for the remaining scope.
3
Analyze the core argument: opposites cannot coexist
Follow the Hanafi reasoning: absoluteness and restriction are opposites. When a restriction is added, the absolute must be abrogated because it cannot remain in effect. This is like the replacement of a whole ruling.
The analogy with part of a hadd penalty is used to illustrate that a partial change is still a change.
4
Examine the distinction from takhsis
Understand the difference in operation: takhsis clarifies that part of a general statement was never intended, while addition introduces a condition outside the original wording. Takhsis leaves the remaining part proven by the same text; addition replaces the text itself.
The Hanafi distinction hinges on whether the condition is encompassed by the original wording.
5
Apply the example of the married adulterer
Consider the addition of flogging to stoning for married adulterers. Hanafis see this as naskh of the stoning-alone ruling; Shafi'is see it as specification. Trace the thamarah al-khilaf in practical fiqh.
This example is given in the passage and illustrates the real-world impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الرابع : فمثل الزيادة على النص، فإنها نسخ عندنا. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : إنه تخصيص، وليس بنسخ. وذلك مثل زيادة النفي على الجلد وزيادة قيد الإيمان في كفارة اليمين والظهار قال لأن الرقبة عامة في الكافرة والمؤمنة، فاستقام فيها الخصوص، وإنما النسخ تبديل، وفي قيد الإيمان: تقرير، لا تبديل، وكذلك في شرط النفي تقرير للجلد، لا تبديل، فلم يكن نسخاً، وليس الشرط أن تكون الزيادة تخصيصاً لا محالة، بل ليس بنسخ بكل حال. ولنا: أن النسخ: بيان مدة الحكم، وابتداء حكم آخر، والنص المطلق يوجب العمل بإطلاقه، فإذا صار مقيداً صار شيئاً آخر؛ لأن التقييد والإطلاق ضدان لا يجتمعان، وإذا كان هذا غير الأول: لم يكن بد من القول بانتهاء الأول، وابتداء الثاني، وهذا لأنه متى صار مقيداً: صار المطلق بعضه، وما للبعض: حكم الوجود، كبعض العلة، وبعض الحد، حتى إن شهادة القاذف لا تبطل ببعض الحد عندنا؛ لأنه ليس بحد، فثبت أن هذا نسخ، بمنزلة نسخ جملته. وأما التخصيص: فتصرف في النظم ببيان أن بعض الجملة غير مراد بالنظم مما يتناوله النظم والقيد لا يتناوله الإطلاق. ألا يُرى أن الإطلاق عبارة عن العدم والتقييد عبارة عن الوجود، فيصير إثبات نص بالمقايسة، أو بخبر الواحد. ولأن المخصوص إذا لم يبق مراداً: بقي الباقي ثابتاً بذلك النظم بعينه، فلم يكن نسخاً.
As for the fourth type: it is like an addition to the text, for it is naskh according to us. Imam al-Shafi‘i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: it is takhsis, not naskh. For example, the addition of flogging to stoning (in the hadith of the married adulterer) and the addition of the condition of faith (iman) in expiation for an oath and dhihar. He said: because the word 'neck' (raqaba) is general for both the disbelieving and believing female slave, so specification is plausible. Naskh is change, while in the condition of faith there is confirmation, not change. Likewise, in the condition of flogging there is confirmation for stoning, not change, so it is not naskh. It is not the case that addition must necessarily be takhsis; rather it is definitely not naskh. Our argument: naskh is the statement of the duration of a ruling and the beginning of another ruling. An absolute text obligates acting on its absoluteness. When it becomes restricted, it becomes something else, because restriction and absoluteness are opposites that cannot come together. If this is not the first, there is no alternative to saying that the first has ended and the second has begun. This is because when it becomes restricted, the absolute becomes part of it, and 'part' has the ruling of existence, like a part of a cause (illah) and a part of a penalty (hadd). For example, the testimony of a false accuser (qadhif) is not invalidated by a part of the hadd penalty according to us, because it is not the full hadd. So it is established that this is naskh, like the naskh of the whole. As for takhsis: it is an operation on the wording by clarifying that some part of the sentence is not intended by the wording, even though the wording encompasses it, while the restricting condition is not encompassed by the absolute. Do you not see that absoluteness expresses non-existence, while restriction expresses existence? So it becomes establishing a text by analogy or by a solitary report. And because when that which is specified is no longer intended, the remainder remains established by that very wording, so it is not naskh.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth type of naskh: addition to the text (al-ziyada 'ala al-nass). The Hanafi position, held by Abu Hanifa and his followers, is that adding a qualification to an absolute ruling constitutes naskh (abrogation) because it invalidates the previous absolute ruling and establishes a new restricted ruling. The Shafi'i school, represented by Imam al-Shafi'i, argues that such addition is merely takhsis (specification), not naskh. The example given is the addition of flogging to stoning for married adulterers—Shafi'i sees this as specifying the general ruling of stoning, not abrogating it. Similarly, adding the condition of faith (iman) to the expiation for oath and dhihar specifies the general command regarding manumission of a slave. Shafi'i reasons that naskh involves change (tabdil), whereas addition confirms the original ruling while restricting it. The Hanafi response defines naskh as 'clarifying the duration of a ruling and initiating another'. An absolute text demands action in its absoluteness; when a restriction is added, the absolute ruling ends and a new qualified ruling begins. Since absoluteness and restriction are opposites, they cannot coexist. Therefore, addition is abrogation of the absolute by the qualified. The Hanafi further distinguishes this from takhsis: takhsis only clarifies that part of the general statement is not intended, but the remaining part still relies on the original wording. In contrast, the restricting condition is not part of the original wording, so the original ruling must have been abrogated. The Hanafi also uses an analogy from hadd penalties: if a partial hadd is not considered a valid hadd (e.g., the testimony of a qadhif is not invalidated by a part of the hadd), then a partially abrogated ruling is not the original ruling. Thus, addition is like abrogation of the whole text. The passage concludes by reiterating the Shafi'i counterargument that the specified part is no longer intended, but the remainder is still proven by the original text, so no abrogation occurs. This is a classic usul dispute with significant fiqh implications, such as the ruling regarding the married adulterer and the conditions for expiation.

Key Terms
الزيادة على النص (az-ziyāda ‘alā an-nass): Adding a qualification (e.g., a condition or additional penalty) to a textual ruling that was previously unqualified.
التخصيص (at-takhṣīṣ): Specification: showing that a part of a general statement is not intended by the wording.
النسخ (an-naskh): Abrogation: the ending of a Shariah ruling by a later evidence, requiring a new ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is addition to the text (al-ziyada 'ala al-nass) considered naskh rather than takhsis?
A) Because the added condition is a new ruling that contradicts the original absolute ruling.
B) Because the original text becomes obsolete and is no longer recited.
C) Because it involves a change in the wording of the Qur'an.
D) Because the majority of scholars agree it is naskh.

Key Takeaway
Addition to a text is naskh according to Hanafis because it replaces the absolute ruling with a restricted one, whereas Shafi'is consider it takhsis that leaves the original intact.
